Louis Becke
The Red page
AUSTRALIAN WRITERS: No. 7.
[Louis Becke died in Sydney, Feb. 19th.
The following account of his literary life
was written by himself last year for this
page.]
My birthplace was the sleepy old town of
Port Macquarie, at the mouth of the Hast-
ings River. ,My earliest recollections go
back to the time when I was six years of
age, and the pilot allowed me to scramble
into the pilot boat when she was launched
down the greasy skids to board one of the
old-time steamers; and I shall always re-
member the ecstasy of the boat facing the
heavy surf that rolled into Pilot Cove —a
place hewn out of the solid rock by convict
hands in the old days when the swish of the
lash and the rattle of the gyves reigned* in
the land.
My father was a Government official —
police magistrate, coroner and many other
things; and I, the youngest son of six, got
the combined thrashings due to the whole
six for my wandering proclivities and run-
nings away from home. Before I was ten
years of age I had twice made my way to
Camden Haven, 23 miles from Port Mac-
quarie, to escape the terrors of day school
and Sunday school.
The schoolmaster was a man named
Blackwood, who was habitually “sprung” by
nine in the morning, and very kind to all
the pupils. In the afternoon his mood
would change, and he used his supple-jack
cane most mercilessly, generally picking me
out as his first victim. I have endeavored
to portray his character in one of my
books entitled Breachley, Black Sheep.
The Anglican parson was the Rev. Canon
O’Reilly, whose sermons consisted solely of
Hell Fire and Eternal Damnation. He,
too, like the schoolmaster, used the cane
freely after the Sunday-school service was
oyer; and I often went away from the
hideous, squat little Sunday-school house —
an adjunct of the more than hideous red-
bricked, convict-built St. Thomas’s Church
—with my hands black and blue. My father
never gave me any sympathy—he believed
in flogging—but my mother gave me lard
for my hands, and bread and jam for my
interior, and I would always promise not
to run away again. I think that the reason
why the schoolmaster and parson disliked
me so much was on account of my stammer-
ing, with which I had been afflicted from
my infancy. On one of my runaway ven-
tures I was accompanied by a red-headed
girl. The mounted police brought us back.
The time came, when I was 12 years of
age, when I ran away in earnest, and stayed
away for _ six years. One of my brothers
was carrying on business as a stationer and
bookseller in King-street, Sydney, and I had
to serve in the shop until I was old enough
to enter the service of the Australian Joint
Stock Bank, the prospect of which filled me
with horror, for I wanted to become a
pirate. Always fond of wandering about
the wharves, I had made the acquaintance
of a Rarotongan native who was an A.B.
ofi the barque Rotumah, which was a regu-
lar trader to Tonga, Samoa and Wallis
Island. His English name was George;
land to George I confided my sorrows. He
had a Samoan wife living in Apia, and told
me that if ever I came to Samoa I would
find a home there. He consented to my stow-
ing away on the Rotumah; and, a week
later, in the early morning, I crept upstairs
to my dear mother’s bedroom, kissed her in
her sleep, and then fled violently down to
Miller’s Point where the Rotumah was lying
ready for sea. George and the nigger cook,
one Harry Mills, hid me away in the fore-
hold, and that evening the t rque was out-
side Sydney Heads, and I was both happy
and miserable —happy at the thought of
South Sea Islands, and miserable when my
mother’s face came before me.
I soon found employment m Samoa, and
rapidly acquired the language; and one of the
first friends I made was the lately-deceased
old warrior Mataafa. And although I did
not become a pirate I became a very success-
ful smuggler of arms and ammunition.
I had returned to Sydney from the Caro-
line Islands pretty well broken-down with
malarial fever and deadly hard up, when I one
day met the late Ernest Favenc, who asked
me to come along with him and “have a talk”
with Mr. .T. F. Archibald, editor of The Bulle-
tin. I did so, and on the following day wrote
my first story of the South Seas. It was en-
titled Ghallis, the Doubter. Mr. Archibald
liked it, and told me to write more. The re-
sult was a collection of short tales and
sketches, which I entitled By Reef and
Palm.
The editor of The Bulletin sent copies of'
the book to Harry Massingham, editor of the
London Daily Chronicle, who offered the col-
lection to Fisher Unwin. He was willing to
buy the copyright for £l5. (The Bulletin
had paid about £70.) Mr. Ar> ibald advised
me to write to Mr. Unwin, and tell him that
if he could only offer me £l5 I would keep
my stories to read to my friends in Heaven.
Then Unwin sprang another £5O, apd the
little work appeared. But months before this
I had slung my scrub-hook and was writing
other stories for The Bulletin, and getting
square meals from the proceeds thereof. The
second volume consisted of 17 Bulletin
stories, which I also sent to Unwin. He paid
me £2OO for The Ebbing of the Tide; and it
was then that Messrs. Archibald and Macleod
told me to go to London; and they financed
me to the extent of £2OO. The Ebbing of the
Tide proved almost as great a success as By
Reef and Palm ; and when I reached London
commissions flowed in from all sides. The
Pall Mall Gazette, the Daily Chronicle, the
Earl of Pembroke and Lord Rosebery did me
great service; so also did Kipling, Richard
Whiteing and other great writers; and I had
to place my affairs in the hands of Watt and
Son, literary agents. After a year in London
I went to spend a month in Ireland, and
stayed there for three years, writing continu-
ously for the English magazines.
From Ireland I went to France to place
my daughter at school at Le Havre, and re-
mained in France for three and a half years.
During this time I wrote a series of stories
and articles for the Pall Mall and Westmin-
ster Gazettes, which were published in book-
form under the title of Sketches from Nor-
mandy. It was not a literary success, but I
was well paid for it. Before this I had writ-
ten a tale of adventure (primarily for boys),
called Tom Wallis, and placed it with my
literary agent, who hawked it about from
publisher to publisher for months. He could
have sold it over and over again for a price
that would have well satisfied me, but he
wanted too much for it. I was then con-
tributing short stories to the Boys’ Own
Paper and the Leisure Hour; and one day the
editor of the latter asked me if I could give
him a long serial. I thought of Tom Wallis,
and managed to get the MS. back from the
agent. Within three days the Religious Tract
Society bought the serial rights for £2lO for
the Leisure Hour and the book rights for a
similar sum. It had a great success. My
aggnt (who drew up the usual agreement
with R.T.S.) netted his 10 per cent, com-
mission, although he had absolutely nothing
to do with selling it. After that experience
I became my own agent.
I wrote Helen Adair whilst in the West
Indies, sold the serial rights (12 months'
use to a newspaper syndicate for £55, and
was paid £2OO for the book rights; Tom
Gerrard I wrote in France, and received
£250 fer the book rights, but failed to sell
it serially. Wild Life in Southern Seas —a
collection of tales and stories, which I con-
sider holds my best work, had a big sale in
the United States and Canada. The
libraries and schools bought it as an edu-
cational work. One story in the book, Hino
the Apostate (8000 words), brought me £4O
as a short serial. Pacific Tales and By
Rock and Pool were both successful works;
and the former was translated into French
and Swedish.
Since I first cast away axe and scrub-
hook and began to write for The Bulletin,
I have turned out a fair number of books —
volumes of short stories and novels; and
the critics have always been good to me. I
cannot remember the titles of them all, but
here are some :
By Beef and Palm, The Ebbing of the Tide, Wild
Life in ■Southern Seas, By Rock and Pool, Tom Ger-
rard, ChinHes’ Flat, Yorke the Adventurer, Sketches
from Normandy, Notes from My South Sea Log, The
Strange Adventure of James Shervinton, Tom Wallis,
’Neath Austral Skies, The Call of the South, Adven-
tures of Larry Blake, IJis Native Wife, Tessa and
the Trader’s Wife, Pearl Divers of Roncador Reef,
Ridan the Devil, Rodman the Boatsteerer, Edward
Barry, Under Tropic Skies, Breachley Black Sheep,
Helen Adair, and about 10 others.
All these works were published at 6s.
with colonial editions at 2s. 6d. I received
many hundreds of letters from men making
inquiries as to how to get to the islands,
cost of living, etc.; and they invariably
wound up by some very pointed questions
concerning the ways of the Brown Woman.
I found literary life in London very plea-
sant, but one has to work hard to keep in
the swim. The high-class English maga-
zines pay well, but it is hard in most cases
for a beginner to get a foothold.
I wish to add that whatever literary suc-
cess I may have achieved is due entirely to
the training I received from the editor of
The Bulletin, who taught me the secrets
of condensation and simplicity of language.
LOUIS BECKE.
From Meredith’s Letters
Of Woman.
The case with women resembles that of
the Irish. We have played fast and loose
with them, until now they are encouraged
to demand what they know not how to use,
but have a just right to claim. If the
avenues of our professions had been thrown
open to them, they might have learnt the
business of the world, to be competent to
help in governing. But these were closed,
women were commanded to continue their
reliance upon their poor attractions. Con-
sequently, as with the Irish, they push to
grasp the baguette which gives authori-v.
And they will get it; and it will be a horri-
ble time. But better than the present
sights
Men railing at women for an excessive
domestic volubility, little thought of the
valve their public speaking will serve for.
All the old tales of women are going to be
reversed....
By-and-bye the world will smile on women
who cut their own way out of a bad early
marriage, or it will correct the present rough
marriage system. No young woman knows
what she gives her hand to; she will never
be wiser until boys and girls are brought up
and educated together. Let me add, until
English girls have wiser mothers. Such
donkeys are those dames in all our
classes !....
At the present time women need en-
couragement to look out upon affairs of
national interest, and men should do their
part ip helping them to state publicly what
has long been confined to the domestic circle
—consequently a wasted force. That it is a
force men are beginning to feel. That the
exercise of it is an education we see already
in the enlargement of their view of life and
of the country’s needs. So there is hope that
the coming generation will have more intel-
ligent mothers....
Sentimental prattle of the mother, the
wife, the sister is not needed when we see,
a§ the choicer spirits of men do now see, that
women have brains, and can be helpful to
the hitherto entirely dominant muscular
creature who has allowed them some degree
of influence in return for servile flatteries
and the graceful undulations of the snake —
admired yet dreaded. Women must have
brains to have emerged from so long a bond-
age
Of His Method.
When at the conclusion of your article
on my works, you say that a certain change
in public taste, should it come about, will
be, to some extent, due to me, you hand me
the flowering wreath I covet. For I think
that all right use of life, and the one secret
of life, is to pave ways for the firmer footing
of those who succeed us; as to my works, I
know them faulty, think them of worth only
when they point and aid to that end.... 1
have supposed that the novel, exposing and
illustrating the natural history of man, may
help us ■to such sustaining roadside gifts.
But I have never started on a novel to pur-
sue the theory it developed. The dominant
idea in my mind took up the characters and
the story midway....My method has been
to prepare my readers for a crucial exhibi-
tion of the personae, and then to give tlte
scene in the fullest of their blood and brain
under stress of a fiery situation. Concern-
ing style, thought is tough, and dealing with
thought produces toughness. Or when strong
emotion is in tide against the active mind,
there is perforce confusion.
Of “Diana of the Crossways.”
“Diana of the Crossways” keeps me still
on her sad last way to wedlock. I could
have killed her merrily, with my compli-
ments to the public; and that was my inten-
tion. But the marrying of her sets me
traversing feminine labyrinths, and you know
the why of it never can be accounted for. . . .
My “Diana” stills holds me ; only by the
last chapter; but the coupling of such a
woman and her man is a delicate business.
She has no puppet-pliancy. The truth
being that she is a mother of Experience,
and gives that dreadful baby suck to brains.
I have, therefore, a feeble hold of her; none
of the novelist’s winding-up arts avail; it is
she who leads me. ...
Here is another American actress applying
for permission to dramatise “Diana.” I
must let her know that Ulrica dislikes the
character. Yet Ulrica says of herself that
she has imagination. Then she ought to be
able to enter the breast of a passionate
woman, a wife widowed, much needing to be
on her guard against the man, ready to fly
with him, hating to intrigue; and while she
totter in this juncture, assailed by monetary
needs, vain of her touch on political secrets,
subject in a crisis to a swoon of the mind-
mark that, O imaginative lady! for there
are women and noble women, who stand
unpractised and alone in the world, liable to
these attacks, driven for the moment back
on their instincts. ... “She behaved basely.”
But she was physically ahd mentally un-
aware of the importance of the secret....
Of the Last Phase.
Since last September I have not held a
pen, except perforce to sign my name....So
here I am, of no use to anyone. . . .We who
have loved the motion of legs and the sweep
of the winds, we come to this. But for
myself, I will own that it is the natural
order. There is no irony in Nature
I can imagine that I shall retain my laugh
in Death’s ear, for that is what our Maker
prizes in men....
When I ceased to walk briskly part of
my life had ended, and I am bored by hav-
ing to read belated opinions of the work I
have done
The shower of letters since my oppressive
SOth (birthday) is abating; still daily I
have one or two for answer. It is hard at
my age to be pitchforked up in the public
eye. Some would like it.
Spiders
The translation of the works of the most
famous naturalist in the world, J. H. Fabre,
continues. The latest volume, which reprints
the famous preface by Maeterlinck that in-
troduced Fabre’s name to us, is titled The
Life of the Spider (Hodder and Stoughton,
per Angus and Robertson, Sydney). It is
just as fascinating as The Life and Love of
the Insect , and Social Life in the Insect
World . Here is the spider’s life, from the
egg to the weaving of the web. Of the mar-
vels of the spider’s weaving apparatus Fabre
is never wearied. The spider possesses the
most wonderful silk-factory in existence.
“The egg-bag of the Eleirse is an important
and complex work. Various materials enter
into its composition: white silk, red silk,
brown silk; moreover, these materials are
worked into dissimilar products: stout cloth,
soft eiderdown, dainty satinette, porous felt.
And all this comes from the same workshop
that weaves the hunting-net, warps the zigzag
ribbon-band and casts an entangling shroud
over the prey.... With a very simple and
never-varying plant, consisting of the hind
legs and the spinnerets, it produces, by turns,
rope-maker’s, spinner’s, weaver’s, ribbop-
maker’s and fuller’s work. How does she ob
tain, at will, skeins of diverse hues and
grades? How does she turn them out, first
in this fashion, then in that? I see the re-
sults, but I do not understand the machinery
and still less the process.”
To our rudimentary knowledge of the mir-
acle of instinct Fabre makes valuable con-
tributions. He finds, for instance, that: ‘‘Th e
instincts have a calendar of their own. At
the given hour suddenly they awaken; "as
suddenly, afterwards, they fall asleep.. .’.To
the insect what is done is done, and cannot
be taken up again. The hands of a watch do
not move backwards. The insect behaves in
much the same way. Its activity urges it in
one direction, ever forwards, without allow-
ing it to retrace its steps, even when an acci-
dent makes this necessary. .. .In the Lyeosa,
at the moment of the exodus (just after
birth), a sudden instinct arises, to disappear,
as promptly and for ever, a few hoUrs later.
This is the climbing instinct, which is un-
known to the adult and soon forgotten by the
emancipated youngling, doomed to wander
homeless, for many a long day, upon the
ground. Neither of them dreams of climbing
to the top of a grass stalk.”
The reason for this momentary Climbing in-
stinct is that the spider youngster wants to
become, for a few hours, an aeronaut. It
wants to see the world, to be carried from the
maternal home, and, accordingly, it climbs to
a height, spins a filament of silk, catches
hold and sails away.
The most interesting portion-of this volume
is that given up to the spider’s web. At the
end of Fabre’s engrossing chapters the reader
knows everything that is to be known about
the geometry and manufacture of spiders’
webs. For instance, he notes that the spider
never repairs her web. Either she goes on
hunting with a web in tatters, or she eats the
torn web every evening and spins another.
“The machine-shuttle in our looms does not
revert to the spoiled fabric; even so with the
spider working at her web.... All the large
spinstresses suffer from a similar incapacity
for patching... .The spider is absolutely
unable to mend her work. In spite of her
thoughtful appearance, the Eleira is incapable
of the modicum of reflection required to in-
sert a piece into an accidental gap.”
The spiral thread of the web is a capillary
tube ifiner than any that physics will ever
know 7 . Elasticity is provided for, in order to
allow the captured prey to tug but not break
it, by its being rolled into a twist; and it
holds a supply of sticky matter in reserve in
its tube, whence it exudes to renew its ad-
hesive properties. And the spider varnishes
herself with a special oily sweat so as to pre-
vent her sticking to her own web. But there
is a central platform formed of non-adhesive
threads. And the maryellous quantity of
thread produced ! It takes some 20 yards of
gummy thread for the web of one species of
spider, and in cases where a snider renews
her web every evening, Fabre calculated, from
observation "during two months, that one
spider had produced something like three-
quarters of a mile of this tubular thread,
rolled into a tight twist and bulging with
glue.
“How is the silky matter moulded into a
capillary tube? How is this tube filled with
glue and tightly twisted? And how does this
same wire-mill also turn out plain threads,
wrought first into a frame-work and then into
muslin and satin: next a russet foam
What a number of products to come from
that curious factory, a spider’s belly! ’
A chapter is given to the telegraph wire
which certain spiders stretch from the centre
of the web to their hiding-place. “Clutching
her telephone-wire with a toe, the spider
listens with her leg; she perceives the inner-
most vibrations; she distinguishes between
the vibration proceeding from a prisoner and
the mere shaking caused by the wind.” Into
Fabre’s interesting speculation as to the
means by which young spiders keep alive and
active, without any food, for months he sug-
gests that it is due to solar heat transformed
directly into motor-power; the motive heat,
instead of being extracted from the food,
being utilised direct —and his analysis of the
geometry of the spider’s web, with the weav-
er’s instinctive knowledge of what is known,
in the higher and dizzier mathematics, as the
logarithmic spiral,” there is no room to
And for Romance, what is more delightful
than this? “With September comes the
nuptial badge, the black-velvet bib. ine
spiders meet at night, by the soft moonlight,
they romp together, they eat the beloved
shortly after the wedding.”

ANTHONY HORDERNS’ FINE ART GALLERY
CERTAINLY the most notable addition to the many features that are presented at ANTHONY HORDERNS’
NEW PALACE EMPORIUM is the FINE ART GALLERY. This new section of our enterprise represents
collections of wares in varied branches of art, which appeal to the connoisseur, and all who have an innate
love for the beautiful. Many of the articles are eminently suitable for Gifts, being pleasing examples of the com-
bination of artistic talent with industrial enterprise.
The New Department may be regarded as a permanent exhibition of the Fine Arts, ranging from genuine
Bronzes, and exquisite French Enamels, Cut Crystal Glassware, to Pottery and Porcelain, which embraces many Fine
pieces of Irreproachable Style and Beauty in Royal Doulton, Royal Worcester, Royal Crown Derby, Coalport, Cope-
land, Minton, Wedgwood, Belleek, Royal Copenhagen, Lancastrian, Bernard Moore, Gouda, and Austrian Terra
Cotta Ware. >
Merit in design and craftmanship entitles these artistic present-day productions to stand cheek-by-jowl with the
Antiques. The Fame of the Fine Art Department has extended far and wide. Visitors are cordially invited to
inspect the art treasures that have been created by the handiwork and genius of man. All articles are marked in
plain figures, at prices which are extremely moderate, and no one is importuned to buy.
ART TERRA COTTA WARE
Comprising' Statuary, Groups, Vases, Figures,
The artistic excellence of this collection shown in the FINE ART GALLERY

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Joy Bringer.
As the rain sweeps down from rampart hills
With the gladful music of countless rills,
lo flood the creek and flush it clear
Ot the debris and dust of a shrivelled year,
So into our days,
With happy amaze,
Lame the gay disperser of waifs and strays,
And memory-silt of the past, dead days. '
M elcome the ring of his voice as song
riotous waters that surge along;
And his bass of merriment rumbled out
Like thunder portending the end of drought;
While, as he laughed,
It seemed we quaffed
The blood of the grape, and that each care
YY as drowned in the purple of beakers rare.
As grass-blades rise at the call of rain
In their emerald jackets upon the plain,
And crickets right merrily serenade
The wind as it plays with each lancet-blade.
So our joy found birth,
And we sped from dearth
O er beckoning prospects of golden hours
With the verve of a spirit we felt as ours.
Then he rose and faced for the closing day—
For the mystic light of the western way;
But ours was the gain of a world grown
new
With the ample gift of a gracious dew,
Of scintUlant thought,
And courage caught
From the master-spirit who swept away
The morbid fears of a sombre day.
S. AUSTRALIA.
CLAUDE MELVIN.
The Song of the True Milkmaid.
I do not wish that I should be
Regarded as a Queen of May,
Bucolic joys are not for me,
I find no bliss in new-made hay;
I love the airs that murmur free
Along the crowded asphalt way,
The breeze with many skirts a-bloom
That lifts the tufted ostrich plume.
I do not want to drive at eve
The brindled heifer through the gate,
To watch the twisted gum trees weave
Large shadows where the milkers wait;
I never really can believe
In things that rural poets state,
But I am glad to hear the sound
Of honking cars where wheels go round.
I never want to see a cow
Again in all my earthly days;
I dream of light, and stir, and row
That linger in the city ways;
The moon upon the mountain’s brow
Shall never move my soul to praise,
But I should love the lights aglow
Where Algernon and Cissy go.
I want to know my dress will fit,
To hear the chatter of my kind,
In crowded stalls at night to sit
While actor-folk improve my mind,
And ordered legs to music flit
Around a plot that none can find,
Or large throats warble out for me
Bunches of catchy melody.
I want to eat delightful things
At pleasant tables, cool and bright;
To hear a sound of trembling strings
Aiding digestion with delight;
I want to taste the wine that flings
Its sparkle on the flashing night;
Some costly gems I want to wear
About my neck, and arms, and hair.
Alas ! I lace my blucher boot.
And shameful is the thing I do,
To set a small and rosy foot,
That ought to wear a number two,
In leather shapeless as the brute
On whose dull carcase once it grew—
A Cinderella most forlorn
I go to tramp the mire of morn.
Oh, for a week of life replete
With all the things I have not here!
These days go by on laggard feet,
And every month is like a year;
I want to know the changing street,
The hum of voices in my ear,
I want to know that I am free
From dishes, pails and Strawberry.
N.s.w.
MARY McCOMMONWEALTH.
The Happy Wowser.
This life is mostly dull routine ;
For “breaks” one pays in coin or worry;
Around the corners idlers lean.
And watch the civilised machine,
Till some excitement makes them hurry;
A street arrest, a fighting drunk,
An accident, a row, a fire —
Before they sadly go to bunk,
Some thrill like this they all desire.
They lack imagination. That’s
The reason of their nightly waiting,
Till ruffled and ejected cats,
Flung out with slams and hostile “Scats !
Into the frosty night come skating.
They see no harm or fun in things
That turn the watching Wowser livid,
And make him do wild Highland flings.
His seething mind is fresh and vivid.
The calm and seasoned sinner who
Sustains his part in Life’s drab drama,
Enjovs a play or book that’s blue.
(He pays up gladly for it, too,
To Paris or to Yokohama.)
The lucky Wowser goes his way,
Collides with Life and leaps and bellows ;
He gets more cheap excitement, say,
Than less imaginative fellows.
If ev’ry mile one got a shock,
And ev’ry grove held thrills galvanic,
If shudders' lurked behind each rock,
We would not make a yawning flock „
Round “daring acts” and fights “titanic.
Poor jaded souls! What are our shows
But silly shams at graded prices!
Real fun the watching Wowser knows;
He ’tis who most enjoys our vices.
N.S.W.
THE RELIGIOUS EDITOR.

SOON
FINISHED

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some
care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin/-’ is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the form
of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short
Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Australian sub-
jects, and not exceeding two columns in length, or,
say, 8000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs',
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded with-
out stamps for return it will be understood that the
copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Personal
applications for return of MS. cannot receive atten-
tion. The Bulletin desires, further, those sending
MS. to distinctly understand that it is not respon-
sible for the preservation or transmission of MS.

THURSDAY, FEBRUARY 27, 1913.
MORE ABOUT THE HIGH COST OF
GIVING.
On a recent summer morning, when the sun
was bright, and the sky was blue, and the
sea was green, and the surf-bathers were wet
and gay, and the tram-cars were making the
same old disturbance, no less than 35 women
wrote in collaboration to the Sydney morn-
ing papers about the alleged high cost of liv-
ing. They said, among other things, that
the cause of the alleged high cost of living
is the tariff, and that if it was obliterated
off the face of the earth with an axe all
would be well. Incidentally, they remarked
that woman is instinctively a Freetrader,
which was another way of‘saying that she
is by nature a bargain-hunter, and if she can
buy sweated and non-union boots from
Greece at Is. less than the old local price,
she regards the shilling as so much gained,
even though the importation of sweated
Greek boots has caused her bootmaking hus-
band to lose his job and his wages. In these
days the cost of living is a subject of gen-
eral concern in Australia, and especially in
N.S.W. The Government is having the
matter inquired into, and great masses of
evidence struggle forward daily to the effect
that something or another is more expensive
now than it used to be. The statement
seems feasible enough, for it is possible to
trace the price-lists of England back with
fan accuracy for 600 or 800 years, and prices
have been rising all the time. So have
wages and rent, while the only thing that
has had a fairly general downward tendency
is the rate of interest. Therefore, the hope
of doing good by an inquiry into current
prices is limited. The very first matter to
ne inquired into is why the old innkeeper of
f alestine was willing to take in the wounded
traveller when the Good Samaritan left 2d
tor his board, when the Australia Hotel
would want at least £lO deposit.
In some countries, when boots or soup or
beiltoppers go up in price, the blame is laid
on the President, and somebody shoots at
him. In others the responsibility is laid
on Providence' or the Devil. In others,
again, it is laid on the tariff, and there is
® clamor of 35 or more uninformed women
that the community should freely admit
foreign, cheap-labor products, and thus kill
local industries, so that the workman’s wife
may see lower-priced goods in the shop-
windows, but be unable to pay for them, be-
cause the old man has lost his billet. A
quaint conviction exists in certain quarters
that it would pay Australia better if it only
got half as much as it does at present for
the food it exports, that being the necessary
and logical outcome of the cheap food craze.
I or no one seems to have yet discovered how
to get the local loaf at 2d., while the ex-
ported loaf fetches 4d., and in a country with
a surplus of food for export dear food pays
vastly better than the cheap article. A con-
siderable section of the cheap food party is
also the high wage party, and it toils hard
to find out how the cost of producing the
food may be constantly shoved up, while the
price of the food is simultaneously shoved
down. The husbands of the 35 women who
lately clamored in the Sydney Preetrade
press about increased prices are—supposing
them to be skilled artisans —receiving about
twice the remuneration they or their kind
could command under the alleged blessings
of Freetrade about the year 1896, and
unless somethin? gigantic has been done
1 ?- ln^er^r P by way of improving ma-
eliminating the middleman, it is
difficult to see how the goods they turn out
can be produced at the old figure. At times
gigantic things are done by improved
machinery—for instance, Eli Whitney’.S
cotton gin enabled one nigger to do as much
work at cleaning cotton as previously occu-
pied 1500 or 2000 niggers, and thus it made
10 shirts more attainable than one had been
before, and changed the whole course of
United States history. But nothing abso-
lutely _ staggering has been accomplished in
this direction of late years. Again, a great
deal can be done in the way of eliminating
the middleman, especially by substituting
local for foreign industry, so that the great,
rich, expensive, bloated wholesale importer
may be obliterated, and the small, local
buyer may deal directly with the local manu-
facturer. But the cheap food crowd appears
rather to admire the importer—the worst
and most useless type of middleman—and to
desire his preservation. It is really diffi-
cult to see what can be done for the cheap
food party unless it can devise something
wholly marvellous in the way of new ap-
pliances, so that higher wages and lower
cost may go together, or achieve something
never yet dreamt of in the wav of economy
or distribution. The pay of the Australian
artisan has increased very largely during the
last 15 or 17 years. His prospects of get-
ting a steady job have increased also.
Despite the higher cost of living he lives
much better than he did, and after living
much better he has a far larger surplus, as
the savings bank returns clearly show. Thus
the Sydney rockchopper, who, not very long
ago, collected about Is. or Is. 2d. per hour,
is now asking as much as 45., and seems
likely to get it. (Considering the danger of
his calling he probably deserves it, but that
is another matter.) Although the price of
drains must needs go up under these condi-
tions. even the most indignant rockchopper
will hardly allege that he isn’t a little better
off than he was in the deadly old Freetrade,
low-wage days of Is. per hour. The extra
cost is no reason why the country should
sack its rockchopper and import cheap
drains, from China. The industrial situa-
tion is not ideal, but it is so much
improved under partial Protection, high
wages and increased prices as compared with
the times of Freetrade, low nrices. mean
wages, soup-kitchens, sand-shifting jobs, rail-
painting jobs, beggar-haunted streets, and
starvation, that it is unwise to go swiping
an axe at the basis of things.
If the actual toiler could secure the full
value, of his toil—which is a return to the
question of the obliteration of the middle-
man and co-operation became the universal
order, and the said co-operation was better
managed than it sometimes is. then there
would be every prospect of further improve-
ment for the mass of the people. But it
must be stipulated that the co-operatinu is
well managed. Robinson Crusoe and the
old-time. Australian aborigines and the Rue
Australia settlers in Paraguay got all they
produced without any deduction for rent, in-
terest, or dealers’ profits, and some of them
even escaped taxes, yet their wealth was
never overwhelming. In fact, they were
all-round failures. Many Australian rail-
way employees seem to get actually more
than they can produce while the article they
turn out —locomotion —is sold at the present
cheap rate, so when working expenses are
paid, the lines show a loss. When the
worker gets the entire selling value of what
he turns out, he will be well off provided he
works sufficiently, and sells intelligently,
and doesn’t run his business like that famous
organ, the Sydney Daily Post, and assum-
ing that. the price of his goods isn’t cut
down to impossible figures by sweated Free-
trade competition. These are important
conditions.
* * ■» *
The fatuous folk who clamor that the
golden road, to cheap living and costly wages,
to high-priced production and low-priced
products lies by way of Freetrade have a
very simple test close at hand. Let them
pass the hat round—the present writer will
put something considerable in it if it is
passed round in good faith by a reliable per-
son—-and send a couple of Australian artisan
families from among their crowd to live and
worii and rely solely on their earnings for a
year or two, say in Glasgow, and then re-
port progress. They will find that many
articles of food are dearer than they are
here, because they consist of Australian or
Maoriland food sold at Australian or Maori-
land prices, with freight added. They will
discover that the cheap living of Bull’s
country is based largely on the art of doing
without things. They will eat meat once or
twice a week, and, incidentally, they will
eat what in Australia they would call offal.
Thev will ascertain that wages are about
half the Australian figure or less, and that
city rents are higher, but they will make
the rents lower by crowding the whole family
into one or two rooms high up in a tene-
ment house, where there is no green thing
in front and no trace of herbage behind. If
it is an old tenement house in the St. Rollox
district of Glasgow, or the Old Town of
Edinburgh, there will be neither a bath nor
a lavatory in the whole rabbit warren. The
family will learn to damp down the fire in
between cooking spells, in order to save fuel.
Also, it will learn many other things. Among
them it will discover that the clamor about
the increased cost of living is just as violent
under Freetrade as it is under Protection.
And it will learn that the cost of living isn’t
everything. In 1911 the British Board of
Trade published the results of a careful in-
vestigation into the condition of the working
classes in Freetrade England, and the Pro-
tectionist United States. The figures look
the public in the eye on pages 416-7-8 of
llazell’s Annual for 1912. The verdict of
the British Freetrade authority was that
wages in leading trades were higher in the
U.S. than in England by 232 against 100—
m other words, they were more than twice
as high.. Rents in the U.S.A. were higher by
209 against 100, which wasn’t so bad a dis-
crepancy by a good long way. The retail
price of a quantity of food, including every-
thing a moderate-sized family might" require
for a week, was higher in the U.S. by 138
against 100, which was a hardly noticeable
discrepancy. The Yankee person might pos-
sibly reduce his cost of living to the J. Bull
figure by adopting the Bull policy, but as
he might then get down to the Bull wage
limit, he doesn’t think the proceeding worth
the candle.
* * * *
Some day, it may be. the cost of living
will be nailed or clamped down, though how
that will be accomplished it is hard to say.
The deed has never been done yet. The ex-
periment should, certainly be tried first in
I reetrade Britain, which, according to the
people who are protesting so violently in
the Australian press, should be an ideal
place for the experiment. Then this coun-
try could hang back and watch results. In
Australia nothing has happened to the tariff
during recent years except its reduction; it
has been reduced by granting very extensive
preferences to the Ma Countfv, and many
articles which were formerly dutiable have
been put on the free list so far as the Ma
Community, is concerned. It is alleged that
this, reduction of duties has been accom-
panied by an increase in the price of exist-
ence both here and in England, and the Syd-
ney Freetrade push demands a further 're-
duction of duties to obviate the increased
expense which followed on the last reduc-
tion. All these matters involve a heap of
doubt. When the theory is advanced that
the sure road happiness is to fol-
low the political methods of Whitechapel
and Shoreditch in London, and the Cowgate
of Edinburgh and the St. Rollox district of
Glasgow, it might be well for Australian
citizens who escaped from these hells, and
who still remember them, to tell their bre-
thren what, the places and conditions are
like. Nothing is gained by an ignorant and
untravelled section of the communitv rush-
ing at things with both hands extended and
its mouth open.
ENGLISH.
The Doctrine of Depopulation.
King O’Malley, the Minister with the
illimitable cowboy hat —provided the his-
toric article isn’t yet worn out —made some
remarks last week about the unemployed,
or alleged unemployed. The explanation of
the man with the Tile of Ages was that
immigration is the cause of unemployment,
and that if there was no immigration all
would be well, and everything in the garden
would be lovely. Supposing this to be true,
it is interesting to inquire what kind of a
country King O’Malley and co. think they
have made of Australia. By the King’s
own account it has become, under their
guidance, such a miserable lost dog of a
place that the present population of much
less than two persons to the square mile is
all that it can carry, and the arrival of a
few immigrants is calculated to make the
industrial ship tip up dangerously and heel
over in a hazardous fashion. Certainly-
Saxony has over 800 persons to the square
mile, and Belgium has 600, but then they
are not governed, in whole or in part, by
any bounding political mountebank in a
cowboy hat. It is about as certain as any-
thing can be that when a country has less
than two persons to the square mile, includ-
ing women, children, cripples, oldest in-
habitants, lunatics and the like, it hasn’t a
force that can even pretend to be reasonably
sufficient for purposes of defence. It is a
mighty poor country that can’t feed enough
people to defend it —such a very poor coun-
try as to be hardly worth defending. And
that seems to be, in the King O’Malley
view, the sort of country that Australia
amounts to. In the circumstances it is a
pity that the great O’Malley doesn’t leave
for some better land —one more suited to the
fertility of his imagination and the richness
of his vocabulary. For either Australia is
naturally not worthy of him, or else he and
his colleagues have made it artificially un-
worthy of anybody. Yet, curiously enough,
this country which allegedly can’t support
any more immigrants, professes to be able
to buy and pay for £75,000,000 worth per
annum of foreign goods, representing the
work of at least a million toilers. That is
to say, it can support 1.000.000 immigrants
so long as they don’t immigrate. If the
country had a larger population and was
better developed it could presumably support
more. When the action of King O’Mal-
ley’s tariff is set against the word of King
O’Malley’s mouth, the inference is that the
convictions of King O’Malley run some-
thing like this: It is feasible for Australia
to-day to maintain at least 1,000,000 more
people provided they are not hero f
taxes to Australia, to help defend Aw
and to supply handy and conventnJ ralla '
tomers for the Australian primary nr s CUs '
if these people come herT»“LS"'„T
to pay taxes here, to defend their
land, to supply adjacent customers fn P^ d
primary producer, and to do here
the same work as they are now fi*
abroad, and to do it for exactly the
customers—well, it seems that King o’\i“ e
i,ey and some other parties have ma<lp
a hash of things that the wholeSlT-
impracticable. At least that seems in i! s
O'Malley’s definition* of T
He holds, apparently, that local industry
will ruin the country—that if looooon
people make goods for Australia abroad thi.
continent will be all right, but if they i
here and try to make the goods here it Si
be all wrong. But perhaps, after all it ;
merely a case of O’Malley swiping ’wildly
with Ins large hat at the blowfly of tiro ,m
stances in the effort to divert attention fm™
the tariff muddle. The tariff holds the h.T
ance so very unevenly between high W n i
wages and low wages elsewhere that chenn
labor goods are now coming in at the „
£75,000.000 worth per annum. This coun
try badly wants the people who make thesp
imported goods—it wants them as citizens
as taxpayers, as customers to the local
farmer, and as defenders. It is always bet
ter that the man whom you pay to make
your clothes and boots and hats should be
your own man who will shoot with you in
time of trouble, not a stranger who will
shoot at you with cartridges bought out of
your own money. But if these artisans are
transferred from the low wage to the hieh-
wage side of the tariff barrier they threaten
to become paupers—dependents on public
charity. That isn’t their fault, for they are
as good workers here as in any other coun-
tryit is the fault of the fool of a tariff
barrier. Incidentally, it is the fault of the
fools who are responsible for seeing that the
barrier is in good repair. And one of them
is O’Malley —the man who believes in keep-
ing the country empty rather than shutting
out cheap labor goods.
Patent Medicines and the B.M.A.
“Baokblocker” outs forth his more or less
reliable view of the case :
Touching The Bulletin’s assault upon “Secret
Remedies” (20/2/’l3). The pamphlet which made
its appearance in the Australian Parliament, the other
day, was a flagrant piece of special pleading, and like
all such efforts it consisted, to a large extent, of over-
statements of the case. Admittedly, many of the
proprietary medicines do much less ‘than is claimed
for them. Some do nothing at all. But many are of
value to the community. The hostility of the British
Medical Association is not a manifestation of altru-
ism, and a high concern for the bodily and financial
welfare of the struggling masses, but of the business
spirit. The proprietaries have, for years, been com-
mitting the cardinal offence of underselling the doc-
tors and their jackals, the chemists. As an example,
in the good old days of not so long ago, if a man
over-ate or under-exercised, and, as a result, got out
of order internally, he had to fall back on a doctor.
The doctor gave him a prescription with calomel, or
something similar, in it, charged him a guinea, and
passed him on to a chemist—who, in many cases, was
the doctor’s own chemist, inasmuch as he managed
the shop for the former. The chemist’s price for mak-
ing up the prescription was anything up to ss. To-
day, the citizen whose internal organs jib slightly in-
vests in a shilling box of Blank’s Liver Pills, and
does himself as much good as his predecessor did at
25 times that figure. In fact, he is likely to do
himself more good. A sensible man knows his own
constitution better than any stray doctor. It takes
little experimenting for him to understand which of
the many proprietary medicines suits his peculiar
apparatus best. Thereafter, no doctor will provide
him with a better cold cure, or indigestion banisher,
or mosquito bane, or corn remover, or sunburn and
chapped skin eradicator than he can buy for a paltry
sum at any chemist’s. The doctors have been realis-
ing this, more and more clearly, for a long time
past. So have the chemists, who make no more pro-
fit out of selling patent medicines than the publican
does out of selling good whiskies and sodas at six-
pence apiece. And hence the B.M.A. pamphlet on
which the Australian Parliament and The Bulletin
have set the seal of their approval.
Take one of the remedies explicitly mentioned —I
mean Zambuk. In the first place, the proprietors
claim that the analysis of their cure, which is given
in “Secret Remedies” is incorrect—the two chief and
most costly ingredients being left out. But setting
that question aside, the thing is unquestionably a use-
ful remedy for burns, scalds, cuts, abrasions and a
dozen other mishaps of the sort. Poor people can’t
afford to go to a doctor every time one of these mis-
fortunes smites them. And if they did, about the
only difference between the ointment he would give
them and the proprietary-made article would be in
the matter of price. So they keep a tin of Zambuk,
or one of the dozen other like healing ointments that
are on the market, in the house.
Very often these patent concoctions are composed
of the best of drugs. The explanation is that the
difference in price between the best and the worst
is not great, and where you are dealing wholesale it
pays to give the best. Chemists’ shops differ in
virtue as widely as pubs. The prescription which, if
made up in (say) Paddington, will be mild and
anaemic, may, on being handed to a Balmain practi-
tioner, be resolved into something with a kick like a
mule. Some chemist’s drugs are good, some old and
fly-blown and bad. The public takes a chance always.
Proprietary medicines are nothing if not uniform.
You get the same goods, in this line in Timbuctoo or
Pekin, that you do in London or New York.
Before coming to a final decision on this question
it behoves the public to appreciate the real cause of
this sudden outburst of virtuous rage, against the
proprietaries, on the part of the B.M.A. If it > s
established that the whole uproar emanates from sor-
did motives of gain, it becomes clear that a lot of the
evidence is more than likely to be tainted. I sav that
financial considerations are not only the chief, but
practically the whole basis of the row. The doctor
has been charging big money for his services, wher-
ever he thought it was obtainable, and the chemist
has been putting an extortionate figure on his wares.
Wherever prices are absurdly high, in any generally-
demanded line, someone may be relied on to come
along and cut them down —to the fury, naturallv, of
the monopolists. The doctors’ union (the 8.M.A.)
and the powerful, though unwritten, understanding
that exists among the chemists makes underselling a
practical impossibility in their ranks. And so the
patent medicine man has come on the scene.
A Matter that Matters a Lot.
Melbourne Argus reports at length a lec-
ture by A. Lewis, organiser of the Victorian
Carters and Drivers’ Union, on “Freetrade
or Protection —Does it matter which?” Mr.
Lewis arrived at the conclusion that it
didn’t matter 2d., or less —but unfortunately
the Argus omitted to correct him, where-
fore this writer draws his attention to a
point or two. At first glance the question
may not appear to matter much to a carter
or driver, though in reality it does —a whole
lot. But some of us don’t cart and drive;
and we want interesting, intricate, skill-
demanding occupations—shipbuilding. , eu "
gineering, steel rolling, weaving, spinning
and a hundred others —and we don’t and
can’t get them under Freetrade. Besides
being more attractive, these occupations are
better paid than carting and driving, and a


man of brains and energy has a chance in
them to rise to a height of something over
6ft. from the ground, which is about the
limit of a carter and driver’s leaping capa-
city. Anyhow, the tariff matters a lot even
to the carters and drivers who escort coal-
lorries across the landscape. As thus: The
Commonwealth now raises roughly £12,000,-
000 a year to keep it from insolvency’ and
in future it will have to raise ever more and
more. The genuine Protectionist proposes to
raise a mere trifle of this through the tariff
—he knows that if all the manufactures that
should exist in a country are in a thriving
condition there will be comparativelv few
imports to tax, but that vastly more wealth
will remain in the country on which, by in-
come, land and other taxes, he can levy' toll.
The carter and driver person, like all who
cannot afford it, will pay very little, if any,
of these taxes. The Freetrader, however,
proposes to raise, his £12,000,000 and more
by a revenue tariff, under which the carter
and driver will pay a good deal. He will
pay nearly if not quite as much as the rich
man on his food and his clothes, and he will
in addition help to pay the rich man’s share
when he pays the rent for the rich man’s
house in which he mostly lives. He will pay
vastly more under Freetrade than he will
under Protection, but he won’t get as good
wages out of which to pay it. The Argus,
of course, knows all this perfectly well, but
it is not exactly the carters and drivers’ ad-
vocate, and naturally keeps silent about it.
The Perished Explorers, Likewise Those
Who Did Not Perish.
From a pressman on the spot:—
The surviving members of Scott’s Polar ex-
pedition who have returned to Maoriland are firmly
locking in their bosoms, or wherever the repository
for these things is, some interesting, and, no doubt,
sensational evidence. Commander Evans and his
men have been besieged by newspaper scribes, but
outside the official story little has been gleaned.
But as every little helps to clear up this Scott ex-
pedition muddle, I pass up this, • gathered despite
Commander Evans’s instructions to those on the
Terra Nova. One thing a considerable number of
people would like to know is more about the death
of Petty Officer Edgar Evans, to whose physical
disabilities on the journey back from the Pole has
been ascribed the subsequent tragedy. This seaman
had bad luck from the start. He did not get away
from Lyttelton in the Terra Nova when the expedi-
tion first started for the South, but he caught her at
the Bluff. If he had missed her there he would pro-
bably be alive to-day. So might Scott and Oates
and Wilson and Bowers. Also they might not. It
it persistently rumored (or was when I was put on
to the job) that Edgar Evans went mad just after
the Pole was reached, and that he was sledged by the
remainder of the party for 300 miles before he died.
Likewise, there was an accompaniment of gruesome
details which would turn a lot of the limelight from
the “deathless heroism” part of the business if it
could be substantiated.
Commander Evans flatly contradicts many oi these
statements, and is at a loss (he states) to know how
they originated. When reminded, that where [here
is smoke there is often fire, he simply shrugged his
shoulders, and adopted a “help-yourself’ attitude. It
is hinted that the records handed out may not have
been strictly accurate, items being omitted
or amended. *When the rescuers under Dr. Atkinson

he appeared to have endeavored to record the hap-
penings of the party right up to the end. His sleep

’ h le , they were shut up in their igloo, although thev

confidence grew that Scott, when he returned from
the Pole, would see to their succor ! The food which
was discovered in the depot left by Douglas Maw-
son undoubtedly saved the lives of most of the party,
if not all. It looks to have been a gloriously muddled
business all through.
Finger-prints as Hanging Matters
The trial of one Harold Thompson for the
murder of Arthur Trotter (Macßobert-
son’s traveller), in his house at Fitzroy, Mel-
bourne, ended in a verdict of Not
Guilty, and the Fitzroy murder remains
on the list of crimes which have baffled
the unintelligence of Melbourne’s de-
tective force. For the melodramatic arrest
of Thompson in the first instance, the one
and only excuse was the similarity between
a photo of his finger-print, already in the
possession of the police, and a photo of one
particular finger-print, discernible among a
number of prints of fingers belonging, appar-
ently, to different hands, which had been left
on the sill of a back window of the mur-
dered man’s house. And for the subsequent
trial of Thompson on the murder charge,
this one particular print was the only justi-
fication. The man himself differed in age,
height, build and voice from the murderer of
whom Mrs. Trotter gave a fairly good de-
scription. She stated that a coat and hat
found at the prisoner’s lodgings reminded her
of the murderer’s outfit, but could not swear
to both coats being of exactly the same colors
and pattern. Moreover, it was clear that
the clothes of the slim, boyish murderer she
described would not have fitted Thompson’s
powerful figure. There was no evidence of
the prisoner having been near to Trotter’s
house on the night of the murder, or having
ever seen the house in his life, until the
police took him there after his arrest, with
the wild hope that he would tremble and turn
pale. He cheerfully admitted to having been
at a pub, half a mile from Trotter’s house,
up to 11 o’clock on the night of the mur-
der, and he gave a convincing reason why he
had happened to visit that pub. Leaving
there he had “knocked around,” he said, and
then gone to his lodgings and to bed, and
was sleeping the bad man’s sleep beside his
unlawful girl at the time when Trotter was
murdered. The only evidence, worth the
name, that the police could bring against
Thompson was the likeness of his finger-
print to that of the photo of the unaccount-
able print on the window sill, sawn off by the
police expert before the photo was taken.
When in gaol, awaiting his trial, Thompson
asked for another finger-print to be taken and
photoed. This latter differed considerably
from the previous one (which had dif-
fered more or less from the photo of the im-
pression on the window sill), but the police
alleged that he had been detected, at a dis-
tance of 15yds., in the act of rubbing his
finger against a wall behind his back. The
rubbing, if Thompson did it, would probably
account for the difference in the two photos
of his finger-prints, but the supposition
that he had tried to effect a difference merely
suggests that he may have been doing his
best to save himself from being hanged on
evidence which no conscientious citizen
should hang a dog on. The suspicion that
Thompson tampered with his finger in order
to bring the murder charge into derision is
no sort of evidence that he killed the late
Arthur Trotter. But the police advance
the alleged tampering as a suspicious, cir-
cumstance, and, strangely enough, neither
the prisoner’s counsel nor Judge Madden
suggested that it was a quite natural thing
for an innocent man in Thompson’s posi-
tion to have done. Had Thompson made a
statement in his own defence from the dock
he would perhaps have confessed that he
■was “trying to beat the cops,” or words to
that effect, in their attempt to hang him by
one finger. However, he was advised to
make no statement, nor did his counsel call
any witnesses for the defence. The police
evidence was left to prove its own weakness
to the satisfaction of the jury, with the re-
sult that the detectives were duly convicted
of having made another “bloomer. All the
unavailing talk about a mysterious finger-
print in this case should draw the attention
of the Law and the public to a most import-
ant defect in the system of procuring finger-
print evidence. From first to last the only
witnesses are police experts. The print
whereby a prisoner is convicted does not, as
a rule, exist within the knowledge of any-
bodv but the policeman who discovers it. In
the Thompson case, for instance, a police-
man said, “Ha! Ha! prints on a window
sill and then sawed the wood off and took
it away with him, as he would have taken a
pair of strange boots away if they had been
left on the sill by some person unknown.
But the strange boots would be within the
knowledge of the people of the house, where-
as the barely perceptible finger-print is a
matter of police detection only. The ex-
nert’s statement that he found a certain
Anver-print at a certain place is uncorrobor-
ated Only a police expert is prepared to
swear that two different photos represent
the finger-print of one and the same nerson
And he swears easily. For instance, Expert
Potter of Melbourne, admitted under cross-
examination that the photo of Thompson’s
alleged print on the window-sill was dis-
similar to the photo, of his finger-print taken
in the detective office,, “so far as measure-
ment between any tico joints was concerned.
Yet he swore hard, and the other police ex-
perts swore hard, that both Prints had been
made bv the same finger. The science of
identification which is controlled by such
professors as these is a dangerous science for
the Law to dabble in.
Our Lethal Milk Supply.
This paper has frequently remarked upon
the rabid Socialism which characterises the
alleged anti-Socialists in the multitudinous
Parliaments of Australia. With the excep-
tion of a few fanatic incorrigibles of the
Bruce Smith type, they devote three-parts
nf the time they have to spare from assailing
Socialism to an eager chase after the so-
called Menace. There is one occasion, and
one only, on which the Liberal may be relied
on to assaiilt Government Interference good
and hard, and do all in his power to check
it; namely,. when an election draws nigh,
and the activities of the State threaten the
license of the elector to do what he dashed
well pleases. Then the anti-Socialist can be
one of the most consistent politicians going.
For example, at the Berry (N.S.W.) Show,
the other, day, M. F. Morton, M.L.A., re-
marked, amid loud applause from the assem-
bled cockies, that “there was far too much
Government interference with the dairy far-
mers. They were quite capable of carrying
on without anything of the sort.” This is
the variety of guff that wins votes from the
selfish, the ignorant and the unprogressive,
also from those who put a low price on
human life. To persons who know it makes
scant appeal. If the short-sighted cocky
could only get rid of a fragment of the men-
tal astigmatism which now afflicts him he
would, see that, in the long run, the ruthless
imposition of the State hand on his business
is the best thing for his pocket, considerations
of decency, etc., apart. In any case, it is
indisputably the best thing for those of him
who are moderately straight and honest.
Dr. Bobart, of Nowra, put the position
tersely at a Show meal in his district last
week, as follows :
We were being beaten on the London market, which
was the criterion of butter prices, by little Denmark,
which was but half as fertile as Australia. There
was no country that was more Government-ridden than
Denmark —more approaching to Socialism —yet it was
the best in butter production. There were stringent
regulations in Denmark in regard to the maintenance
of dairy herd standard. If a man had not a good
herd there, he had to cast it away ; if he did not,
he would be sent to gaol.
The trouble in Australia is not, as the Lib-
eral Parliamentarian invariably discovers
about election time, too much Government
interference with Private Enterprise, but not
enough. It is especially inadequate in the
milk trade. A day or so ago Dr. Clubbe, a
Sydney doctor who is by way of specialising
in children’s ailments, let fall what one of
the local dailies termed “a bombshell” in
his evidence before the Food Commissioners.
He announced that the Sydney milk supply
was “universally bad.”
“I will undertake to'say that in much of it there
is a higher bacterial count than in ordinary sewage
matter,” observed the doctor. ‘‘The cost of obtaining
clean milk would not be great, but at present the
men engaged in the industry are ignorant of the first
principles of cleanliness. If we doctors operated to-
day on the same principle as a century ago, when
we knew nothing of germs, we should fail. Yet
milking is done on the same principle as a hundred
years ago. In summer weather it is unsafe to feed
children with Svdney - milk. Sydney will never get
an improved milk supply until there is a bacterial
standard; and until the producers are educated to
realise the importance of cleanliness.”
As the Nowra practitioner remarked, Aus-
tralia, with all its advantages, is habitually
beaten in the butter market by Denmark.
The reason is Denmark has a better, because
more stringently State-superintended ar-
ticle, to offer. He might have added that
the supplier of indifferent milk is habitually
beaten in the local market by the man who,
though the State doesn’t compel him to do
so, takes particular care to produce only the
cleanest and the best. There are, fortu-
nately, on the South Coast, as in other
dairying centres, progressive farmers who
laugh the hollow laugh of scorn at the idiotic
“standard test” for milk, now in existence,
and have no truck with anything that does
not give a generous percentage over the test.
They are, in every instance, the most pros-
perous of their kind. They scoop the prize
boards at the different Shows, and bank big
monthly cheques for their milk. The repu-
tation of their herds is profitably high. One
farmer of this type in the Berry district re-
cently sold £l5OO worth of Young Holstein
cattle in four months. His brother bought
a three-year-old Ayrshire bull last year, held
it for six months, and then doubled his
money. In brief, there is profit in clean
dairv-farming. But not every dairy-farmer
will be clean, unless under compulsion. And
hence fhe need for State interference with
him. Such a practice is no hardship as re-
gards the desirable type of cocky. In the
case of the other sort, no consideration should
be shown, even by “right-thinking Liberals”
—no more than is shown to poisoners and
similar malefactors.

The Sydney rockchoppers, whose trade
motto is—or should be—“A short life and a
dusty one,” have just made some fresh
claims, and the Contractors’ Association is
entering the usual protest. At a meeting
of the Association, held last week, one
Gough is reported to have said (S. M. Her-
ald, 21/2/T3), “a great deal of capital had
been made by the men out of the unhealthy
nature of the work, but in his opinion the
reports were exaggerated.” The Lone
Hand magazine, in the issue for July, 1909,
printed an article on the rapid and steady
drift of the rockchoppers towards the ceme-
tery. After interviewing a few poor wrecks
of men in their sick-rooms, the magazine’s
representative went out amongst the jobs
to get the contractors’ side of the story. An
extract from page 317: —
Mr. A. Gough, who had two gangs engaged on deep,
open cuts at Leichhardt, willingly showed the writer
around the work, and gave all information asked for.
In reference to the words, “risk of disease,” used in
the course of talk, Mr. Gough said: “There is no risk
for the man that stays a year or two at rockchopping.
It’s an absolute moral. The deep, open cuts, Mr.
Gough said, “are even more productive of dust dis-
ease than the tunnels. In such works the men
breathed a constant atmosphere of dust. Look, he
said pointing to a cut of eight or nine feet m depth,
in which a row of men chopped at short intervals ;
“there is the dust, and that is what they take in
with every breath. Look at their faces and hair.
Called by name, one of the choppers lifted his
face which was white with the impalpable sandstone
dust that is indubitably the cause of this shocking
mortality among the sewer workers of Sydney.
This paper does not know if the Gough of
1909 is identical with the Gough of 1913;
but it has an idea that the 1909 Gougii had
the right hang of the subject. Perhaps the
Gough of 1913 can produce a mob of hale
and hearty rockchoppers who have spent
several consecutive years at the game and
don’t suffer from pneumokoniosis ( dust dis-
ease”) ? If so, he must be quite a different
Gough.
About the only decent thing in connection
with the unspeakable fezzed person who is
making a sort of last slipshod stand on the
Bosphorus is the tobacco which wears his
name, and the information comes with some-
thing of a shock that various Sultans did
their utmost to prevent smoking among the
Faithful. W. A. Penn tells us that when
the feeling against the weed was strongest
smokers were hunted down, pipes driven
through their cheeks, rolls of tobacco hung
about their necks, and they were taken out
to be hanged mounted face to tail on don-
keys. This was very horrible business, but
it was inspired by a tradition that Mahomet
had foretold the use of tobacco, and had
stated that its users would be infidels. Penn
further states that English sailors took the
smoking habit to Constantinople, but this is
probably a fable. As a fact, there is a lively
dispute as to whether all tobacco really came
from America, or whether Asia grew varie-
ties of its own long before there was any
America on the map. If the Chinese were
smoking about the time of the bang and
crash of Troy, as seems probable, it is
scarcely reasonable to think that the Central
Asiatic Turks got their first whiff from Bris-
tol or London.

Premier Massey’s land legislation of last
session, by which the Maoriland occupiers of
land under Lease-in-Perpetuity were allowed
to convert their holdings into freehold, is
already showing the black spots in its Tory
nature. In many mineral-bearing areas
Leases-in-Perpetuity were granted at rates
based on the interest at a small percentage
on the surface value. No mineral rights were
given to the leaseholder, but on the contrary
the prospector was given free entrance to any
part of his land and allowed to dig for gold
or coal or anything he fancied might be there.
Even the settler’s homestead was not pro-
tected, and a man with a miner’s right ap-
pears to have had the privilege, if he liked
to use it, of sinking a shaft under the draw-
ing-room sofa. But under Massey’s new
system the tenant of the State is allowed to
purchase the land with complete mineral
rights at a fractional increase upon the price
originally fixed as the capital value of the
surface alone. According to a statement
made by Joe Hanan, in the debate on the
Land Laws Amendment Bill, there is land
in the Auckland province with a surface
value for Lease-in-Perpetuity purposes of ss.
an acre, which has an assessed mineral value
of £5O or £lOO per acre. Such lands the
holder of the lease can now buy right out for
55., and every acre purchased represents a
big loss to the State. But then Massey’s
is a Reform party, which aims at making the
settler manly and independent.

“Montere” : “WArrawee” (B. 23/1/T3)
makes what he calls a practical suggestion
for getting more people to ride in the Syd-
ney trams. It is perfectly true that a great
many fares are never collected, particularly
in George-street during the lunch hour, but
the chance of winning a prize in a State
lottery would hardly smarten up the tired
conductors or induce the people who are in
a hurry to get fed to chase them with- their
pennies. On many of the lines the problem
is not so much how to attract people to get
into the cars, or even make them willingly
pay their fares, but how to carry the people
who want to be taken home to tea. Real
reform would lie quite in the opposite direc-
tion to “Warrawee’s” proposal. The Chief
Commissioner should be forced to recognise
that the expansion of the city is its only
immediate hope. Population chokes the
nearer suburbs and makes them a horror and
a crime against decency, and the population
is likely to stay crowded there because a
pennv is as much as the average citizen can
pay for his tram ride. By a readjustment
of fares all this could be altered. Let the
charge for riding over a single section be
made 4d., that for two sections 3d., for
three sections 2d., and for more than three
sections Id. In this way there would be
sufficient inducement for men to go far out
and live in good air amid wholesome sur-
roundings, and still more inducement for
those who continued to cling to the neigh-
borhood of the city to take a little healthy
walking exercise. Johnson told a deputa-
tion the other day that he must consider the
penny section people first because they make
the tramways pay. This means in , plain
English that he must consider the slum
landlord first and foster the congestion
policy. My scheme would burst up the slum
landlord in one act and do a lot towards
bringing down rents, and if the comfort and
health which would thus accrue to the
people showed as a debit balance in the
tramway accounts —well, the people own the
tramwavs, and health and comfort are worth
paying for.

From Norwegian Consul Pauss :
A contributor to The Bulletin (13/2/T3) makes
some sensational statements as regards illegal over-
loading of the Norwegian vessels engaged in the phos-
phate trade to and from the South Sea Islands. At
the request of the shipmasters, permit me at once to
contradict the statements made therein.
These Norwegian ships are chartered at a lump
sum per month to the Phosphate Company; conse-
quently it is not in the interest of the owners to have
their vessels overloaded, as neither they nor their
shipmasters benefit by it. The masters of the two
vessels engaged in the trade carry, moreover, their
wives, and naturally value their own and their wives’
lives as much as any Australian does his.
On no occasion has any of the ships’ crews, in-
cluding as they do Britishers, Germans and other
nationalities, made any such complaint at this Con-
sulate ; on the contrary, the same fellows re-engage
themselves time after time.
I may also say that I have the deadweight scale of
the vessel referred to in my office, and the figures
given by your correspondent are wrong.
The Consul may be quite right; but his
arguments are not. particularly convincing.
For instance: —
(1) These vessels are chartered for a lump sum
per month. This paper suspects that if an owner
shows that he can carry 1400 tons, he will command
a trifle more lump sum per month than if he can
carry only 1000' tons.
(2) This paper doesn't suppose any master expects
that his ship will go down and drown his wife and
himself. Still, ships do go down. And even captain’s
wives are drowned sometimes.
(3) There never was an overloaded vessel yet that
went out without a crew. The fact that a vessel can
get a crew doesn’t therefore prove that it isn’t over-
loaded.
On the whole, therefore, this paper suspends
judgment —all the more enthusiastically be-
cause Consul Pauss doesn’t dispute tin
other contributor’s statement that the Gov-
ernment Resident Commissioner [at the
Islands] took a hand and put some restru
tions on the manure-ships’ alleged tendency
to sink themselves to the neck og there-
abouts.
Gloaters.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Chief Secretary, Mr. Flowers, has finished
with the Rev. S. D. Yarrington so far as the con-
troversy about opium dens in and around the city is
concerned.
“I have no further time for Mr. Yarrington,”
exclaimed Mr. Flowers this morning, when the
former’s statement at a meeting of the Mission Zone
Fund yesterday was brought under his notice. “1
think he has been sufficiently advertised,” continued
Mr. Flowers. “There are others like him who need
a little advertisement. Mr, Yarrington ought not
to gloat over the fact that there are opium dens. He
should go to the nolice and inform them. It is a
matter for sorrow that he should gloat.”
—Sydney Sun.
The slily sparkling eye denotes
The meek and saintly man who gloats.
The hovering saint in glad distress
Floats round the den of Wickedness.
Wafted on blessed coat-tails, he
Spins round his find of Infamy.
Who drags him backward by the coat
A villain is who spoils good gloat.
And first-class gloat, ’tis very clear,
Is not as plentiful as beer.
Surf beaches still, you’ll kindly note,
Provide some most attractive gloat.
The holy ones, by tram and boat
Towards Manly go to do their gloat.
But those who ecstasy would own
Must do a special gloat alone.
So, like a ferret or a stoat,
The Wowser hunts for hidden gloat.
When found, like some tense pointer, he
Stands o’er his Great Discovery.
Then how he dwells on it and dotes !
Observe him now! He gloats! He gloats!... .
Now see we how he kept the trail
For maqy weeks o’er hill and dale—-
With red eyes glaring at the sward !. ...
This is his heaven —sweet reward !. . . .
Step lightly ! Do not clear your throats !
Don’t cough, or talk!.... A Wowser
Gloats! ....
The breed will quite die out, I fear
When placards shout: “NO GLOATING
HERE !”
N.S.W.

The Pitiful Plight of Certain Tools.
That peculiar person, J. T. Packer, secre-
tary of the precious Independent Workers’
Union, of Victoria, worked himself into a
state of great unrest or stew or something
the other day, when his blessed organisation
was turned down, and his organisers turned
out, by David Mitchell. Some months ago
David’s cement-making employees requested
an increase of wages for unskilled labor
to 9s. per day. He offered Bs., which they
refused, and ceased toil. Presently J. T.
Packer wandered along and assured Mit-
chell he could provide unlimited labor at
Bs., and Mitchell, having exhausted his
stocks, accepted, and struggled along with
1 acker’s clerks, shop-assistants, unem-
ployables, and sundries. Of course
this couldn’t last long, and Mitchell’
being latterly threatened with a boycott of
all his merchandise, climbed down last week,
conceded the 9s. claimed, took back as many
former employees as he could get, and sacked
I acker s pets”—with a solatium or honor-
arium of a few pounds per head, and the
otter of a job of woodcutting at Lilydale.
ibis action almost caused Packer to lose
his mental balance, and he raved at large :
He had made tools of the independent work-
Jtten to tide him over a difficulty, whereupon
he had thrown them to the scrap-heap,”
e £-’ e , tc L Next Mitchell’s manager
Ottered Packer a brief explanation—to the
effect that he (Mitchell) had expected the
Associated Contractors and the Employers’
Ifederation to back him up, but that
they declined to do so—also some briefer
and more forcible comments on Packer’s
expressed opinion of Mitchell ; after
which the Independent secretary assured
the public that he had misled him-
self, and that Mr. Mitchell was a per-
fect gentleman, etc., etc., and that the man
who was really to blame was poor John
Weary, who, as Minister of Labor, had de-
clined to grant the request of the Independent
strike breakers for a Wages Board in that
industry. Of course Murray is a tired and
sleepy person, but he was at least wide
awake enough to see the joke. These men
who had accepted conditions against which
other men had struck, had actually asked for
a board to arbitrate on the matter! But
I acker saw nothing humorous in the situa-
tion, and his dupes saw less when they found
themselves between the upper and nether
mill-stones. Still, there is that woodcutting
work available at Lilydale—fine, healthy,
invigorating exercise, at which a number of
men are to-day making a very decent living,
without strike-breaking or other unpleasant-
ness.

exasperation. Gray’s place in the Tas-

seat the other day. He is a kindlv, cautious
and conscientious man who has not touched
strong drink for half a century, and has

tical composition as a hot-cross bun.
