The Sentiments of “Singemoine” :
At the finish of the Scott matinee, on Saturday
afternoon, I found myself torn between conflicting-
doubts as to whether, on the one hand, I was a
Disloyalist and an Empire-burster and a Hound
generally, or whether, on the other, the theatre-
going public and the artists who cater for them were
devoid of any sense of proportion—almost I had
written of any sense of decency. Perpend. Out
there, in the illimitable wastes of Antarctica, lie the
bodies of ( aptain Scott and his colleagues. They
died well and with a gracious absence of bellow-
drama. Captain Oates, that typical Englishman of
the best type, sounded the bote. No sensational leave-
takings for him, no dying orations, no anything of
the cheaply emotional order. “I may be away some
time,’ he said simply, and strolled but to be frozen
to death. One nietures him as slightly embar-
rassed at being obliged to do so theatrical a thing
as die for his comrades. Scott, though probably in
CAN’T SHAKE IT OFF.
How often we hear persons with colds make the
above remark; but Dr. Sheldon’s New Discovery
shakes them off promptly and effectively in a few
doses. Price, Is. 6d. and 3s. Obtainable every-
where.*
a state of semi-delirium while composing his last
appeal, remained always captain of his soul. He did
not splurge. He was self-restrained to the end. To
such men the knowledge that their misfortunes were
to be used to wring maudlin tears from matinee
patrons would have produced an anguish keener
than death.
I have witnessed some banal spectacles in my time,
but the reading by a veteran actor of Scott’s last
literary effort struck me as the limit and dead
finish of everything. Slowly, agonisingly, what time
the myriads of females present sniffed in luxurious
misery, and fanned themselves, and devoured choco-
lates, the practised old elocutionist intoned those
poor, halting phrases. At intervals, he paused
effectively, and bit his nether lip, as stage strong
men do when battling with emotions that do them
honor. “He’ll break down. I know he will. He’ll
never finish it,” hissed a young woman near me ex-
citedly to her friend. If he had, the joy of 90 p.c.
of those present would have been complete. How-
ever, he didn’t. He gave it to us to the end. Then,
composing his features into one of those wintry
smiles which the ideal undertaker assumes on the
way back from the cemetery, he led on a popular
actress of other days. The happy thought had
occurred to someone of selling the message by auc-
tion. The lady held the document in a hopeful man-
ner, while a ready-tongued little man besought the
populace to bid for what he termed “this beautiful
poem.” A couple of theatrical managers fought
for it amid admiring cheers. One of the patriots
secured it ultimately, together with an invaluable
advt., for £5O.
Then the old actor got going again. In an impas-
sioned discourse he laid down the astonishing theory
that “all was well with the nation which was cap-
able of producing heroes like those they were honor-
ing that afternoon” (applause). “Let their noble .
ends be the answer to those who say that England
is degenerate.” (Deafening cheers.) It recalled to
me that other splendid non sequitur in “Milestones,”
where the youth who enthuses about iron ships is met
with the maddening query, “What of the wooden
walls of old England ? Aren’t they good enough for
you ?” Of course, there is no relation whatever
between the degeneracy, or otherwise, of a race and
the way individual members of it die. No explorer
ever passed out more gallantly than many of the
men and women of France’s futile and deplorable Old
Regime in the time of the Terror. Macaulay has
a special word of praise for the dignity with which
Nuncomar, that most corrupt and perjured of Ben-
galis, met his awful end. The Indians who were
blown from the mouths of cannons during the
Mutiny did not show the white feather in one case
out of 50. The Turk, low as he has fallen, still
knows how to expire creditably. Even the miserable
Korean aroused the admiration of his Japanese
assassins the other day by his fortitude in the pre-
sence of imminent death. Plain, incontrovertible
facts these. But Saturday’s audience at Her Maj-
esty’s was not the sort to assimilate them. In fact,
the citizen who had had the nerve to state them
publicly would have stood a good chance of being
stoned to death with chocolates and programmes.
The piece de resistance was a Christy minstrel
show. Blackened beauty-actors, in naval uniforms,
supported an interlocutor, several tambourinists and
so on. Why not have put the sable mariners in an
igloo and introduced a few conundrums about blubber
as an article of diet? The people who could stand
the entertainment which was offered would not have
jibbed at this perfectly logical development of it.
“SWEET COUNTY KERRY," AT
SYDNEY ROYAL
The villain's horse being faultily shod for the
race by Allen Doone, so that the play
may end happily.
The solitary item which struck one as more or
less in harmony with the painful event that was
being celebrated was Boucicault’s playlet, “Kerry.”
It is in an appropriately minor key, and it was ex-
cellently done by half a dozen good artists. J. O.
Williamson, in the title rOle, was superb. Mrs. T. H.
Kelly’s representation of the anguished wife was in-
stinct with pathos and charm. And Miss Lizette
Parkes —in brief, the thing was admirable.
But the whole well-meaning show was out of the
picture. Such methods of wringing coin from the
multitude are seemly enough in the case of a benefit
for a popular cricketer ; where the shadow of death
broods over the scene they are almost indecent. If
there is any vestige of an obligation cm Australia to
provide money for the dependents of deceased British
explorers, the deed ought, in future, to be done
partly by the Government and partly by voluntary
private subscription. In any case, there should be
no more of this ghastly business of doing a merry
song and dance on the luckless heroes’ graves, so to
speak.

If there had been more moving pictures
and a little less of Prof. David’s hard-boiled
heroics, the big show at the Town Hall on
Saturday night, given by the combined cine-
ma tocrats (Edwin Geach, manager), in aid
of the Scott fund, would have been more en-
joyable. _ Evidently David is convinced that
the public does not believe the following, to
wit, that: —
The world has acclaimed Scott and his comrades
deathless heroes, and rightly so. These men dared
the dangers of the Antarctic chiefly for the honor of
their country. They are exemplars for all time of
one of man’s highest attributes, the will unconquer-
able, to either triumph gloriously or to fall with
honor, etc.
If the estimable Professor was convinced
that the public believed all that sort of
guff, he wouldn’t be continually harping on
it as he has been for the past week or so.
If he would give a faint boost to the Rus-
sian dog-boy, jjimitri, who wanted to go on
his lonely and collect Scott (unless the cable
is a liar), it would be a welcome change.
With those few remarks it may be conceded
that the show was a credit to all concerned.
Making its trial Australian trip at the
Exhibition, Adelaide, is a remarkable ani-
mal-circus and "wonder show,” got together
in Europe by A. E. Martin and Rodgers.
From Germany comes Gannon, who weighs
7cwt. ; and Cornelius, who is Bft. high; and
Greta, who is about an inch shorter, but has
time. to catch up, being only 15 years old.
Against these can be set Smaun, from Bur-
mah, who measures 30 inches, and weighs
221 b. _ The notable thing about all these
people is that they look well on it, are nor-
mal in face, and in no way repellent. There
is a charming tattooed lady, done in seven
colors, as far as one is allowed to see —and
she is done much farther according to post-
card photos. There are various side shows,
including all the animals who presently
gather on the stage-—geese with a clown
driver, a “mind-reading” dog, a monkey that
shoots, an educated donkey, cockatoos, acro-
batic monkeys, and dogs which play a three-
act drama on their own. There are also
educated fleas, but these, for various rea-
sons, perform in a small room by themselves.

Adelaide King’s has a strong bill with
the delightful Risley-act Rinches foot-
balancing a crowd ; the Delinos on a wire ;
Leonard Nelson, who has got the house sing-
ing his choruses ; and the cartoonist Zif do-
ing an attractive turn. File picture houses
are proving that the public will crowd to
see unusually good work. West’s stage a
strong “As You Like It,” though Rose Cogh-
lan makes an absurdly mature Rosalind
The little Pav. has jumped to the front with
the Panama Canal series. Wondergraph still
relies mainly on the Lady Bathers, part at-
tractive and part unintentionally humorous.
* *. * *
“Brusher”: Howard Ashton has the walls
of the Athenaeum Gallery just now, and
lovers of the Streetonesque are trickling
freely into that much improved picture mart.
Recent alterations have banished the plat-
form (it can be put back when wanted for
lecture or concert) that cramped the dis-
playing ground, and have thrown a better
light on the subjects. Ashton’s success in
many of the small and medium-sized pic-
tures —there are 68 on the catalogue, and
all of them oils —is discounted by the failure
of his bigger efforts. No. 19, facing the
doorway, is a stiff and amateurish looking
study of autumn trees and smudgy grassy
ground, priced at 150 guineas. “Evening in
the Hills” (No. 20), a terribly painty freak,
is a work of the daring art that begins in
bluff and sometimes ends in poetic effect. The
effect is not achieved in this case. A number
of impressionistic treetops in the foreground
are the least pleasing part of “The Red
Roofs of South Yarra” (No. 22), which is
otherwise very remindful of Streeton. The
altogether satisfactory landscapes, with
more art than finished work in them, are
too numerous to specify, and it is in these
that the artist gives promise of doing greater
things presently. The effect is pleasing and
convincing, yet free from conventional pret-
tiness, as a rule. Two or three canvases
with unclothed female figures on them are
conspicuously weak. Figure drawing and
human flesh tints are out of Howard Ash-
ton’s line of talent. Of such things as “The
Bathers” (56) one may say that the artist
merely smudges in the beginning of a pic-
ture without offering the public any reason
to suppose that it would be much of a gem
if it were finished.
* s* % sjc
Two thousand persons of inquiring mind
crowded into Melb. Princess’s last Saturday
to look for reasons why an indigant prelate
had hurled a lovely ad. at “The Monk and
the Woman.” Later in the evening they
went away wondering what all the song had
been about. The Marlowdrama that opens
in a monkery and closes in a mild rainfall
on a dry, earthquaked stage contains no-
thing disrespectful to anybody’s ancient
Faith. Au contraire —as they say in Gay
Paree —the entertainment heaps butter on
Holy Church, and introduces the emblem
of the Cross as a practical means of pre-
serving the star boarder of the monastery
from sudden death, after which the Royal
Orangeman of the period is slaughtered "for
his giddy indiscretions, and the audience is
forced to the conclusion that piety is the
best policy. All things considered, Marlow’s
picturesque costume. drama was strangely
lucky to get a misguided ad. from a person-
age who didn’t know what it is loaded with.

Everything happens ages ago in “The
Monk and the Woman.” The scene of the
occurrences is understod to be France (al-
though the name of the country goes unmen-
tioned), and the amorous King who desired
the Woman who mashed the Monk who wore
the Cross that stopped the soldiers who
were about, to shoot is Henry of Navarre.
A most ridiculous monarch is Henry in the
play. He doesn’t know enough about reign-
ing to prevent the Woman he wants from
getting married to the wrong man under his
very royal nose. Every time he places the
Monk under arrest the holy man escapes
from custody and turns up in the nick of
time to thwart his evil designs. Henry and
all his male friends, traitorous and other-
wise, seem to be struggling helplessly un-
der the Church’s displeasure, whereas the
monks can’t go wrong anyhow. Even the
tedious comic relief monks, who deserve the
worst that could happen to them, live
charmed lives when the murderous soldiery
are about. This is in strict accordance with
the best Christian principles, nevertheless
the present writer wishes that the murderous
soldiery would draw their swords in the holy
calm of the first act, and cut out half of its
dreary dialogue. Changes in the cast, since
the drama boomed in Sydney, have placed
Richard Bellairs on the throne where Kay
Souper formerly met his downfall, and have
transferred the armor of Henri de Montrale
from Tom Lloyd to Bob Inman, who looks
supremely unimpressive with his stummick
clothed in tin. Vivian Edwards is a quite
Poetic brother Paul, and Miss Tanswell,
Miss Holmes, Miss Harvey and Miss D’Alton
are capable and decorative in their minor
parts. Miss Nellie Bramle.v, as in Sydney.

The Patty Frank troupe of acrobats,
which arrived at Melbourne Opera House
on Saturday, are as neatly entertaining in
the job of tumbling as they are in the art
ot building themselves into sky-scraping
towers. The pack is deserving of full marks
tor the smartness of its performance, while
the small tossed-about kid in the crowd gets
honorable mention for keeping his bones un-
broken. Two other new arrivals, Newman
and Hughes, shun the novel and original in
their cycling stunt. For the rest, the Beni
/.oug Zougs continue their riotous capers,
while Prince’s concertina, Miss Walmer’s
legs, and the colored crayons of Claude Le
l mstner give the programme its features
of Music, Poetry and Painting.
At St. Kilda fVic.) last week the Smart
Setters tried to chase Dull Care into the ad-
joining ” suburb, by presenting “Prairie
I ranks,” a .miniature musical comedy from
the frivolous pen of Edward Elliott. The
piece, filled the second half of the programme,
and its resemblance to real comic-opera was
early exposed by the snanning of the thread
of dramatic interest on which the musical
numbers were hung. The musical offerings,
however, scarcely suffered from the accident;
they were tunefully rendered by Walter
George, Oswald Preston, and Miss Georgie
Martin, and that they had no appreciable
bearing on the business of the evening didn’t
seem to matter.

Abdy, whose marriage to a lad named
Maurice Grant is reported from London.
This is her third trip to the altar. As Mrs.
Robinson, a merry widow, she caused some
excitement four years ago by espousing old
William Abdy, an immensely wealthy crank,
lie left her most of his money when he
died. Since then, she has been reported
engaged to somewhere in the neighborhood
of half the bright young men in London.
Her betrothal to Grahame White, the avia-
tor, was for some time regarded as an
accomplished fact. However, it fell through,
“QUEEN OF THE HIGHWAY ,”
MELBOURNE KLNG'S.
The Indian gentleman proposes marriage to a
lonely lady.
or never transpired, or something: The
other day she met an alleged prince on a
Channel steamer. He was young and comely,
so she asked him to her hotel in London to
have a drink. What happened then will
never be rightly known. She said he stole
her jewellery. To prove her bona-fides, she
gave him in charge, and had him prose-
cuted. He claimed that she had given him
the gauds to pawn. After some hesitation,
his story was believed. His position was the
more perilous by reason of the fact that he
was a bogus prince, and a person of ex-
tremely shattered means. She is a generous
old soul, and many a financially broken in-
dividual will curse her retirement into
domesticity, and pray that it may be of brief
duration.
AT POVERTY POINT.
Pontius Potter”: “Henri Singemoine” moans the
world-old moan of the elderly when he compares the
, ® Step, the Gaby Glide and other importations
with the ,skirt dance of the last decade (B. 6/2/’l3).
Henri s maiden uncle probably made much the same
remarks when the waltz and the joyful polka delivered
the death-knock to the extremely graceful minuet.
Anything from Salome to the Bunny Hug is better
than the dreary round of Irish, Scotch and other jigs ;
ami the Jelly Wobble beats the Sailor’s Hornpipe by
yards. But why import these revelries? As a
spectacular contortion the Murray Mooch or the
Jokook Twist would meet with much approval, and a
moocow cotillon would simply be the rage of a
continent. [“Pontius” is a poor judge of ages.
“Henri S.” is miles away from being elderly. He is
simply cheerful.—Ed. Bulletin.]
“QUEEN OF THE HIGHWAY ,”
MELBOURNE KING'S.
The faithful nag’s return minus his rider!
Sixteen faints on stage and attempted poison-
in; by dastardly Injun of the said noble nag.
Gaby Deslys, who was the ex-King of Portugal’s
most expensive lady friend, and whose tropical sketch
at London Palace was recently sub-edited and re-
clothed by the censor, blossoms forth with a lengthy
article in an English magazine. Gaby complains that
in England she is misunderstood. “When I appear
en pantalons the whole audience seems to hold its
breath. Why? My intention is to make it laugh.”
She explains that it pains her rather to appear in her
part of Mdlle. Chic, because in real life she is not
That Sort of Girl. And, naturally, not being That
Sort of Girl, she publishes various naughty letters
which she alleges she has received, including one
from a male stranger suggesting that thev might
spend a few days together in Paris. Of course, she
was shocked at that; yet she confesses that she has
no time for the Marriage Tie. Matrimony breeds
mutual cantankerousness; “but in the union libre
that we practise, two beings are as charming, one
towards the other, as possible, for rupture is easy and
immediate.” Still, let us remember that she isn’t
That Sort of Girl. She merely believes in marriage
and divorce being made so easy that either can be
effected at a moment’s notice without the interven-
tion of any silly third party. It is a simple creed.
You can pick it up almost any evening on almost
any pavement. By-the-way, the lady is often called
Gaby Deslegs, nowadays.
“One Who Pays”: Asche is easily the best Bottom
of the modem stage, building on the foundation of
Weir, of the Benson company; but Weir never had
the mind of Oscar. He plucks out much, also, from
that subtle and original thinker, Beerbohm Tree ; but,
again, Tree was not so personally gifted for this
character. As Caliban, I think Asche would be the
best the stage ever saw. Obviously, Rignold could
never equal him in Bottom or Caliban, though
I remember him as good in both. By the way, Asche’s
young-man actors talk exactly alike. Shut your eyes
and you will never know whether Penny, Worlock,
Hignett or somebody else is declaiming the blank
verse. This is not right. See how distinctive Asche’s
elocution is. One would never mistake his voice for
that of any other living actor’s. Kingston was the
first I heard with the unpleasant elocution of Asche’s
—like saplings thrown off the roof of the mouth. I
admire Grimwood almost all round; yet everybody,
from Asche downwards, is bad in the play scene. The
spirit is wrong. It ought not to be rubbishy bur-
lesque, but a study of poor chaps really trying to do
well.
“Cockaigne”: The newest London hit is at the
Apollo, with a farce called “General John Regan,”
by an Anglican clergyman, the Rev. Hannay, whose
nom de plume is “Birmingham.” The play begins
with the arrival of Billings, an American, at the
town of Ballymoy, in Ireland. Just for fun he asks
everybody: “Why have you not a statue to your dis-
tinguished native, General John Regan, the deliverer
of Bolivia?” The idea is so warmly taken up by
Dr. O’Grady (acted by Hawtrey) that Billings half-
believes he has blindly struck an actuality. The
whole place gets in a ferment; and the statue is
executed and inaugurated by an official from the
Lord-Lieutenant., From this flimsy material a
rattling good farce is cokered up, and it runs through
with uproarious laughter. The piece is bound to
reach Australia—with the tardiness of “Mile-
stones,” and following “Bunty Pulls the Strings,” in
the year nineteen hundred and fast asleep.
“Eryander”: About Gladstone and nigger songs
(B. 13/2/’l3). It’s hard to realise the G.O.M. as
uplifting his extremely “preachy” voice in “Dooda-
doo-da-day!” which is the chaste refrain of his
allegedly favorite “Camptown Races.” But sheer
nonsense is often acceptable to men of big brain-
tonnage. Herbert Spencer had a craze for comic
opera—-the more preposterous the better—and George
Meredith had an amusing knack of turning ordinary
observations into improvised operatic airs. Also,
here s from my own experience. The headmaster of
my one-time school was a large and lofty man, with
an awe-inspiring voice and a countenance of super-
natural austerity. He simply breathed classics and
mathematics, and no boy of us had ever seen him
smile. So, when I was sent to his study one even-
ing with a note from one of the under-masters, I
was electrified to hear through the closed door the
doctor s voice exalted in one of the Yankee non-
sense-songs of the period—“Kemo-kimo-da-ro-wa ;
smg-song, Kitty, won’t you try me, oh?” As my
knock had apparently not been heard, I opened the
door a few inches, and, lo! —the great man was
gambolling round the room to the august strain
aforesaid. He didn’t see me, and I fled, for I
didn't know what dreadful fate might be mine if
the great man knew that I knew.
Stargazer”: Barney Shaw is a great kidder of
women. In “Man and Superman,” for instance,
superman is woman, and the grey mare is the better
oss right through the piece. Her sex-instinct is
too much for the wit of the most intellectual animal
m the opposition ranks, Jack Tanner, and the life-
force works her way all the time. Here Pshaw is
as big a humbug as the most conventional drama-
tist that ever compiled jerry-built melodramas of the
Worst Woman brand for the West End market.
Tanner, wise in his other dealings, becomes a fool in
the hands of the heroine; Malone, senior, the hard
and astute business man, degenerates into a weak ass
the moment he encounters his scheming daughter-in-
law. All this is done merely to support Shaw’s
theories. Really, there is no more truth in the
philosophy of “Man and Superman” than there is in
the conventional commonplace sentiments, Shaw’s
views being voted “so true” because of the impudence
with which they are expressed as definite facts. Tan-
ner, when all is said and done, is only a mug in
love, and his scurrying about the earth in motors to
break away from Ann is arrant rubbish, like the
make-believe reluctance of a running hen. With
Shaw, truth is of small consequence; after all —
the play’s the thing.
“Harrison O.” : Bernard Shaw tells patrons of the
Kingsway Theatre (where “John Bull’s Other Island”
is happening) that “the more applause there is dur-
ing the performance the angrier I feel with you for
spoiling your enjoyment and my own.” I greet this
remark with loud and prolonged cheering. The habit
(for which there was something to be said) of hiss-
ing a bad play or rotten acting has fallen into
disuse; but the handing out of in judicious applause
becomes a greater curse as the whiskers on the face
of time grow in length and beauty. The popular
mime is met with an outburst of noise when he
makes his entrance. Upon a first appearance an im-
ported mummer, of whom little is known, and who
may eventually prove the press agent a bigger liar
than usual, is received with uproarious approval be-
fore he or she has spoken a word. In the case of
a female performer the senselessness is often re-
newed each time she enters in a new dress. Again,
any speech or observation which is sufficiently com-
monplace to make a direct apppeal to the collective
understanding of the house is treated as if it were
a Bruce Smith Empire Day peroration. In the case
of a decent comedy (when anything so irregular is
allowed to occur) this is particularly annoying, as
the large section of the audience which takes its
pleasures noisily mostly sees a point 20 seconds
after it is made, and a second flash is often lost be-
cause of the asinine disturbance. Of course, bellow-
drammers are specially constructed to call forth in-
terruptive applause, just-as old operas like “Faust”
and “Rigoletto” were so arranged that the Soldiers’
Chorus and “La Donna e Mobile” might be duly en-
cored. But it is as impertinent to interrupt a
Shakspearian performance with approving clamor
(and this was done during the recent run of “An-
tony and Cleopatra” in Melbourne) as it would be
similarly to break in on the death scene of “Tristan
and Isolde.” Wagner strenuously discouraged ap-
plause at Bayreuth, and in time the common sense
of this was recognised at Covent Garden, so that
interruptive appreciation of grand opera is now un-
known, except in the case of old-fashioned works of
the Verdi type. Apart from the fact that it mili-
tates against an intelligent appreciation of a per-
formance, indiscriminate applause adds greatly
to the length of a show ; so that it is nothing un-
usual these times for a “first night” to last until
after half-past 11. With the early-door rush, ap-
plauding commences even before the show, guber-
natorial personages and ladies, about whom there
clings an odor of illicit romance, being invariably
received with delicately modulated acclamation.
“S.T.S.” Almost pathetic to study the long list
of “entertainers” who try to catch the public eye in
the crowded lists of English papers. The singers are
generally “at the piano,” the humorists are always
“refined,” and all play as a safe card any past
engagement by a Lord Mayor or a Duchess. Among
names more or less known to Australians are War-
wick Pryce, “piano-entertainer”: Louis Nikola,
“magician-entertainer”; and Mrs. Slapoffski, “Aus-
tralian prima donna.”
DEADWOOD DICK PHILOSOPHY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Must Die! Title of latest detective-murdering film.
Mum and Dad and little Billy, also little Flo,
Crane their necks, and laugh, and shudder at the
picture show.
Twenty corpses in an evening is the usual thing;
13ut there are a lot of horrors,
Realistic, minor horrors
For their relishing.
Mum and Dad, when they were kiddies, twenty years
ago,
Had to suffer for the “Deadwoods” where gore used
to flow ;
Now with kids and silver laden, they skim up the
street
Just to see the vital fluid—
Same old brand of Deadwood fluid—
Flicked upon the sheet.
Bellowdrama, shilling “shocker,” shickergraph and
tract,
Morning, evening, Sunday paper—take it for a fact,
All of these and many others want for nothing more,
If they can but get a bucket
Half a gallon or a bucket
Of illegal gore.
Man is still a howling savage—woman is his gin,
And the blood-lust to the pictures draws them subtly
in.
When corpuscles cease to int’rest—when blood’s charm
has sped—
We’ll all be dead!

CONSTANT MURDERER.
BILLSHAKE IN THE BAR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They lounged in a tumbledown Western bar;
One was a manager, one was a star ;
The liquor they drank was a villain’s brew ;
But they were a copper-lined, fearless crew.
The manager held to the light his glass,
And watched the slow spirals of poison pass ;
It wavered like watersnakes full of spite.
He shuddered and swallowed it out of sight.
The star, with his heaviest limelight frown,
Like a hero swallowed his potion down,
Then boomed with a sort of unholy joy,
“Ah, to what base juices we come, dear boy!”

VOICE FROM THE D EEP: “Don't be greedy,
Hans; chuck us your wick! ”
There is something that calls for further
exolanation in the statement that, immi-
grants are responsible for an “alarming in-
crease” in the lunacy rate in Victoria. Dr.
Jones, inspector-general of the insane, re-
ports that Victoria is experiencing an epi-
demic of “pathological nostalgia,” exactly
similar to what prevails in the United
States, where the proportion of cases of
home-sick insanity among new-comers is
found to be “largest among immigrants from
countries in which the local lunacy rate is
not very high.” Granting that the assisted
newchum of to-day is apt to go melancholy-
mad as the result of new environment, there
must be something the matter with modern
civilisation. The Victoria of the gold rush
years contained no population to speak of
that wasn’t living under conditions harder
: and stranger in their newness than the ex-
isting victims of “pathological nostalgia
have ever dreamed of. The same truth ap-
plies, in a less degree, to new immigrants to
.America, as compared with the multitudes
of 50 years ago. Why wasn’t lunacy the
rule among early Victorians, who made their
dreary passage out to the Promised Land
on crowded sailing ships, and found no home
comforts when they got there? They must
have been made of vastly different stuff
from the assisted immigrant who is steamed
from Britain to Melbourne in six weeks,
taken in hand by a Government agent on
arrival, and sent to a more or less easy bil-
let to go “dotty.” What Dr. Jones has yet
to explain is the true inwardness of
“pathological nostalgia.” Perhaps some
explanation lies in the fact that the Eng-
lish immigrant of 1851 came from a country
with 308 people to the square mile, while
the one of 1912 comes from a country with
619 people per square mile. His brain may
have been flattened in the interval by the
pressure of population.
“Eryander”: Re scurvy and the Antarc-
tic. I have more than a nodding acquaint-
ance with the gruesome disease in question,
and I know that a man may have scurvy
for weeks without any more marked symp-
tom than “that tired feeling.” This would
account for the strange weakening of strong-
man Evans, and the malady in this stage
was probably present also with Scott and
the others, though the bodies showed no ex-
ternal signs of it. The usual cause of
scurvy is lack of vegetable acids in the
blood, and lime-juice is a specific when the
trouble is thus produced. But in persons of
scorbutic habit the disease may arise from
exposure and anxiety. I was once bashed
about the Atlantic for three weeks in a dis-
masted timber ship. Our cargo wouldn’t let
us sink, but our decks were awash all the
time, and, though we didn’t lack lime-juice,
three of the crew developed unmistakable
scurvy. One was knocking hard at death’s
door, and also ringing the bell like the very
devil, when we were picked up, but shelter
and safety set him on his legs again within
a week.
A dreadful thing happened in Sydney the
other day, and the Daily Telegraph was
quite right to be very upset about it: —
Rifle Clubs had to stand off the Randwick rifle
range on Saturday in consequence of all the targets
being engaged for musketry. Riflemen feel annoyed
that their arrangements should be upset by the en-
croachment of musketry.
That men who are in training for the regu-
lar work of Defence should need instruction
in the use of their arms is, of course, ab-
surd ; and no wonder the pot-hunting civi-
lian riflemen, who want to practise for some
of the prizes at the next matches, call it an
encroachment. Under the old State regime
the utterly unimportant business of training
the Defence force in the art of defence was,
thank Heaven, never permitted to interfere
with the innocent pleasures of rifle clubs
and other people.

“0.K.” : Cabled that one of the most im-
pressive moments of the Scott Memorial
Service at St. Paul’s Cathedral (London)
was when the names of the five deceased
explorers were introduced into the collect,
and the parson said, “We humbly leave them
in Thy fatherly keeping.” It must have grati-
,fied the Almighty to be told that a congre-
gation was content to leave Him in charge
of certain specified members of the dead,
without stipulating for any class distinc-
tions to be made between them! For it
seems that with Christian worshippers class
distinctions are strong even in thoughts of
death. At St. Paul’s Cathedral, in Mel-
bourne, the Dean of Newcastle (N.S.W.)
gave particular praise to the deceased ex-
plorers for their democratic spirit. . In his
memorial sermon he said: “Fine it is to
think they would not leave Evans behind
them. Though only a man before the mast,
he was their trusted comrade, and they
pressed on with the burden.” Dear bre-
thren. let us find comfort in the knowledge
that Evans has been piously confided to the
‘Lord’s fatherly keeping, though, as the Dean
of Newcastle remarked, he was only a man
before the mast.
* * * *
Those helpless dames on Yarraside who
have been putting down their names at the
Immigration Bureau as in need of an im-
ported Merely Sarah Jane or two, are be-
ginning to fight shy of the business, and the
registry offices and the Australian domestic
are more rushed than ever. It appears that
the Lands Department officials who super-
vise the immigration business hand most of
it over to the local John Hop—otherwise
John Cop—who inquires into the social, moral
and financial status of the family which
desires a fresh young English servant
or two. John goes to the butcher, baker and
others to find out how the family pays and
how it behaves itself, and everybody soon
knows that the police are inquiring. If
there is a family skeleton it is bound to
rattle its bones, and if there is a family
bottle it is certain to be smashed. So immi-
grant help is largely “off” in the homes
which possess a giddy son, or a tragic past,
or a prodigal daughter, or a wild bacchan-
alian aunt with vine-leaves in her hair.
Only the Wowser family remains to take in
the imported Bridget, and there she is likely
to find harder work and get poorer wages.
A DEADLY THREAT.
"Did your wife .threaten to go to her mother?”
“No! She threatened Is bring her mother here!"
Weird remark by Speaker Henry Willis
(N.S.W.) in a contributed article to The
Australian Journalist (21/2/T3). He
complained of unfriendly treatment by the
editor of the Wellington Dominion thus: —
I suppose if this editor of the Dominion were to
see me in Sydney he would hurl a brick at me. His
twin brother in the Sydney Morning Herald office
says he started his career with the Book of Common
Prayer and the Arabian Nights under his arm. Re-
cently this gentleman apologised to an old lady at
Woollahra for putting my name in print. She had
as “the mother of three children”.,lost many a good
thing from his pen as my name was on the same
page. I do hope it was not an extract from Burton’s
blue page of the Arabian Nights which Lady Burton
would not publish.
AN ILL-TIMED ADMONITION.
PARSON (praying outwardly): “Rend your
hearts and NOT your garments .”
MEMBER OF CONGREGATION (cursing inwardly):
"Confound it! I believe that is just what
I have done! ”
“Led by His Majesty the King practically
everyone in the vast Cathedral wept openly,”
said Adelaide Mail when describing the
Scott memorial service at St. Paul’s, Lon-
don. So once more the King led the way.
No base ruffian was so unmannerly as to
weep till the monarch got fairly started.
But it is also stated that: “Sobs of the re-
latives of the historic dead were heard above
the cadences of the hymn.” So, though
George wept first, it was the blessed privi-
lege of “the relatives of the historic dead to
weep loudest.”
The Yankee “religious en-
gineer” is the newest sort
of buzzer on the narrow
path that leads to the col-
lection plate. Two of the
species will arrive here
early in March, and Aus-
tralia is to be “swept”—
that is to say, the religious
calm will be broken, and
every soul that has not got
strong moorings down will
be driven on to the Golden
Shore. The shaky souls
that enjoy drifting help-
lessly before the “engin-
eer’s” wind will have a
great time, and will whoop
like glory when they go
aground; but they will in-
evitably be floated off again
by the spiritual spring tides
that are, presumably, the
work of the Devil. In a
marine way of speaking,
these revivalists never seem
to get the soul far enough

BARRICADES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I can’t get along the streets! — Shout of enraged
Sydney man.
They buried an electric main, and filled the hole with
clay ;
They mended all the asphalt path, and sadly went
their way ;
Then we, who had to take the road while others plied
their trades,
Walked gladly o’er the spot whereon had stood their
barricades.
But scarcely had the winds and feet dispersed the
sifted sands,
When up there came a band of men with shovels in
their hands ;
Anon, behind a barrier, the pave with picks they
broke.
“We gotter putter noo pipe down,” remarked the
foreman bloke.
Then, for a month, we took the road whene’er we
went that way ;
The simple job it lasted long—-from work they used
to stray
To eat a pot 0’ beer, or see the Arbitration Court.
(The tangled traffic round the job with blind umbrel-
las fought.)
They got it done at last. I think some shopman
made a row.
Then once again the road was cleared—l know not
when or how.
The wounded footpath’s jagged hurts were healed
with asphalt black,
And some brown hajid of mercy strewed fine sand
upon its back.
But so long had the barricades railed off the workers’
blot
That citizens like horses shied, whene’er they reached
the spot,
And only minds of habit free strode swiftly o’er the
scars,
Instead of running off where clanged the swift electric
cars.
For one short week or less, the path of railings has
been clear,
And timid folk walk straight along, and never out-
ward sheer.
But, see 1 beside the kerbstone wait some brown, tool-
laden men!
‘The time of grace is up! They’ve come to tear it up
again t

The rising of to-morrow’s sun is not more
certain than that Victorian Chief Justice
Madden, when sitting in divorce, will upend
himself and orate to the world at large, on
one-sided evidence. Thus, at the February
sittings, a Mrs. Kennedy informed him that
she and her partner had dwelt together more
or less peaceably for four years in Banana-
land, but on removing to Melbourne he toiled
awhile, then went up country to get work,
and that was the very last she had seen or
heard of him. She asked his Honor for a
decree, and got it. That was all quite regu-
lar, but there was no need for Mr. Madden
to notify the public that “this was one of
those wretched cases that were becoming a
curse to the country, where a man married
a woman and then walked off and enjoyed
himself, leaving her to work to bring up ‘his
children.” Certainly Kennedy walked off,
but there was no evidence that* he was enjoy-
ing himself, or doing anything at all, or that
he was even alive. He may have fallen into
a waterhole, or river, or perished from ap-
pendicitis, or been run over by a traction
engine, or train, or died of fever, or gone
nmd. or been killed by a snake, or a bullock.
He may have perished while in the act of
sending all his earnings to his wife. It is said
to be wrong to speak evil of the dead, and it
isn’t much better to speak evil of the pos-
sibly dead, when that person’s possible dead-
ness is the only charge against him. If
disappearance is a sure sign of villainy—
well, can Chief Justice Madden account "for
all his ancestors? Did none of them vanish
off the page of history in an unaccountable
fashion?
Victoria’s Chief Justice got quite angry
lately when he fell into a hole in the law that
he didn’t seem to be aware of. The matter,
of course, concerned a divorce case in which
the deserting husband was proved to be
living handy to his wife’s domicile. His
Honor was shocked to learn that he wasn’t
living alone, either, and after swiftly grant-
ing a decree he wanted to know why alimony
wasn’t applied for. Petitioner’s counsel, a
competent divorce lawyer, assured him they
couldn’t do so because they couldn’t prove
respondent’s income; whereupon Mr. Mad-
den wondered whatever the Legislature could
have been thinking about. The chances are
that the Assembly wasn’t thinking about
anything in particular, and that the House
of Review overlooked the oversight as usual.
In another case His Honor discerned a gap
that doesn’t exist. Here a husband had re-
peatedly assaulted his wife and child before
deserting them, and Mr. Madden wanted to
know why Parliament hadn’t given power to
order such men imprisonment or the lash.
Of course Parliament has given power to
order these penalties for various assaults;
but it hasn’t given the power to Judges in

From “Alma Mater”:
Sydney University has a suffragette movement, of
a sort, all -to itself. The burning que«tion there is
whether oV, not a woman will find a place on Car-
michael’s Senate. Chancellor Normand MacLaurin is
violently Hbstile to anything of the sort, and sugar-
expert E. W. Knox even more so. Professor David is
the champion pro-woman Senator; with Prof. Mac-
Callum a good second. When Mr. Knox was inveigh-
ing against the higher education of women one day
at a Senate meeting, he was asked whether, if he
wanted to learn anything about radium, he would
attend the lectures given by Madame Curie. In the
tone of one who had been charged with Disloyalty to
the Throne, he replied; “One—perhaps. I would not
sit under her.” That is how Mr. Knox comes to be
known in ’Varsity circles as “One—perhaps.” . More
even than a seat upon the Senate do the University
women desire to compete on equal terms for the
teaching positions at the University. A girl once got
a position by signing the application with her initials
only. A year later, when the Chancellor came to
confirm it (this is a mater of form, gone through
periodically in the' case of University appointments),
he sent for the Registrar, in a great state of mind, to
know if it was really a woman that was holding the
position. As she had done her work satisfactorily, it
had to be confirmed. In connection with University
extension lectures a woman was in the running to
give some on crystallography. The antis opposed it
strongly, but The pros won the day. Dr. Dalzell s de-
parture to the Lister Institute, as a holder of a Beit
fellowship, will leave a vacancy on the University
teaching staff which will also be hotly contested by
the pros and antis. # »

when the wearer is on duty, but swung tree,
like a full-grown sword, when ceremony
is on. Have visited warships of many
nations, and never found any of them just
it” for cleanliness except the British and the
Dutch but I must now join in the Japs.

T i Y ‘ Z r‘r : There are strict laws to enable
John Hop to deal with persons who in a
state of inebriety take charge of horse, ass,
or mule in a wheeled vehicle, but there are
none to assist the estimable Hop in coping
with drunks found afloat in a boat.
In eight cases out of 10 boating dis-
asters in these down-south parts are
more the result of the tempestuous wet
inside the. boat than the violent ele-
ments outside; and it is high time more
drastic measures were devised to prevent
seamen _ from going down to the sea
in skiffs, especially those incompetent mari-
ners whose inability is augmented by beer.

The blowing up of the new house of Lloyd
Ueorge, British Chancellor of the Exchequer
reveals a weak point of the suffragette move-
ment. The great burst might have been
ascribed to Orangemen or Tory peers, but
tor two circumstances: (1) The wrecked
premises were much pervaded by dropped
hairpins and hatpins; (2) ladies of the
Drummond and Pankhurst order couldn’t
avoid airing their vanity by declaring that
tne explosion was caused by members of their
party. _ >Vhen lovely woman goes throwing
her neighbor’s house at the sky, she should
leave no hairpin behind her, and she should
keep dead silence afterwards.
* * * *
There are very few Sydney people at large
who, being thirsty, would deliberately pass
by the innumerable pubs, tea-rooms and
soda fountains that line the streets, and lap
water out of a drain. But he is a very rare
citizen indeed who does not absorb milk in
some form once or twice a day. And much
of Sydney s milk is alleged by a well-known
doctor to contain “more bacteria than or-
dinary sewage matter.” So the staunch tee-
totaller who sucks soda and milk through a
straw, and the demure flapper who licks ice-
cream off a spoon are in deadly danger,
while the partly-collapsed drunk who merely
sits_ in the gutter is fairly safe. It is a
curious state of affairs, and Private Enter-
prise, the beloved friend of the S. M.
Herald, is the responsible party. Milk is
a Mghly-perishable product, which should be
fairly rushed from cowbail to breakfast
table. To ensure a reasonable margin of
safety, the cow of supply should be fairly
close to the jug of demand. But leaden-
footed Private Enterprise steps in and ladles
out far-travelled milk that has been stricken
with old age. A long drink of milk now
costs as much as half a yard of ale in a
public bar. And it seems to be steadily
foaming up towards the cost of a private bar
refresher. At first sight, this looks to be
very satisfactory for publicans and drunks;
but even these take milk in some form dur-
ing the long procession of the hours. The
purification of the milk supply is a vital
question in which all who are not already
dead are interested. Even the Private En-
terprise milkman might join in the crusade
for the reform of himself. There is a popu-
lar idea that no milkman ever drinks milk;
but he may one day take too much whole-
some beer and mistake a soda fountain for
a bar. In such a case, he would probably
die horribly in advance when he came to.
His knowledge would kill him.

H E: “But why have you thrown Harry over?"

The Circus came to town on Saturday at
Sydney to augment the fund fc r Scott and
party’s dependents. Led by a brass band
that would have set Wirth’s teeth on edge,
a procession of motor-cars rolled round the
city, while the actresses and othors aboard
and accompanying them did catchpenny
turns at odd corners. It was an easy job for
everybody but the fellow with the big drum
and cymbals or kerosene cans, or some such
devastating implements. If Charity began
at home with him he must long ago have
deafened the neighborhood. Later in the
day “popular actresses,” etc., switched the
circus off and put on a nigger minstrel show.
A mock auction was also held, at which two
well-known theatrical managers bid against
each other for a copy of Scott’s dying depo-
sition. The grief-stricken public parted up.

SAVING THE SERVANTS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Melbourne Age reports that the Victorian Lands
Department, which has the handling of immigrant
maid servants, sets the police oft' among the local
tradesmen to make a searching investigation into
the character, habits and antecedents of persons who
make applications for girls. The department particu-
larly wants to know whether the applicants are in
business, if they pay their debts, are good-living
people, are slow going or “cut a dash,” what the
tradesmen think of them, whether there is any
disease or mania in the family, and as more than
one constable construes it, “whether the old man
drinks or walks in his sleep.”
Do you want a smart Matilda
For your kitchen out at Kew,
Or your boudoir at St. Kilda?
Be most careful what you do 1
Turn your back on all temptation,
Be extremely circumspect,
Cut each frivolous relation;
In your person, faith and station
Be unfailingly correct;
For the State can trust its lambkins*
only to the true elect.
Should you fail to pay the baker,
Should you nark the butcher’s man,
Or neglect the undertaker,
Get a servant where you can !
Go to chapel with the missus,
Give the plate just what you should,
Kiss no unconnubial kisses,
Cut no dashes. Needful this is,
For we’d have it understood
That the maid may be a corker, but the
master must be good 1
’Tilda’s brought across the ocean
With great care, at heavy cost;
We must show her due devotion,
Otherwise she may be “lost.”
If you are a naughty person,
With a worldly point of view,
You might put an curse on
Little ’Tilda Ann Macpherson—
Your petition we eschew.
Any mere Australian hussy’s plenty
good enough for you 1

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
iaiTum : A N.S.W. school inspector,
"■ ho is a good Australian, the other day drew the
continent he loves so well, in outline, on the black-
board of an outback school. He asked the young-
sters what it was, but only the brightest boy in the
school had a notion. The bright boy said he thought
it was a rabbit.
He wandered into Briggs’s bar, where we
were liquidating on our way to the sale, and we at
once recognised him to be a brother joint of the
country s backbone. He asked rather anxiously if a
gentleman had inquired for him there that day.
Receiving a negative reply, he murmured, “That’s
strange. He said he’d be here about two, and it’s
half-past now.” He went on to explain that he had
met the gent in the township that morning before
the bank was open, and after a drink or two, obliged
him with the loan of a fiver, which was required as
deposit on a horse. The money was to be returned
at the pub at 2 p.m. We all expressed sympathy and
an indignation at the practice of such despicable city
tricks in our quiet hamlet. But the whiskered one
indignantly defended his acquaintance from our im-
putations, and expressed his unbounded confidence in
him turning up with the fiver all right. His sim-
plicity and confidence exasperated Briggs, who, scent-
ing more fivers about, offered To bet him even’s that
his client would fail to turn up. The bet was read-
ily accepted, and the other boys who asked for tick-
ets in the same good cause, were promptly accommo-
dated. Then we had a bob in, and while the drinks
were being served, I was horrified to see a well-
mounted and well-dressed “Johnny” approaching.
Dismounting, he entered, and cordially greeting our
hairy brother, said: “Sorry to have kept you wait-
ingi old man ; awfully obliged to you for your kind-
ness this morning,” and handed him a fiver. Briggs
passed over the stakes in dead silence, and whiskers
invited all hands to have another. At the races the
next week I saw two men of Galilee running a
thimble-and-pea joint. One was the “Johnny” and,
though whiskers and dungarees were gone, I recog-
nsed in the other the simple, trusting soul who had
struck Briggs’s pub just as the time w r as ripe.
“Fax,” from the Antarctic side of the
Murray: lie “Jersey” (B. 2/6/’l3) and impure milk.
The assertion that “a truthful description of even
the cleanest cocky’s method of milk production
would cause a commotion in the healthiest reader’s
stomach” may be true of N.S.W., but is a libel on
hundreds of dairy farmers throughout Victoria. The
cow cocky c f the older generation is sometimes illit-
erate and the boss sweater on earth, but dirty in his
dairy he emphatically is not. I live in the centre
of the biggest dairying district in Gippsland, and
can assert that 99 p.c. of the milking sheds and
dairies are constructed on the most up-to-date
hygienic principles, and that scrupulous cleanliness
is the rule, not the exception. With the co-opera-
tive manufacture and marketing of butter brought
to its present high level, the cow cocky has learnt
that dirt doesn’t pay. For instance, at the second
largest butter factory in Gippsland last year 98 p.c.
of the cream received was graded superfine; and if
“Jersey” knows anything about milk, except that a
pint jugful costs 2d., he will recognise that such a
record proves the almost total absence of all contami-
nation. Then as to diseased cattle, there is no reli-
able means of detecting tuberculosis in its early
stages except by post-mortem examination, the much-
vaunted tuberculin test being absolutely unreliable.
When the disease becomes advanced, the beast is
weeded out because the universal system of weighing
and testing demonstrates that her milk yield has
fallen below the profit mark. “Jersey” can rest
assured that in Vic., anyhow, dairy farming is a
business, and that indifferent milkers are not toler-
ated in a dairy herd. He may also be surprised to
learn that Victorian cookies pay annually 6d. per
head for the Government inspection of their cows,
and that the inspector has the power to destroy any
beast infected with tuberculosis or lumpy jaw. Give
the Devil his due; even if the cow cocky adds to
the mirth of the continent per Norman Lindsay by
growing luxuriant whiskers and waking up the
rooster, at anyrate he is clean.

“Pick”: “Vagabond” (B. 6/2/T3) offers
the blessings of a whole ship’s company to anyone
who can tell him and them how to evict a colony
of cockroaches. I don’t go much on sailors’ bless-
ings, as a rule; all the same, if “Vagabond” dusts
the cracks and crannies inhabited by his enemies
with the ash from burnt potato peelings, he will
scatter them like Wowsers fleeing from the wrath to
come.
In outback tanks and dams the loss of
water by evaporation is big enough to make a man
jump when the figures drop on him for the first
time. On the comparatively small tanks (surface
catchments) on the northern railway lines in S.
Australia, including the overland to Oodnadatta, the
loss is anything from 10ft. to 13ft. a year. The
Board controlling the big Goldfields scheme in Wes-
tralia prints some of its experiences in its annual
reports. The Chief Engineer shows that, while
1,150,000,000 gallons were pumped and 156,000,000
gallons supplied by gravitation during last year (to
June 30), 995,000,000 gallons were lost by evapora-
tion. This means that, even at the Mundaring weir,
which is in one of the most favored spots in Wes-
tralia, 50 per cent, of the water goes out through
the pumps, 7 per cent, by the pipes unaided, and as
much as 43 per cent, is taken up by sun and wind.
And the Board hasn’t yet found the address of sun
and wind to serve an assessment notice on the ex-
travagant couple. Of the rain that fell on the
catchment, it is estimated, by the way, that only
2.18 per cent, got to the reservoir last year. So of
every 10,000 raindrops that fall on even this good
catchment, only 124 reach the consumer. The other
9876 are lost en route.
* * * *
“Gouger”: Eel fishing has been brought to
a fine art by the natives of the Bloomfield River
(N.Q.). Sometimes the fish is speared. That is when
he wanders on the face of the waters ; but when he
stops at home unduly another procedure is adopted.
Binghi sits on a rock overhanging a deep hole, and
patiently drops into the water chopped-up pieces of
snake, gohanna or bird (snake for preference). The
tucker lures the eel to the spot, and it will even
reach up the rock a distance of 2ft. above the water
for more. Binghi promptly serves it out with a
nulla-nulla. Another method is to dive, accom-
panied by a short-handled, three-prong fish spear,
which, when the prospective meal is located by hand,
feeling along the ledges and crevices, is driven into
the body. The rest is easy. A great delicacy to black
brother is fresh-water turtle. There is one big
hole on the Bloomfield where I have seen 30 natives
mustered for the slaughter. Suddenly two young
men dive in, swim under the rock, and turtles, fish
and eels rush wildly forth. Instantly there is pande-
monium in the hole. Bucks, piccaninnies and old
men jump into the water after the frightened fish,
and to watch them struggling with a frantic turtle
or 3ft. eel is a sight to make a cinematograph’s
fortune. From this hole alone I have seen 60 turtles
hauled in triumph and loud applause.
“Pick”; Can any abobserver vouchsafe
reliable information concerning any genuine per-
formance by the latter-day black tracker? In the
days when Edward Kelly and his mob made things
dizzy for the Victorian police, the Queensland
trackers employed to assist in the hunt proved
their ability by following a trail which, at one
point, led across acres of bare rock, and at another
along the top logs of a pack fence, but since then
I have not heard of one Binghi who could do any
more in the way of tracking than could be done by
an ordinary bushman. Black trackers are still
largely employed by the police authorities in all the
States, but my experience prompts me to write
them down generally as frauds. I have known of
several cases where trackers have been put upon
bogus trails which they have followed with sublime
pretence for miles. Their favorite dodge is to
finally lose the spoor in some impassable thicket or
swamp; but it would be interesting to know how
often suspicion has descended upon an innocent per-
son through the testimony of a murky sham. A re-
cent case down the South Coast (N.S.W.) way could
be quoted for one.

THE CARAVANSERAI
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There grows a bush on western plains,
A hardy tenant of the sands ;
Scant tribute draws it from the rains
That skirt and shun the desert lands,
Yet, in its season, though the dunes
Are bare and harsh with constant drouth,
It glows with petalled semilunes
As sweet as any of the south.
To those who leave the peopled zone
And draft the mob, or hunt the pelt,
The tract of cheery brush is known
And charted as “The Flowery Belt”—
The optimistic plant that grows
And blooms, in plenitude or dearth,
Or any blighting wind that blows,
When other weeds are drooped to earth.
Faint in the air its sweetness bides,
From many nectaries outborne.
Wherein good fare each bloom provides
For film-winged waifs on ways forlorn.
A scented sign beside the road
To whet the taste and catch the eye,
As ever goodly hostel showed
Attractive to the passer-by.
And never dusty traveller felt
More thankful for the timely ease
Than farers by the flowery belt—
Wing-weary gnats and errant bees.
“Good fare for insects all,” it cries;
“Come, outcasts of the wilted grass ;
Here's succor for the tiniest flies!
Make merry, comrades, ere you pass.”
Small wonder that its fame should spin
When welcome waits each thirsty guest.
“A very passable good inn
Where gentlemen may dine and rest;
The cellars hold the proper thing ;
The tariff is extremely low',”
Says Burnished-back to Opal-wing:
“I recommend mv friends to go!”
What though these serve ulterior ends,
And honev-scented schemes unfold
For using skilfully their friends
To circulate their pollen-gold ?
But each is welcome at the board ;
And each is happier for his gains,
When guests and hosts are in accord
And none is loser for his pains.
When lone I ride across the sands,
I doubt if it should be my lot
To fare so well at human hands.
And meet a host that reckons not;
Or find an inn for human strays
From which all luxuries are dealt,
Whose signboards with such welcome blaze
As those along the Flowery Belt!
VICTORIA.
C. G. A. COLLES.

The late T. Bent’s famous remark about
some political opponent being unfit to carry entrails
to a bear (Bent, of course, didn’t call them entrails),
is remembered and treasured by all collectors of in-
sults. The Bulletin pointed out some time ago
that the clean, vegetarian animal would have fled in
horror from any politician so shamefully burdened.
An item from Ohio (U.S.A.) sort of confirms this
paper in its belief. A camp cook who was in danger
of being hugged to death by a bear, spat a mouth-
ful of tobacco juice into the forest thing’s eyes. It
immediately dropped the man with the awful habit
and disgustedly waddled away like a fat clubman in
a motor-coat. If the bear is to be dragged into any
future political controversies, he should be given due
credit for his undoubted cleanliness.
“Mallarapoonie” : “Gouger” (B. 2/1/T3 ,
on the subject of bats, reminds me that one of the
principal homes of the small nocturnal flier is
among the big tableland ranges of the Upper Clon-
curry (Q.), where the last of the great outliers of
desert-sandstone still stand in age-old evidence of
the huge pre-tertiary sheet that once covered the
greater portion of this continent. These old weather-
scarred monuments of an era long since devoured by
the Grasshopper of Time are wind- and rain-worn
into innumerable caves and hollows, which are, as
a retreat for bats, equal almost to a Parliamentary
recess for a harassed Government. I was prospect-
ing years ago among the mountains of the Upper
Fullarton, and had occasion to climb one of the old
desert-sandstone knobs in order to examine the face
of it for opal-bearing indications. I had scaled to
the lip of a great cave, and drew myself over the
edge of it with some curiosity and no little trepida-
tion, for I was justified in believing that I was the
first human being who had ever trespassed in that
aeon-old recess. The cavern receded into the cliff
for some 20 paces, and terminated in a kind of great
underlay pit that seemed to go down indefinitely.
As I stooped on the edge of it, peering into the
blackness, I thought I could hear the faint tittering
of a few bats. Purely as an experiment, I picked
up a boulder of eroded sandstone and hurled it out
into the darkness. I heard the rock whistle down —-
heard it strike, burst into fragments, and go rum-
bling into the bowels of the mountain. Three
seconds later I wished I hadn’t; for I was met in
the face by a blast of foul, leathery wings, as half
the maddened bats in Christendom hustled like all
Gehenna for the open air. So great was the bat-
tornado that it was only by crouching low to the
rocky floor that I was saved from being swept away
in the stampede of terror. In another cave, in the
same region, I came upon four others of the bat
tribe that were entirely new to me. They were
some 20 inches across the wings and of a beautiful
slate-grey, running to pink at the points, like a
galah. Search of the records fails to show that this
species has been listed. I believe them to be ex-
tremely rare. When camped within that region it
was astonishing the number of bats that came
around, swooping and darting at moths and insects
in the firelight. One night, I remember, two bats,
swooping after the same moth, collided in mid-air
and fell into the fire. They scrambled out hurriedly,
half-singed, and fled into the night, shrieking with
rage. I could hear them gibbering and chattering
volubly in the distance, and the language they used
was dreadful. The moth escaped.

“Kyalo” : We’ve been hearing a lot lately
about what a man and a horse can do, but how
about a woman and a horse having a touch of the
limelight ? Before the railway arrived, a well-
known horsewoman of the district, Miss Becky Fitz-
gerald, rode from Hughenden to Winton (Q.), a dis-
tance of 120 miles (and a lonely track it was in
those days). It was summer-time, and there was
water at two places only, yet she did the trip in
daylight without dismounting more than twice. She
was a dandy rider. The moke was just an ordinary
western horse with four legs and a fly-swish.

“Reka”: When “Tiaki” told an Abotale
of a stingaree Bft. in diameter having been caught
by the Maoris at Orakei (M.L.) some time back in
the dark ages, we were politely sceptical. The
Auckland dredge, however, did a little fishing on its
own account the other day, and proved that “Tiaki”
may have been less of a liar than we thought. Any-
how, it landed a 'stingaree 7ft. in diameter. The
back was shaped like a brown turtle-shell and was
about as hard, but it was the tail that was most
appalling. This weapon, quite a six-footer, was at
the top as thick as a man’s wrist, and spiked hor-
ribly with inch spikes. The catch occurred at a
point that is rather a favorite bathing place.
“Sunflower”: Comparatively little is
known about the wild flowers and flora of Austra-
lia’s dependency, Papua. Yet, there are
some beautiful blossoms in the back-
woods, particularly in the spring.
Amongst these is the gorgeous scarlet
bloom of the D’Albertez creeper, which
drapes the trees on the banks of many
rivers. Akin to it is a creeper locally
known as the blue D’Albertez, which
grows in the scrubs, entwining the
branches of high trees. Another wild
flower blossoms like the wattle, and re-
sembles it in all except color, being of
a coral pink shade, instead of golden.
Then there is another pink blossom which
in shape resembles three miniature tas-
selled lamp shades arranged one above the
other, and suspended by a cord through
the middle. On the banks of some of the
inland creeks grows a small plant which
has a beautiful blue flower, in shape simi-
lar to the blue-bell, though slightly larger.
Among other wild blossoms mention may
be made of one, star-shaped and white,
having six petals and resembling the
frangipani; of a heart-shaped leaf blos-
som of pure white color which when seen
amongst the green leaves resembles pel-
lets of snow ; of the red and the yellow
hybiscus; of the yellow flower of the
wild cotton ; of a bell-shaped flower of a
miniature creeper the inside of which is
a soft yellow color and the lip a dark
blood-red hue; and of the creamy white
and light pink shades of the wax-like be-
gonia and hoya blossoms. Many of these
wild flowers of Papua are scented, and
give off a perfume like attar of love-glance.
“Werrimee”: Of the two blacltfellows
playing cards in the camp, one was a stockman who
had returned to his tribe and primitive life for a
holiday, neatly dressed, and with a few shillings in
his pocket. He soon lost the coin. Then he staked his
coat, Billy, his opponent, putting down 10s. against
it. That was lost also; and vest, boots, hat, shirt
and trousers followed in quick order. Naked and
disconsolate the loser stood up and held out his
hand to the winner. “Lend me ten bob,” he re-
quested in a tone that implied that there was to be
no argument about it. The money was handed to
him. He counted it carefully, and threw it down
again. “Sell me trouser an’ tchirt,” he next com-
manded. This was done, and donning them, boot-
less .and hatless, he started off to get work.
Re a Breach of Judicial Etiquette.
A letter from Melbourne: —
The Victorian Bar has not quite ceased to be an-
noyed with Chief Justice John Madden for some-
thing he spoke from the bench a while ago. Madden
remarked that a libel case was being spun out unduly
by counsel for the sake of the fees. The litigants—
especially one of them—were known to have a lot
of money, and the lawyers—so the Judge seemed to
think —were going for more than a fair thing. But,
in the opinion of the profesh, his Honor ought not
to have blurted out the bare truth, seeing that he
was once a barrister himself, with merely moderate
scruples on his own account. All Judges, having
previously been participants in the same old game,
are considered to be guilty of unsportsmanlike con-
duct if they give the game away. Even though they
be sincere penitents, they are rather like, unto the
converted mummer who denounces the morals of the
theatrical world, or the wealthy, retired draper who
publishes the tricks of the trade. And why, asks
the Bar, should not a barrister do what is commonly
done by his brother the doctor of medicine? The
doctor spins out a case, and the surgeon charges an
abnormally fat fee for his operation, where the
patient is wealthy. Some doctors, in fact, have no
compunction where the patient is not wealthy, hut
merely able to pay. They practically compel the
nurses to join them in a conspiracy of imposition
by forbidding the sympathetic pillow-patters to “get
the patient up.” There is no be-wigged authority
who can publicly accuse a medical practitioner of
prolonging a case in the interests of his bill, there-
fore legal practitioners object to being shown up
by the Bench. It is bad for business, because it dis-
courages litigation, and it does no good to the liti-
gants who are trying their luck. The public have no
protection against the rapacity of Gentleman One.
The law is made for lawyers by lawyers, and a Judge
who happens to admit that legal procedure admits of
scandalous dishonesty to the "public, and then goes
away and says no more about it, is only calling at-
tention to the futility of his remarks. He is in a
position to play the part of a reformer, but he does
not attempt any reform, or call for anv reform, or
suggest any reform. Half the ills that the public
suffer from the law’s delays and delusions might be
cured by a committee of Judges, sitting with the
single-minded object of giving the public the largest
possible amount of justice at the least possible cost.
Such a committee can never he. Judges are the
fathers, brothers, uncles, cousins and fathers-in-law
of men who are living on the iniquities of legal pro-
cedure. They will never turn dog on their profes-
sional relatives, In their courts they sit all day,
watching the game they used to play—and saying
nothing against its character. Judge Madden’s casual
comment on one particular game was clearly a
breach of etiquette on a par, almost, with the mis-
conduct of the bird who fouls her own nest.
Britain has at last arrived at the idea of
a compulsory system of military-physical
training in connection with Continuation
Schools. The Government is throwing out
hints of its intention to propose a means
of developing the chest measurement of the
rising generation, if the weedy, risen genera-
tion is quite agreeable. But the weedy
risens are disposed to reckon themselves
Boys of the Bulldog Breed, and to scout the
suggestion that the breed is getting to be a
joke. It will be hard to convince fat, elderly
citizens of the truth —citizens who pat their
stummicks approvingly, and describe embon-
point as “condition.” They reached matur-
ity in the pre-SANDOW period, and are in-
flexible Rule Britannians. The beginning
of the modern educational influence in Brit-
ain was Eugene Sandow, the German who
settled in the country and gave it notions of
muscle even as Handel of yore gave it
notions of music. A statue of Sandow
ought to be erected on the Thames Embank-
ment, or somewhere.
For Women Only.
A document which blew into this office tv,
other day expresses to perfection the lfl ,
Liberal Mind. To read it is to he? 6
fancy a A oice bleating in a wilderness *
tea-things while ladies and also irm 01
women cheer restrainedly or shudder as
occasion demands. Although it was o nl
issued the other day it contains most of th
anti-Labor gags which were exploded as fav
back as six years ago, and for futilih
smugness and cant, and a particularly m
savory sort of disingenuousness, it i= Jr'
limit. Some extracts: —
Dear Madam, —
In asking you to become a member of the
tralian Women’s National League, we desire to i '
press upon you that its aims are of such a nature'?'
to command the support of every woman who wish«
to see Australia prosperous.
Our League places loyalty to the Throne and Fm
pire in the foreground, believing that the Unitv nf
the Empire is the strongest safeguard - of Australia’,
long coastline and her scantily peopled area. 8
We ask you to join with us in opposing by solid
and combined effort the aims of the Labor Socialistic
Government, which threatens to overwhelm Australia
and engulf her in the throes of industrial and aril
warfare. We are not against Labor, and honor and
uphold the Toiler, but we are against the Anarchist
- —the Socialist (who seeks to destroy, not to build
up)—and the domination of the Trades Hall and
Caucus Rule.
We advocate Freedom for both Employer and
Employee, and the right of all to work and
benefit by the result. Capital and labor should
co-operate on grounds of mutual interest. We up-
hold the principle of individual liberty and private
enterprise, and oppose Government Trading when it
interferes with the same.
Our Federal Platform upholds the Federal Union
as embodied in the Constitution, and one of our
principal Planks is to restore responsible Govern-
ment. We also advocate the Policy of progressive
and suitable Immigration and a sound Defence
Scheme.
Why this balderdash, about “loyalty to
Throne and Empire”? If there were any
political party of the least weight or in-
fluence which advocated the over-turning of
the Throne and the bursting-up of the Em-
pire, one might understand it. But there is
no section, worth noticing, in the whole of
this immense continent that clamors or
machinates or wishes either to separate
George V. from his job, or to dismember
the British Empire. For a political party
to put “loyalty to Throne and Empire” “in
the foreground” is, if the phrase means no
more than appears on the face of it, as im-
becile as it would be to put in the same
proud place “antipathy to diphtheria,” or
“a dogged determination to continue wear-
ing clothes.” If the aspiration has any sig-
nificance at all, if, in fact, it isn’t mere
superfluous and redundant idiocy, it amounts
to a yearning after Liberalism’s old, not to
say doddering, love, Hired Defence. The
dragging-in of references to “Australia’s
long coastline and her scantily peopled
area” support this view. In that case the
sooner the Leaguers get sense the better
for Australia. The British Government
and various British Admirals have stated in
the plainest terms, of late, that the United
Kingdom has no intention of defending Aus-
tralia’s coastline and scantily-peopled area.
Those duties the Commonwealth is expected,
by the Empire, to undertake, henceforth, on
its own. Attempts to mix up loyalty to
Throne and Empire with the policy of the
asses who conducted the Dreadnought agi-
tation are regarded by the people who are
running the Empire with apprehension and
dislike. It is stated in Plank 2 that the
present Australian Government “threatens
to overwhelm Australia and engulf her in
the tjiroes of industrial and civil warfare."
When did this peaceful bourgeois crowd do
anything of the sort? No one is preparing
for civil war after practically six years or
“Labor-Socialistic” Government. The only
serious hint of anything of the sort has come
from that earnest law-abider, Bruce Smith.
He threatened that, if the people didn't vote
the way he wanted, in the last Referendum
there would be a “bloody revolution.’ But
no one believed him. As regards Flank 0.
“Freedom for both Employer and Employee
means, simply, anti-Unionism. It needs no
elaboration that a nation of “free em-
plovees would he equivalent to a nation 0
helots. The “free” worker, during about
3GO days of the year, is as advantageously
situated as the free employer with no capi-
tal and a burst renutation. _ To ask tne
modern worker to forego Unionism, as tne
Lady Leaguer blandly does, is tantamont
to requesting the capitalist to. drop nr
wealth into the ocean. The one is as mm
a charter of independence as the other, m
first part of Plank 4 is seemingly a sort ot
anti-Referendum argument. But what tne
Leaguer’s ambition to “restore responsible
Government” in the Federal arena means
passes all comprehension. In fact, the s m
picion arises that it is pure guff. And tne
same applies to the wearisome humbug
about immigration and defence. 0 s
up, the Australian Womens Nation.
Leaarue stands for the Toryism of ten years
ago. posturing behind a species of demo< <
tic mask. Its moth-eaten old platitudes ma
do for women; they would certainly
good to fairly intelligent men. 11 *
League makes good, to any marked extern,

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Father Le Maitre, head of the Passionist
order in S.A., was for eight years an officer
in the Belgian army. He also took the
diploma in agriculture at Brussels Univer-
sity. and speaks practically every language
in Europe.
John Aspery, who died at Ferndale, Sack-
ville Reach, Hawkesbury River (N.S.W.)
last week, aged 80, had this one distinction,
he was born and resided all his life in the
house in which he died. It is a common
enough distinction in England, but Aus-
tralia hasn’t netrified to that extent yet save
in spots. The Hawkesbury is one of the
spots.
John McLaughlin, the Sydney solicitor,
is putting up a record as the State’s boss
litigant. Since 1908 he has been fighting
the City Bank and the Sydney Telegraph
concerning property of his which was ad-
ministered by his wife while he was ill. The
case against the Telegraph was settled
after much argument, but the Bank matter
still rumbles along. The Privy Council has
now taken it in hand, and has allowed
McLaughlin leave to appeal from the last
decision of the High Court (Isaacs dissent-
ing). It’s remarkable how often Isaacs
does dissent.
DR. WINT HROP HACKETT, M.L.C. ,
boss of the WEST AUSTRALIAN.
The Bulletin is informed that, by rea-
son of a carelessly or casually written line in
its obituary notice of the late Louis Becke,
some misapprehension may have arisen. Mr.
Becke was married at Port Macquarie
(N.S.W.), in 1886, to Miss Bessie Mary
Maunsell, daughter of Colonel Maunsell, a
retired British officer, then P.M. at that
lonesome town. There were two children by
the marriage—a son who died when still very
young, and a daughter, now resident in
Europe. Mrs. Becke, who survives her hus-
band, is at present in Sydney.
Some of the Labor people in Adelaide are
beginning to hint to their leader, ex-Premier
John Verran, that he lacks dignity. Yerran
compounds his speeches of passages from the
Scripture, familiar phrases of the Primitive
Methodist conventicle, and such casually
affable terms as “bloke” and “nal.” The
Labor Herald suggests that “bloke” and
pal” are not absolutely necessary, and that
this sort of pose gives the Philistines a
chance to rejoice in Ashdod.
Concerning a vigorous brother in a mis-
taken cause:—
William Gittos, the best-known exponent of Chris-
tianity amongst the Maoris since George Augustus
Selwyn threw up the job to bish, in England, has re-
tired,_ after about 60 years’ argument with Henare.
And in the old savage and semi-savage days Ilenare
was not the comparatively simple proposition he is
now, when he has adopted many of the habits of his
Christian brother. Gittos reached Maoriland in 1841,
and as a youth learned to dread Henare when he was
on the warpath. But when he (Gittos) went forth
for the Church of John Wesley (about 1853) he
rapidly gained a strong influence over the natives in
the northern Wairoa and Kaipara, and more than
once prevailed on powerful bodies to keep their right
arms from grasping the tomahawks, and, by an au-
dacious courage that would have been a fortune to a
poker player, stared down fierce eyes along the gun-
sights. The saddest thing is the futility of the
strenuous work. In the transport of the soldiers of
the _ Lord were Bibles and rum bottles, tracts and
billiard tables, the Psalms of David and the three-
star of Gehenna. And Henare reckoned that
being “saved” was kapai (very good). Alas, poor
Henare, his days are few in the- land. The pace of
the Christian was too hot.
