William C. Brooker, an Adelaide business
man who has a prosperous establishment in
the heart of the city with a long lease, is
relinquishing it to go into evangelical work.
He is 40 years old, and so ought to know his
own mind. He is not a Wowser, but a keen
commercial man who knows his way about
as well as any of his trade rivals.
Concerning a forgotten Messiah: —
Passed out last week, here at Wickepin (W.A.),
old man Fisher, the “Nunawading Messiah” of Vic-
toria’s last generation. He came to Westralia 10 or
12 years ago, and founded a settlement called New
Jerusalem, built a church on his own property, and
conducted services on Sundays and new-moon days.
It was his “perk” to stand on the doorstep of the
church and salute all and sundry (particularly the
girls) with a holy kiss. Between kisses he had
visions. About 1909 a split occurred amongst his
followers, and those who clove to him dwindled down
to about half a dozen. That left him so little to do
at the church door that', to make sure of a congrega-
tion of at least one, he married a girl of 18 —he
was then 79—and enjoyed about three years of matri-
mony. To the last he persisted in avowing himself
a sort of second Christ, and he had sure and certain
hopes of an early resurrection. So far, however,
nothing unusual appears to have happened at the
local cemetery.
South Australia’s biggest loss in the edu-
cational line, since a bicycle accident killed
Inspector-General Hartley years ago, is the
early death of Alfred Williams, just under
50. It was a brilliant inspiration of
the late Tom Price to offer the post
of Director of Education to a mere sub-
urban schoolmaster, who at once proceeded
to justify the startling choice. Williams’s
big work was the completing of a High
School system. which can take the clever
State school kid on to the University; but
the improved lighting and ventilating and
desking of all schools in the State was a
great practical reform, too. He was almost
at once invited to join the University Coun-
cil. Unfortunately he had not the physique
necessary for a man who wants to do several
men’s work at once, but he refused to recog-
nise the fact. Within four years from his
promotion his health was broken down, and
now in the seventh year he is dead. Maughan,
who has had much the same training, suc-
ceeds him.

Thomas Lyne, just appointed captain of
the British battleship Goliath, at the Nore,
is the first British naval captain who has
risen from the position of a “common
sailor.” At all events, he is the first for
two or three centuries, because until lately
an ancient regulation was in force which
prevented any rise beyond the status of war-
rant officer.
Dr. Hertz, who did great things in Jew-
hannesburg, is appointed Chief Rabbi for
Lnglish subjects ; so the hopes of Dr. Abra-
hams, of Melbourne; are dead. Still he
could hardly have pressed his claims, as all
the Australian Rabbis hold that there is no
occasion for a chief. America and Germany
have none. But the Rothschild, or Tory, in-
fluence was not to be denied.

George Burwin, who must have been Aus-
tralia’s oldest native, or thereabouts, died at
Tumbarumba (N.S.W.) the other day. He
was born at Windsor, on the Hawkesbury 91
years ago, and had been a bushman all his
days. As a young man he overlanded cattle
to Port Phillip and Adelaide, and as a some-
what older man he was a member of one of
the expeditions sent out to see whether Leich-
hardt was dead or merely dreaming again.
Apropos Powers’s appointment to the
High Court Bench, and the fact that, though
a barrister, he preferred the work (or pay)
of the “lower branch,” and so got himself
disbarred. The biggest Chief Justice N. S
W Mes has known was James Martin. And
the Australian Chief Justice whose judg-
ments have been least often disturbed on
appeal is Way, of Adelaide. And both by
a coincidence, were at one time solicitors
Martin often appeared in the police court'
M ay was still a member of a firm which de-
voted itself mostly to attorney’s work when
he climbed on to the Bench. But it doesn’t
follow that because Powers and Martin were
both solicitors they stand on the same foot-
ing. As a case in point, Tennyson and Jaw-
bone Neild were both poets.
Died in Seattle, on the Pacific Slope, Tre-
mayne Dunstan, who a few years ago made
the highly respectable old Pitt-street (Syd-
ney ) Congregational tabernacle rock on its
foundations. He was a Cornish preacher
w h<| could froth beautifully; but his busi-
ness incapacity was monumental, and when
the duns drove him hard the pastor simply
bit tlie ears of the most susceptible ewes of
his flock. When the ewes started to bleat
he fled to the United States, but thought
better of it, and returned to tell his story in
the Insolvency Court. Luck was, however,
against him, and the best he could do was
found something that was 10 per cent,
church and 90 per cent, marriage shop. Up
never relished the job, and presently faded
out a second time to America, where, after
a spell as a travelling lecturer, he more
or less settled down again to his old shep-
herding job.

Reverend Albert Knight, the Leeds parson
who went giddy with a Sunday-school teacher
of the opposite sex, is said to be bringing the
lady to Melbourne, aboard a steamer which
comes due next week. He will arrive in the
character of the Silver King, for in an
alleged statement to the British public, sent
by_ wireless, he said : “Feeling I had brought
ruin and disgrace on the church, I thought
by dying in one country I might recover a
new reputation.” Which means that, the
night before the boat gets to Melbourne, the
unfortunate ex-shepherd intended to kneel
upon the deck and cry, “Wilfred Denver is
dead ! To-morrow I begin a new wife.”
LOUIS BECKE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They’re at their age-long harvest still—the angel
Death and Time—-
But ebb or flow, we all must go, and leave the
broken rhvme.
Wide blue with whiteeaps here and there—the glory
of the day—
A space of seascapes wond’rous fair, in Islands far
away ;
Faint silver on the distant reef, on skylines scarce
a fleck,
But fleecy clouds of blest-relief that welcome Louis
Becke.
Who’ll miss the well-loved stuttering speech ?
Who’ll mind the distant date
IV hen by the mast and palm-fringed beach those halt-
ing words had weight!
Who’d dream those sad, kind, manly eyes, when
traders were “in holts,”
In summer Isles of Paradise could glint behind a
Colt’s?
We only know “By Reef and Palm”—the world he
made his own— :
(The later wounds, without a balm, are better never
known).
We live and fight by day and night in carking care
and strife,
And take our pen in death to write the story of our
life.
Farewell, my friend—’twill ne’er be told—or told
in printed line
(Your destiny in days of old was strongly linked
with mine).
I trust my track shall run as true, though come it
late or soon,
When my name shall be missing, too, from “Some
Birthdays in June.”
N.S.W.
HENRY LAWSON.
THE NATURAL CONSEQUENCE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The burning of Mr. Lloyd George’s country villa
has been followed by another outrageously wicked act
of incendiarism—the destruction of a tea-house in the
Koyal Botanical Gardens at Kew.
Mrs. Flora Drummond, when interviewed regarding
the suffragettes’ latest outrage, exclaimed: “How
womTnl”-JalL 0f 11161111 lam Pr ° Ud ° f Such
The prisoner was dressed with care;
A costly orchid decked his coat;
The parting in his glossy hair
Climbed nobly up to parts remote.
His monocle and loose-hung jaw
Expressed a mute, eternal “Haw!”
His speech was crisp, his grammar good;
At intervals he pawed his tie;
He looked, as ev’ry real gent should,
The judge and jury in the eye.
I asked a lawyer, tall and thin,
“What sort of scrape is this boy in?”
“That chap belongs,” the lawyer said,
“To London’s Very Nicest gang!
With bombs he killed nine statesmen dead,
And if his luck is out, he’ll hang.
It is a finish crude and grim,
But his mamma is proud of him.
“She used to be a suffragette
When loaded bombs became the thing;
Lloyd George with snails she used to fret,
And worms at Winston Churchill fling.’
But first to Fame she made her bow
When she blew up a Liberal cow.
“From that to arson was a stride
That even narrow skirts might take;
In motor-’buses she would ride,
While Ministers would flee and quake;
I or years she was a well-known sight
With handbag full of dynamite.
“The dear old lady now looks back
With pride to that sweet, early day
When down to ruin and to wrack
She brought the things that barred her way.
It’s int’resting to hear her views
As she sits stuffing home-made fuse.
“She says, of course, explosives now
Are not so good. And how she dotes
Upon the memory of the cow
She first blew up for women’s votes!
When he blew up his group of nine,
She cried, ‘How perfectly divine l’ ”
The criminal had all the signs
Of noble birth. His weeping Judge
Was built on bulldog-monkey lines
A warder gave the doomed a nudge.
I glimpsed, as he walked towards the noose,
His proud old mother knitting fuse.
N.S.W.
E. O’F.

The last few lines are as near as this paper can get
to the facts ; and it can only get there by guessing a
little. Most N.S.W. gas cos. have been busy making
capital out of nothing that is visible to the naked
eye, and very few of them go on the stage and show
their own particular juggle. Six months ago this one
had £96,295 of reserves, including premiums on new
shares; and its capital was £66,130. Subsequently
£240 of capital was allotted, and presumably the
co. got the same amount in premiums. That brought
the capital up to £66,370. The capital now, how-
ever, appears as £132,740, and no explanation is
made of the change. Reserves to the extent of
£64,993 have been visibly drawn upon; but in the
table it has been assumed that two shares for one
have been issued, and that the extra £1377 required
has been forthcoming from premiums, undisclosed pro-
fits or other sources. The juggle is, of course, the
result of the new Gas Act, which gives this co.,
among others, six months to capitalise its reserves.
Thereafter it may pay only 10 p.c. so long as the
present price of gas is maintained. But as shares
have been doubled, 10 p.c. now is as good as 20
p.c. would have been; and as the co. has hitherto
paid only 15 p.c., the new law is really putting
money into the shareholders’ pockets. Even with the
capital doubled, the co.’s position is strong, and, as
the table shows, over £30,000 of reserves remain;
and, if a valuation supports the present book value of
the assets—in other words, if a valuation shows the
reserves to be real—the directors will surely make use
of their six months’ grace to capitalise this £30,000.
There is all the more justification for this because
so large a proportion of the co.’s reserves was derived
from premiums on the new issues. On the figures
available there is enough to warrant the issue of one
new share for five present shares —or even two for
every nine. For the rest, all that need be said is
that, though the co. has been spending a lot of
money lately, and has provided itself with a new
manufacturing outfit, its financial position is, as
usual, very easy: its liabilities amount to £13,836,
while its liquid assets (including £20,674 of N.S.W.
Funded Stock) run to £32,774.
The Electric Light and Power Supply
Corporation, Ltd. (Sydney), which started with a
deficit of £9876, representing Parliamentary rights
and other preliminary exes.:—

The share capital is £50,000, in £1 shares; 7000
rank as ordinaries, and were issued to the promoters ;
all others are preferential. The co. was formed to
supply Balmain with a garbage destructor, and, in
return, Parliament, at the request of the local coun-
cil, gave it the right to start up in business as a
purveyor of electric light and power. Having got
hold of Balmain, it started to feel out as- a com-
petitor with the gas co. and municipal enterprise,
and made contracts with Newtown, Leichhardt,
Petersham and Ashfield to supply them with current
for periods up to 57 years. This called for more
capital, so 7 p.c. debentures were issued; there are
now £67,300 of liabilities under that head, plus
£29,456 under others, including bank overdraft.
This means a big, bite out of revenue before share-
holders can get anything on their own plates. Busi-
ness has, however, increased so rapidly that there is
now a fine margin beyond the requirements of a 7
p.c. dividend. This surplus is being used (1) to
write down plant, etc., on account of depreciation,
and (2) to get rid of the preliminary expenses and
other paper assets. There is a good deal to be done
in that way yet before the substantial assets can show
20s. in the £ of the capital. Also, in estimating
the probable future it has to be noted that several
of the contracts which have been entered into pro-
vide that the co. shall treat the other party as favor-
ably as the City of Sydney municipal electric light
and power department treats its customers under
similar contracts. As the municipal enterprise isn’t
out after dividends, but simply wants to make in-
terest and a bit over for sinking funds, etc., this
will most likely serve as a check on this co.’s profits.

Like other N.S.W. gas cos., this one now admits that
its published accounts hitherto have been —well, what?
Cooked is a nasty term. Yet the present accounts
show two balance-sheets, one as at Dec. 31, 1912,
and the other as at Jan. 31, 1913, and the latter
discloses about one-sixth more assets than the
former. Hitherto the object of the N.S.W. gas cos.
has been to show that they weren’t bad sharks, that
they weren’t biting very much out of the public,
and that there really wasn’t any justification for
municipal competition or State interference. In pur-
suance of that policy they usually disclosed just
enough profit to cover as big a dividend as they
thought it prudent to pay, and stuffed away surplus
profit (which they didn t show) in new works. Then
came the Carmichael bomb and finally the new Gas
Act, prohibiting further bonus distributions after six
months grace had expired, and limiting the dividend
on new and old capital. A new position was thus
created. It then paid the cos. best to shake out all
the hidden profits from their sleeves and capitalise
them. That meant a revaluation of assets straight
away, and this co. has just completed the job.
Apparently the fixed assets have been written up
from £56,608 to £65,356. The difference between
these amounts is added to reserves; and out of the
reserves a bonus of 20 p.c. will be paid on March 7.
Simultaneously a call of 2s. 6d. will be made on
the newest lot of 60,000 shares, which will make them
paid to 45., and Is. on 30,000 shares paid at present
7s - J There are also 30,000 fullv-paid shares,
they will draw their bonus in cash. The bonus will
mean paying out in all £9150; the calls, made
simultaneously, will bring in £9OOO.
As at Dec. 31 in the several years the position
has been as follows:

The visible surplus after paying the dividend has
been small; and even giving the co. credit for
tne written-up values, it has not done anything ex-
traordinary. But the future is very bright. Revenue
has more than trebled in 10 years, and it hasn’t
stopped growing; further, owing to the rapid multi-
plication of consumers along existing mains, the
profit should be larger in proportion to the capital
outlay than it was a few years back. The debts are
small; and it will be possible to double the present
fixed assets by getting in the uncalled capital. And
here arises an interesting point. The Gas Act says

something. Or, if it didn’t know anything, it had
extraordinary luck.

The reserves include: General renewal fund, £7784 •
meter renewal fund, £1221; depreciation fund for
works on leasehold, £2450. Those don’t look verv
f°e9l srw rVe +k and * t* 16 ™ amounts added to them
( £2150 for the past half-year) look as though they
might have to come off the profits in order to pre-
sent the real results for the half-year. And if £2150
is deducted from £3301 there isn’t enough left to
pay the 10 p.c. div. However, things are probably
better than that; in other words, fair depreciation,
etc., probably doesn t call for as much as £2150 in
six months. You can look at it another way. Seven
years ago the more or less liquid assets were £5461
more than the liabilities. They are now £3948
more, showing a shrinkage of £1513 in seven years.
noPo? 1 , 6 ’ however, fixed assets have increased from
£26,884 to £41,269, or by nearly £15,000; and
as no new capital has been got in, it means that cur-
rent revenue in excess of working exes, and divi-
dends has supplied the money. As against that ex-
penditure on additional plant, mains, etc., there has
no doubt, been some general depreciation, though it
is not likely to have been so much as that The
position may, therefore, be quite satisfactory. The
accounts ought to make things plainer than they
do. ror instance, the value of most of the assets is
shown less depreciation.” Yet, on the other side,
there are depreciation reserves. This may mean
that depreciation was formerly written off, but that
current depreciation is not written off. If that is
so, the remark, “less depreciation,” is so liable to be
misread that it wouldn’t be going too far to call it
misleading.

This co. is carrying a heavy load of debt, represented
by assets which are probably not earning very much.
This shows some of the figures:—
Liabilities.
Dec., ’O2 June,’l2. Dec.,’l2.
Mortgages and deposits. . £94,132 £91,405 £90,775
Overdrafts, etc. .. .. 25,740 33,242 31,519
Assets.
Hotels, properties, etc. . . 180,482 181,871 183,587
Stock and plant . . 35,412 „ 42,875 37,500
Book debts, etc. .. 19,010 28,529 30,153
Gash and liquid assets. . 166 70 7
Capital is £108,000; and the liabilities, apart from
ordinary trade debts, exceed that amount. Interest
runs to £5OOO a year or more; and that goes on no
matter whether trade is dull or brisk. And, as men-
tioned above, it is highly probable that a good deal
of the expensive money is bringing in little or
nothing. “Hotels, properties, etc.,” represent
£183,587, and the properties include the big Cas-
cade estate, on the slopes of Mt. Wellington. Some-
day it may be exceedingly valuable: meantime there
is no sparkle in this asset. As a matter of fact,
there’s a lot to be said in favor of cutting the Cas-
cade Co. in two, and turning the estate and land-
subdivision business over to a separate concern. Apart
from the fact that the people who successfully run
a brewery are liable to know nothing at all about
estate agency and land subdivision, it is inadvisable
that the figures relating to the two should be lumped.
The brewery may, for instance, be doing very poorly;
yet a profit may be made on the sale of some of the
estate, and the truth about the real business of the
co. is then disguised. This wouldn’t matter, per-
haps, if the two businesses would run on together
indefinitely; but one is a terminating business—you
can’t go on selling the same land for ever. Meantime
the investor can note definitely only two or three
things: (1) the size of the mortgages and other pre-
ferential claims on the assets; (2) the poor additions
to reserves, in spite of the public’s flush pockets and
the plentiful supply of invitations to have another.
The former circumstance doesn’t add to the security
of the principal, of course ; while the latter gives no
hint of an increased dividend, but suggests that it
would be impossible to pay even 6 p.c. if times were
a little less affluent.

The Australian Wood Pipe Co. (Syd-
ney) was formed about the middle of 1909, to make
and sell certain wood pipes. The deficit to begin
with represented patents and other goodwill items,
plus preliminary exes.:—
Towards lie- De-

Dec., 1910.. 183" 10 p.c. = £B9B 932 6170
Goodwill items, £ll .. 6181
June, 1911.. 2447 10 p.c. = 1085 1362 4819
Goodwill items, £2B .. 4847
Dec., 1911.. 1592 lOp.c. = 1370 222 4625
June, 1912.. 3221 10 „ ~ 1500 1721 2904
Goodwill items, £B6 .. 2990
Dec., 1912.. 3349 lOp.c. = 2160 1189 1801
The business is new to Australia, and it has the advan-
tage of having a pushful crowd behind it. Partly on
that account, partly because Australia has lately wanted
things done in a hurry, and partly because this
country finds it easier to chop down a tree than
laboriously turn ironstone into metal, the co. didn’t
have to wait long for a big turnover. Starting in
Sydney with a capital of £15,000, it soon got con-
tracts in Victoria, and found it desirable to put up
works also in Melbourne; and now an order for
£30,000 worth of the goods has arrived from the S.
Aus. Government with the condition that works also
go up in that State. The original 15,000 shares have
therefore been swollen already to 49,100, 37,500 of
them fully paid and the rest paid to 10s. The new
works at Adelaide are now under construction. For-
tunately for the co. the plant isn’t elaborate; the
Sydney and Melbourne works, along with anything
that has been paid on those in S. Aus., stand in
the books at only £14,623. An annual 10 p.c. has
been written off against depreciation. This allow-
ance is deducted from the profits shown above. Among
the contracts in hand is one for over £39,000 worth
of pipes to connect the Government’s new Umberum-
berka reservoir and Broken Hill; and if the pipes can
stand in that climate it will be a whooping advt. for
the co. And it will be all the more whooping be-
cause Broken Hill is higher than Umberir.nberka,
wherefore, unless pumping is maintained continuously,
the pipes will be dry at times. The original deficit
has been almost wiped out. That is to say, the capi-
tal is now almost wholly represented by substantial
assets. But the character of those assets has to be
kept in mind. Property, plant, etc., stand for
£14,623; stocks on hand, £15,430; sundry debtors
and deposits, £13,796. If anything went wrong with
the show, the first two items wouldn’t be gilt-edged ;
if it had to realise, the co. might get 10s. or 15s. in
the £ for them. However, nothing that suggests
trouble is showing on the horizon. Now and then,
as the table shows, a few pounds are added to the
goodwill, presumably for patents. But the big thing
is not covered by patent rights. Anybody can make
wood pipes—a further point to be kept in the eye
in appraising the quality of Wood Pipe stock as an
investment. Not that it makes it a bad-quality in-
vestment : it merely lays the concern open to com-
petition, which has a steadying influence on charges,
profits and dividends.

This co.’s line taps a dry part of Western Riverina
and connects with the Victorian State system at
Moama. The big landlord flourishes in those parts,
and the average 16in. of rainfall doesn’t encourage
the small man to look that way with much en-
thusiasm yet awhile. Meantime the co., serving a
poorly settled district which is liable to rather bad
spehs o f drought, jogs along in a sleepy fashion.
Still its position has improved a good deal in 10
years:—
T . ~ 1902. 1912.
Liquid assets .. ..£12,337 £24,011
Liabilities .. .. 31,814 20,670
Excess liabilities .. ..£19,477
Excess assets , ..
That shows an improvement of £22,818, represented
either by reduced debt (there are still £2O 000 of
4 p.c. debentures outstanding) or increased deposits
( t he se now total £15,783). This makes the co.
about £BOO a year better off than it was 10 years
ago. There are no big things ahead of the co. ; on
the other hand, what with the improved financial
position and the saner methods of working the west-
ern pastoral country now in vogue, there is not much
likelihood of the bottom falling out of the business
On the contrary, the tendency seems to be towards
a slow but steady improvement in the yield Re-
pairs and renewals appear to be well provided for out
of current revenue.

A great mistake, when erecting new works or re-
arranging old ones, is often made in constructing the
building and installing machinery ere looking round
for means of transport of the raw material, half-
manufactured and finished goods. Messrs. Adolf
Bleichert and Co., of London, have issued various
catalogues and leaflets, illustrating and describing a
few of the many transport systems and plants which
they have supplied, and which show the solution of
the transport question and the advantages of em-
ploying such appliances. W. and J. Lempriere,
Melbourne and Sydney, are the Australian representa-
tives, and would be pleased to hear from those in-
terested. A specially trained engineer is retained to
consult and advise.*

In all smithing work high-class iron can be more
safely manipulated at varying temperatures than
steel or ordinary iron. It is for this reason that
Best Yorkshire Farnley Iron is preferred by engi-
neers, where toughness, great strength and close
grain are needed. Whatever the specification, Iron
of the Farnley type does not vary, but is simply the
best that can be produced from what is really
wrought iron. “Farnley” Bars of all sections sup-
plied from local stock bv Sole Agents of the Farnley
Iron Comp., Ltd. Bell's Asbestos, Ltd., 315 Kent
Street, Sydney.*

From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yards
of road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per cub.
yard, and the cost of the spalls per cub. yard aver-
aged 2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub.
yard, broken and spread. Their last contract under
the handbreaking system amounted to 6s. Sd. per
cub. yard stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is. ;
total, 7s. 3d., as against 4s. lOd. — a saving of 2s. 5d.
per cub. yard. This result was obtained by using a
Bigelow 16in. x 9in. Patent Stonecrusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers.* [2

Water and Sewerage Board, are now removing all
machinery to Melbourne works to manufacture similar
pipes for the new O’Shanness.y water scheme for Mel-
bourne water supply, the cost of which will be over
£100,000. The work is to be completed within 12
months.*
Messrs. Babcock & Wilcox, Ltd., have received
the order for the supply of all the steam generating
plant and accessories for the electrification of the
Victorian Government Railways. The acceptance of
this firm’s tender was strongly recommended by Mr.
Merz, the English expert. The contract amounts to
£258,000.*

Thus in six years, a period which has included good
times and bad in the metal markets, the ore has
averaged just a shade under 9s. per ton in dividends.
This, of course, includes the profits from the old
tailings ; but as there are 1,005,195 tons left for the
Zinc Corporation to draw on, the revenue from this
source will last many years to come. Take, then,
9s. as a fair average. The quantity of ore in the
mine is estimated at 3,250,000 tons. At 9s. per
ton that is equal to £1,462,500. Add the surplus
liquid assets, £228,730; total, £1,691,230. There
are 200,000 shares =£B 9s. per share. If the co. does
as well as it has averaged in the past six years, and
if the reserves are up to the estimate, and if the
outlay on new plant in the future keeps to the aver-
age of the past six years, £8 9s. per share is what
it ought to pay; and further developments will be
so much bonus. ’ftiis paper naturally doesn’t know
what those developments will be. There is ore below
the bottom level; but the ore in the bottom level
isn’t as good as the average of the upper levels. And
there is ore, proved by diamond drills, west of the
present bodies; but quantities and values are not
known. And there are over 300,000 tons of rich
slimes on hand which some day may give a good
profit. The present co. was formed 19 years ago, and
the original co. was formed nine years before that.
“Since the inception of the c 0.,” says manager Wain-
wright, “the ore extracted has been 3.234.728 tons” ;
but whether that means the present co. or the orig-
inal co. isn’t clear. Anyhow, it doesn’t matter much.
What is striking is that the ore reserves, “on a con-
servative estimate,” exceed the quantity taken out.
It is a great mine. If one could be as sure of the
markets as one can be of the mine, everything in the
garden would be lovely.
Skinner’s, Ltd., issues an unsatisfactory
prospectus in Melbourne. Capital, £60,000, in £1
shares ; 25,000 to the vendor co., 25,000 to the pub-
lic, 10,000 in reserve. Besides 25,000 shares the
vendor co. takes £16,500 in cash. The concerns to
be taken over include the catering business formerly
carried on by the late Henry Skinner, with a couple
of small, or comparatively small, suburban businesses
(Holder’s and Ortner’s), and an option over another
catering business (Crichton’s), which can be had
for 1000 shares and £1550 cash. No statement of
liabilities and assets is furnished: and there is no
telling whether £5OOO or £40,000 is being paid for
goodwill. In any case, the bulk of the goodwill
consists of the value given to a business by a man
who is now dead. Further, nothing more than an
“estimate” of past profits is furnished. The busi-
ness may be all right. If it is the prospectus does it
a rank injustice. For it is one of the worst pros-
pectuses of which this paper has any knowledge.
Chairman James Munro let himself do a
little forecasting at the Queensland National Bank
meeting in Brisbane. “Without actually committing
the bank in any way, and unless something unfore-
seen occurs, we hope and believe we shall be in a
position to pay a dividend in about seven years. Of
course it is possible that business may improve, and
that the time may be slightly shortened ; and when
we do pay a dividend we hope to be able to start at
the rate of 10 p.c.” The Bulletin (23/l/’l3)
showed that, at last vear’s rate, it would take about
nine years to pay off the balance which it is neces-
sary to get rid of before the shareholders can handle
anything; so, as the directors probably put a bit by
for safety, and as things have been steadily improv-
ing, seven years may be accepted as somewhere near
it. The capital is £413,590 in £5 shares paid to
£3. The 60s. shares are now quoted at about 455.
Interest at 6 p.c. on 455. for seven years (com-
pounded) is worth 21s. If the estimate of seven
years is right, and if the bank will pay 10 p.c. for a
start, and if it will go on paying 10 p.c., present
market price will thus give the investor 6 p.c. for
seven years, and after that he will have a £3 share
carrying 10 p.c. for 665.
* * * *
Pulganbar Quicksilver and Copper
(N.S.W.) hasn’t lately been saying much about its
new treatment plant. Some months ago the weekly
whoops got louder and louder, and preparations were
made for a grand opening ceremony. The last re-
port merely whispered that the directors had visited
the mine and spent two days testing the general
working of the reducing plant. “Like all new instal-
lations, a few minor alterations were found neces-
sary,” so the ceremony was postponed. The few
minor alterations were estimated to take a month,
which suggests that there were a few major jobs to
be done as well. Anyway, something should shortlv
be heard. The question still is whether the mine is
really a mine or only a collection of museum speci-
mens —with the odds in favor of the latter. Plant
so far is reckoned to have cost something like
£25,000, which has come out of local pockets.
The Upper Clarence district, above Graf-
ton (N.S.W.), is unwinding a longer-winded sprint
than past records suggested would be the case. The
Garibaldi syndicate is now being turned into a com-
pany of £20,000, in 80,000 shares of ss. each. In
the excitement prevailing over the continued discov-
eries at the various mines, it is hard work to fasten
on to anvthing reliable. One report talks about
6000 oz. ore, there being so much gold that the mass,
when someone tried to dolly it, “simply squashed like
lead.” Such congestion heats the brain; but as a
cooler there is the recollection of the numerous and
unsuccessful revivals this district has suffered from.
The gold appears to be very spotty—good when
vou've got it, but horribly inclined to disappear.
The Solferino is an old field, worked by primitive
methods 40 vears ago. Something undoubtedly seems
to be left there, and it might be worth the while of
some company, with money to spend, to do a little
more than the customary pig-rooting and crude re-
covery. Without the money to spend, there can be
only one end.
lodide (N.S.W.) is completing the eree-
tion of a winder, and will soon start crosscutting
a t 190 ft., which should say something decisive;
and with engine power the shaft should go down to
300 ft. while the crosscut is lengthening. The high-
grade stuff at the. 92ft. level, though forgotten by the
market, is still in the mine, and the management
“ arranging the shipment of a lot that will bulk
±.13 a ton gross. There is a likelihood of the
present £lO shares being cut into tens.
J* H. Fawcett gives Baker’s Creek
(A.S.W.) a hope: he advises more development. But
the mine has been ransacked, and it is a toss-up
whether the co. would get the money back if it put
£12,500 into prospecting. Of course, nobody likes
to abandon a mine which has paid £272,500 in
divs. ; still, the fact has to be faced that every mine
has an end somewhere. Lucky the mine whose end is
marked plainly: it saves much lingering misery and
painful extraction of calls.

* * * *
Bullfinch Prop. (W.A.) has started crush-
ing. In addition to large blocked-out bodies under-
ground, there are some thousands of tons of ore from
the original shoot stacked on the surface, besides a
few hundred tons from Gilbert’s and Kearney’s lodes.
The co., therefore, has something to go on with ;
but whether it will be able to maintain the returns it
talks about is doubted —very much doubted. Mean-
time an optimistic report by Fred Morgan has been
sent for presentation to shareholders in London next
month, and director Robinson, formerly of Mel-
bourne, is visiting the mine before going to the
meeting.
* * * *
Great Boulder (W.A.) is considering its
option on the locally-boomed Great Victoria. So
far, results have been very patchy, and latterly
assays have been poor and appearances bad. It is
quite correct to say that there is a lot of gold in the
mine, but between gold and gold in payable quan-
tities there is all the difference in the world.
* * * »
Picked up for next to nothing, the South
Edna May, at Westons (W.A.), has been floated in
Melbourne with a 50,000-share list. The venture is
purely speculative, and there is absolutely no justifi-
cation for the figure to which exaggeration has
carried the shares.
Associated Northern (W.A.) still reports
high and medium values in rising on the new shoot
at Ora Banda, and with the prospect of an early
dividend and the possibility of telluride in depth, in-
terest in the show is being sustained. By the way,
a curious official bloomer has come to light. On
January 9, the management coded £B2 in mistake
for 825., as the average value from 65ft. to 95ft.
along the east drive. The Board thought that £B2
was a bit tall, and cabled for confirmation prior to
publication. Curiously enough, the original mistake
was confirmed, and the amazing figure got into
print. Letters of explanation are now on their way
to head office. * * *
Pilbarra (W.A.) is looking very dull and
dreary. With tin standing at a high price, it was
fondly hoped that capital would come along and
boom the place. Some of its representatives did enter
an appearance, but went away again without doing
anything. As a result, Wodgina and other centres
have languished, and would have gone from bad to
worse had the Government not granted free crush-
ings of 20-ton parcels to prospectors so as to give
them a little money to go on with. A lot of cash
is needed to enable the tin deposits in this outland-
ish locality to be properly developed, but heavy
transport and other charges choke investors off.
* * *
Headed by one of the local papers, the
W.A. goldfields are petitioning the Government to
limit the number of Dagoes employed in the mining
and firewood industries to one in ten. At present
the percentage of Italians and Austrians working
underground is hard to arrive at because Macaroni
becomes Jones and Petropaulovitch is changed into
Smith in the time books; but it is declared that
the proportion is nearer one in five than one in ten.
As for the fuel industry, Dagoes and others bearing a
close resemblance to them practically run the show.
Golden Butterfly (W.A.) has recovered a
further £l4OO from its show near Kookvnie, and
stacked an additional 665 tons of slimes for future
treatment. The mill is now idle pending improve-
ments, and installation of extra residues plant, which
is expected to be running sometime in April.
At the Yuanmi the shoot is shortening
in depth, and has broken up badly. A new parallel
make of ore was struck a few months ago in the
bottom, but, so far, nothing has been opened up
to justify any whoop. # „
Mountain Queen (W.A.) has not yet ful-
filled directors’ expectations of £2OOO profit a month,
but is confident of doing so directly it gets an un-
interrupted month’s run. The surplus for January
amounted to £l3OO, and while there s life there s
hope. # » »
Another Vic. mine goes under—the Jubi-
lee, Scarsdale. The payable ore at the lower level
is exhausted; the manager can’t recommend further
levels from the present shaft (the next
one would have to go out 700 ft. to get
the ore) ; and the £25,000 estimate for
a new shaft staggers the Board; so the
mine has been closed down, and the directors pro-
pose a clearing-out sale. The mine’s records date
back to 1887. For 11 years a co-operative co.
worked it and gouged out £39,835 of gold, of
which about half was profit. Since ’9B, when the
present co. took it over, £502,958 worth of gold
has given £145,800 in divs. There was never a
half-year from 1900 onwards to 1911 that didn’t
give shareholders something; but from January,
1911, to the present only £9OO has come out of
the bag. Original shareholders can’t complain;
their £1 share has given them £4 Is. If the bot-
tom (No. 17) level had been up to the average, a new
shaft might have been faced ; but as it wasn’t, the
tired directors throw up the sponge with a final
curse at the high wage-costs ruling.
* * * *
Cassilis, Gippsland, wants three things to
set it on its feet: more gold, more stone, more
water. The average of the dirt crushed used to be
SOdwt. ; now it is lOdwt. The ore was a tough
mixture, and a pot of money was spent on plant,
much of which is on the scrap-heap. Finally came
the hydro-electric plant to reduce costs. This should
have been a blessing; but the co. never had the
pluck or the capital to build a second dam necessary
to furnish constant power. Just the want of this
has meant a shut-down since January, and the co.’s
big plant only handled about 4000 tons of ore for
the past half-year. This gave products of the gross
value of £4452. Costs were £4529. Nobody can
get fat on that. The load to be shouldered for the
current term includes a bank overdraft of £3825 to
begin with. So far as the co. has any hopes, they
lie in the stopes over the No. 7, the picking up of
the shoots in No. 8 level, and (later) in using the
plant for a new mine.
Poseidon Alluvial (Vic.) isn’t lacking
support just now, and there seems an off-chance of
the man who fluttered in at 30s. getting out on the
rise, providing he took the precaution to average.
The future depends on what gold will be swept up
from the deep gutter, whither operations are now
trending from the new shaft. What is being done is
to drain the remaining block of the Poseidon lead
from the south-west drive off the south-east, and
simultaneously to tap the deep channel. One of the
swells of the Mines Department wrote the Poseidon
lead down long ago as a duffer. Yet it lives, in
spite of that, on the reputation of prospects of 2oz.
dirt being got from the deep ground and a 440 z.
yield from the old gutter.
. Wandiligong, one of the most consistent
of Vic. dredging propositions, is near the end of its
tether. Another couple of years will about see its
ground exhausted. It has paid £2 18s. Bd. per share
in divs. ; and there is probably Bs. or thereabouts
still to come out of the holding.
* * * *
Mining annals of Forest Creek, one of the
earliest goldfields in Victoria, which was fabulously
rich in the ’so’s, close —temporarily, anyhow—with
the burial of the Francis Ormond. The old show has
been a consistent call-maker for a long while past.
* * * *
Oaks Amalgamated (Q.) has just under-
gone some changes, and F. Laby becomes general
manager. He has had Victorian experience, and is
expected to reduce costs. There is no great quantity
of good ore showing in the mine, and for the present
he reckons he will be doing very well to keep five
stamps going on mine ore, leaving five free for out-
side parcels. *
Hampden (Q.) strikes what may be a
large body of 13 p.c. pyritic ore at 412 ft. But
Hampden 'wants the confidence of the market, and
the purchase of the Macgregor at a figure much
above the price paid by the inside men for most of
this derelict’s scrip made that confidence even thin-
ner. Also the co.’s scheme of giving people long
options on big parcels of reserve shares is rotten, its
most obvious result being the creation pf a covered
“bear” by the people who hold the option.
* * * *
Engineer Newman’s report to the direc-
tors of the Kulamadau (Woodlark Island) is a
plain-spoken testimony to the rottenness of past
management. No attempt has been made to segregate
costs, so waste has been rampant. The ground is
soft and easy to work, vet the output doesn’t reach
a ton per man per shift. The total mine cost, New-
man says, ought to be cut down from 15s. Bd. to
9s. or under. No attempt has been made to get a
metallurgical balance by assaying mill heads and
tails and Wilfley table products, with the result
that the management is in darkness on that most
important point—the degree of fine grinding re-
quired. Stores have had the habit of being taken
out without orders being put through the books.
Timber costs 50 p.c. more than it should. These
and other things show what St burden the mine has
had to carry, and yet it has managed to struggle
along somehow. The other side of Newman’s picture
is painted a brilliant chestnut. The lodes are classed
among the “deposits in tertiary andesites,” like those
at Waihi. There doesn’t appear to be much evidence
of regular shoots of ore, the richness of the lode de-
pending apparently upon the presence of galena-
blende-caleite veins. After the mine has had the
knocking into shape urgently needed, and the surface
equipment has been largely remodelled, at a cost of
about £20,000, some good things ought to be in
store. An efficient plant to treat 200 tons of ore
per day by amalgamation, fine grinding and cyanid-
ing, is estimated to give a profit of 15s. per ton of
ore. Total costs would be 255., and 100,000 tons of
40s. ore are said to be a not at all extravagant esti-
mate of reserves. Anyway, 50,000 tons of half-
ounce ore is declared to be an absolutely safe figure.
Newman, who makes this plain-spoken report, was
one of Capt. Richard’s young men at Mount Morgan.
When Richard was asked to go to Waihi to report
on that tottering proposition, he chose Newman as
his assistant, no doubt to guide him past the geologi-
cal pitfalls, because the ex-chief of Mount Morgan is
no geologist. Before that he was at Broken Hill,
working both underground and in the mills;; and
before that again he had a particularly brilliant
course in Sydney Uni. Engineering School.
Mount Elliott has 150.000 shares of £5,
and looks as if it would pay 10s. per quarter for two
years or so. France holds most of the scrip, having
been encouraged thereto by a brassy circular, headed
“Polvtechnique des Mines D’Or,” which tipped these
shares for “ £2O and £2s”—on the alleged state-
ment of General-manager Corbould that the mine can
pav £2 a vear for “some years.” With people
solidly holding such stock for such figures, the pur-
chase of 500 or 1000 shares hardens them to adam-
ant. There is no scrip in Australia to speak of, and
Sydney “bears” have had to bid up to get any that
had to be delivered quickly. This is the sort of
thing that booms a market and produces utterly fic-
titious values. # * .

Coronet Hill Development Co. looks a
reasonable gambling proposition. The big iron out-
crop to be exploited (there are four leases of 80 acres
each) is two miles in length, at Mount Davis, North-
ern Territory. Five years ago, S.A. Government Geo-
logist Brown summed it up as a strong formation, worth
prospecting ; and recently Aloysius Kelly, brother of
Bowes, declared himself “more impressed by the
Coronet mine than by any other formation 1 have
seen since the Broken Hill days.” A shaft sunk to
100ft. on one of the leases cut 7ft. of oxide of copper,
which appears to be going down with the shaft. A
bulk assay gave copper 15 p.c., silver 22oz. and ldwt.
of gold to the ton. There may be a lot in it. The
Development Co. proposes £20,000 capital in 2000
shares —500 to be held in reserve, and 1000 to go to
owners, promoters and underwriters.

WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, February 24, 1913.
My Dear Arini.—
Humors of divorce are buzzing all around
us like mosquitoes. The names connected
with the various unhitchings belong to well-
known folk. Of course they can’t be men-
tioned except verbally and in the absence
of witnesses, but later oh there will be some
thrilling revelations. Husbands and wives
are leaving their own partners and setting
to corners in a kind of matrimonial kitchen
lancers. The _ D’Arcy Osbornes have “got
their absolute.” People are remembering
the Mrs. D’Arcy Osborne was the pretty
Dorothy Mair, who was once engaged to poor
Leslie Gaden—-the man who died after a
reckless fight with a tramear some time last
year.
Then there were further developments.
Everybody was wildly interested in news
that Mr. Theo Marks was on the eve of
marrying Mrs. D’Arcy Osborne when last
mail left London, where the bridegroom has
been staying for some little time. His
family has a knack of making marriages
or a more or less sensational character,
simply because as a family the Marks are
more than common tall' and attractive,
lheo has been a beauty man about town
tor years, and many hearts have beat for
him in vain. Alfred Marks, whom Sydney
knew as an extremely handsome vouth, mar-
ried a really beautiful Gaiety girl, Claire
Leighton. The sisters of these brothers are
blessed with good looks. People used fairly
to mob Irene Marks (Mrs. Yivers) when
she appeared at races or garden parties, so
well did she fit the hackneyed lines about
the daughter of the gods, divinely tall and
most divinely fair.
Mr. Theo Marks and his bride, it was re-
reported, were to spend their honeymoon at
Monte Carlo. Unless all their plans have
been altered, they are there now. I am
told by those who have observed Mr.
Theo at his. favorite pastime, he is one of the
! 3eS il~C 1 i nean^n g co °l es t and most imper-
turbably good-humored— gamesters that this
vast country possesses. He loses £5OO with
the same bland and courteous cheerfulness
that he displays upon winning 2s. 6d. The
place where rouge wins sometimes and Wane
sometimes, but the Blanc family always
should suit him down to the ground.
• T , h ,- e en ? a sement is announced of Mr. Ber-
tie Morris, the fortunate prey being a South
African maiden. For years and years no
local tea-fight has been really complete with-
out this indefatigable social warrior; and
, a . laek - the struggle is over, the lance
ot fashion is couched or laid to rest or burst
or . what not, the social revolver, bowie
knives, etc., are returned to the armory, and
the hatchet is interred. One of the saddest
features of matrimony is its habit of wrest-
ing our Galahads from us. Mr. Morris is
secretary to Mr. W. A. Holman, the Labor
Attorney-General who dragged Government
House from the clutches of Lord Denman,
and in so doing, according to Mr. Henley,
M.L.A., dealt a stunning blow at the unitv
of Empire. But that Mr. Morris, despite
his associations, is personally a Socialist
and an Empire-burster I am not prepared
to say.

•Tim Burns has been fixed for the 27th of
next month.
c. rh ?Cameron-Lindeman wedding at
Strathneld was a large and festive affair,
lhe bride is the eldest daughter of Mr. and
™' C. F. Lindeman, of Bromhall, Strath-
field. The bridegroom, Mr. Roderick M.
Cameron, hails from New York. It was a yel-
low wedding, and the very latest novelty was
introduced by the bride, who had color show-
ing up the pattern of her lace bodice and
sleeves, the beautifully dainty point de gaze
being backed with pink ninon. The brides-
maids, Misses Lilian Hoare, Nita Linde-
man, Kate Campbell and May Moses, wore
the fashionable tint of yellow with excellent
effect, and the contrast of their brown vel-
vet hats made good. Two child train
bearers, Marjorie Lindeman and Marion
I riend, had the same color scheme in frocks
and muffs. It was an autumn wedding,
and looked most artistic. The elect of
Strathfield showed up at the reception, Mrs.
Lindeman wearing art blue velvet, finished
with gold embroidery, and a hat with the
pluckings of a large ostrich adorning it.
Messrs. Colin Caird, Norman Dangar and
R. Lindeman were groomsmen. It’s about
up 0 p CaLd to figure as principal at
an affair of this kind himself. He has been
almost but not quite” at multitudes of
fashionable fixing-ups. As a best man there
is no point of the game in which he needs
coaching.
Dr. and Mrs. Herbert Marks are off to
England on the first Orient liner that is
willing and able to supply them with a suffi-
ciency of cabins. They mean to be awav
about a year.
Mrs. Edwin Ryrie, Sydney’s Dresden-
China shepherdess beauty, who has iust
come back from her travels, had a peculiar
though very sincere, compliment paid to the
charming childishness of her little girl That
maiden so strongly impressed a dusky poten-
tate met en route that he wished to buy the'
girlie, and offered a lac of rupees for her.
lie may not have been a potentate, but at
aay he was dusky, and as a story of
this kind is never told without it contains
a dusky potentate and a lac (not lack) of
rupees, it bears on its face the stamp of
verity. Since her return, Mrs. Ryrie is living
the quiet life, goes nowhere and sees nobody.
Cranbrook is full of packing cases and
gripsacks. Our departing Governor and
family came down from Sutton Forest last
week, after gladdening the hearts of Mr.
and Mrs. Mackenzie, of Nowra, with a visit
per motor, and now are actively preoaring
for the getaway. The newcomers, Sir Ger-
ald and Lady Edeline Strickland, are get-
ting busy already. The useful Victor Hood,
as advance agent, has got out cards for the
initial garden party on March 27. Sir Ger-
ald and his fair-haired missus are no stran-
gers, and what little we have seen of them
up to now we have liked. He is a man with
a past to live up to, as the only dancing
Viceroy Australia has yet seen. It is a
treat to watch him at a ball. He doesn’t
dance as if it hurt him, as most Govs. do.
Then lie has a power of language. At a
dinner party, he never (so those who have
been there tell me) allows his guests to feel
silence _ falling with a dull sickening thud,
but chips in and talks for all he is Avorth.
And anyone who has heard the thud of a
r;atly big silence will appreciate the value
of that.
Lady Edeline Strickland wears her first
name all the time, and you must call her
Lady “Eedlin” (unless of course vou are of
such humble rank that she wouldn’t have
you call her anything). The reason why
her baptismal appellation doesn’t come off
is because, as the daughter of an Earl (de
la Warr) she is of higher rank than her
husband, who is merely a bart., and doesn’t
count as a nobleman at all.
All our social magnates are this season’s
goods. With absolutely no left-over, cold
storage bigwigs in Society’s larder, we ought
to have a merry time next month when
things begin to make up. The Town Hall
people are new. Mrs. Cocks, though she has
not yet shown us her social side, looks
smart, and festive enough for anything.
Then Government House has a new ruler.
There is no Drake, and no naval supremacy
to worship from afar, except our very
own Australian made-for-local-consumption
brand. So things ought to be shaken up
considerably, until the rattle thereof resounds
from Cape York to the Leeuwin.
Several Queensland visitors are in our
city at present, but most of them are merely
birds of passage on the wing to Hobart.
Mrs. H. _ Midson is making a short stay
here. Miss Cockburn and Miss D. Ander-
son, of Brisbane, were among the guests at
a tea party given by Mrs. Midson last week.
Wise leaves > at short notice,
on the Marmora for London, where he will
engage in the Vend Appeal struggle. Sir
Robert Finlay, who is to lead for the
Crown, personally requested the Australian
Government to retain the Sydney K.C. —the
man who Avasn’t offered a seat on the High
Court bench, because, presumably, the place
was wanted for Piddington.
Mr. and Mrs. Ernest Watt have taken
Lady Tree’s house in Sussex.
. Curnow, son of the late editor of
the S.M.H., went to London last week to
join the staff of the Times.
Such a funny state of things has cropped
up in connection with Dr. Mary’s Booth’s
championship of Dr. Gertrude Halley. After
Dr. Mary had worked up press and personal
indignation about the way in which Dr.
Gertrude had been turned down for the post
of School Medical Officer, a meek and mild
little par appeared in one of the dailies to
the effect that Dr. Halley was all right,
thank you, and didn’t need any defence put
up on her account, as she was perfectly well
satisfied with the decision of the Depart-
ment which appointed a mere man to the
job. A vigorously conducted meeting of pro-
test was engineered by Mrs. Bogue Huffman
and Dr. Booth at the Queen Victoria Club.
In a few well-chosen words they dealt with
Dr. Halley’s “case,” just at the time when
that lady was protesting her hardest that
she had no case for any other ladies to utter
any well-chosen words about. Impartial on-
lookers at ,the queer “impasse” consider that
Dr. Mary Booth is absolutely and entirely
right in her attitude of protest agin the
ways of officialdom; while Dr. Halley, by
upholding that fetich of her profession,
medical etiquette, can never put herself in
the wrong. That both Doctors Booth and
Halley may come out on top is the fervent
Avish of all admirers. History repeats it-
self. This little breeze is just like what
happened when Stanley, the explorer, went
to find Emin Pasha. After that errant Aus-
trian had been discovered in Africa and
brought home in triumph, it came out that
Emin had never wanted to be found at all,
and as soon as he decently could, he dis-
appeared from civilisation and lost himself
again with the greatest promptitude and
despatch.
(i Cady Cullen presided at a meeting of the
. After Care” Association last Thursday look-
ing very fit after her return from Tasmania,
where, with the Chief Justice, she spent
her summer holiday. The After Care Asso-
ciation deals with patients who may need
attention after leaving the hospitals for the
insane, and in a quiet way it does kind sym-
pathetic work in preventing relapse. It
can’t do very much, as it has very little
money.
My forgetful sex again! A Maoriland
correspondent who recently went round the
Islands on the Atua met on the voyage a
dear old Scotch lady, exceptionally agile and
energetic for her 60 years. “As we neared
Samoa,’* says my correspondent, “she in-
quired if I meant to visit the Stevenson
monument, and confided that friends in
Scotland had entrusted her with a bunch of
dried heather to place on the tomb. When
the mountain on which R.L.S. lies came in
view the enthusiasm of most intending
climbers waned ; but the old lady and I de-
termined to try it. We drove as far as we
could, and then toiled with a couple of
natives up the awful incline, and arrived
gasping and exhausted at the top. ‘Now,’
said I, after a short rest, ‘we’ve no time to
lose. Let’s have the ceremony right away.’
My old lady had a wild scrimmage in her
handbag. ‘Dear, dear,’ she wailed, ‘if I
haven’t forgotten to bring the heather!’ But
you’ll please not mention it in Edinburgh.”
In a week absolutely devoid otherwise of
notable events, and as bare of social news
as a comet, the Jap warships left last
Thursday. In the sere and yellow leaf of
their visit they gave an extremely well-done
party. It has by now receded into that un-
comfortable middle distance of happenings
which makes it too old for news and too re-
cent for history, but it is worth a par. all
the same. The flagship Azuma was in-
spected with great curiosity by guests who
wished to see what it looked like after having
been sunk and fished up again from the bot-
tom of Port Arthur. But it was a battle-
ship at play as Sydney folk saw it at the
party, for all the grim guns were wreathed
in flowers. Mrs. Jarley presided as goddess
of the scene, in tableaux representing the
four seasons in symbolical style. Alleged
historical personages, doing various stunts,
were the attractions of the decks. No war-
like shows were presented. The star tab-
leau was excessively peaceful, showing a
gentleman of a literary turn inditing a
poem in a very prettily-done grove of paper
cherry blossom. The legend above described
him as a royal poet (duly qualified to wear
two swords) writing his world-famous war
ballad as described above. The .Taps are
adepts in making curly chrysanthemums out
of colored paper, miniature gardens out of
sand and greenery, smoking microscopic to-
bacco pipes and generally behaving like art-
adoring innocents. After an hour spent
amid this kind of thing, also watching a
friendly bout at wrestling and some harm-
less sword play between smiling opponents,
it needs a violent wrench of one’s mental
capacity to remember that these are the re-
creations of some of the grimmest and
fiercest little war-dogs on our planet.
It was so hot at the party that, going
down the ladders to the lower deck to
have to drink, the men looked
like Julius Knight descending to in-
fernal tortures through the circular
trap-door in “The Darling of the
Gods.” The “torture” was delightfully
cool. with a sparkle and plenty of ice
in it, so it was worth going for. Ad-
miral Tochinai and most of his officers speak
English, and there was little difficulty in
making some kind of small talk even with
the youngest middy on board, as the Jap
youths love the opportunity of improving
their English and hasten to converse. Sur-
geon Ishiwa was much liked by all the jnen
who met him here. He is fleet surgeon, and
a very high and mighty officer. At Jeno-
lan, to a man who asked him, “You likee
this, you think welly ni’?” he replied. “Sir,
I have seen most of the finest scenery in the
world, but your Blue Mountains surpass
them all. They are stupendous.” His
English was all right, and his capacity for
pulling the leg of his acquaintance was
really remarkable. Another interesting per-
son was Mr. Yasugi Nobutani. of the Azu-
ma. He belongs to a noble Jap family, one
of whom —Hirose is the family name—per-
formed a notable life-saving feat in the re-
cent war. He lost his own life, and was
described (in English) as “a faithful, brave
and nice warrior, who died in his 23 years
old.”
The conversion of our tallest mountain
to Catholicism was the reason of the week-
end trip of many citizens of the Old Faith.
On Friday the Cooma train was filled with
pilgrims who went to see Archbishop Kelly
hold mass on the summit of Kosciusko. The
hotel finds itself full, for summer among the
heights is becoming popular with pleasure-
loving folk, and the Catholic pilgrimage
was such a novelty that it attracted many,
just out of curiosity.

There was a party at the Women’s Club

Mr. Govers, of the Immigration Depart-
ment. has been to Melbourne, where he met
the British Commissioners of the Dominions
Royal Commission. They are going on to
Maoriland, and will be here on 11th April
Rider Haggard is the showman of the
group, and he is always put on to speak a
piece, as people ask for him, though bv
rights Sir Edgar Vincent is the spokesman
and captain of the team. He has a crowded
past career, having done various kinds of
official stunts of an elevated character. Sir
Edgar, who is 54 and was formerly a lieu-
tenant in the Coldstream Guards, married
a top-notch London beauty, Lady Helen
Duncombe.
Having not long returned from England
Mr. Govers has some suffragette stories. He
says that he visited Winchester Gaol, where
militants had been incarcerated, and the
peculiar feature of their imprisonment was
that the officials never had to resort to for-
cible feeding to break a hunger strike,
whereas in Holloway and other places that
method of administering nourishment is con-
tinually practised. At Winchester they
broke the strike much more humanely, by
simply going on with the routine of placing
each meal in its turn within the prisoner’s
reach, then without word spoken the ward-
ress would take the untasted meal of the
hunger striker away. Then the next meal
would be brought in and taken away, and
so on day after day. Four days of this re-
duced the strikers to reason. When they be-
came ravenous for food, they just couldn’t
resist eating. Almost by instinct they
reached out for their tucker, and the strike
was broken.
Miss Alice. Currie, whose lisrht soprano
has been trained to a certain distance in
altitude at the Garcia School, gave a concert
on Thursday night at the Concordia Hall to
show cause why she should go to Europe to
have the altitude moved up a note or two
higher. Assisting were Miss Rubie Stella
Henry and Mr. Reg Gooud. with songs, and
Mr. Henri Staell at the violin. Mr. Staell.
by the way, lives in a humpy by the surf
all these, hot. months, and only just towels
himself in time to catch the Manly boat
when with great reluctance he has to come
to the city. Geo. S. Holburn ran this con-
cert.
Mrs. T. H. Kelly, in black velvet, looked
a very aristocratic Mrs. Desmond to J. C.
Williamson’s Kerry at the Scott Fund
matinde on Saturday. The soft darkness of
the velvet showed up the whiteness of her
hands, which, was all-to the good of her part.
This generation was surprised to see J.C.W.
as a mummer. “Fancy, he acts, too,” was
their exclamation. “What a clever man he
is.” The “Jimmy Williamson” of Stofel
and Kerry is a person of the dim and dis-
tant past. The nigger minstrel show
brought forward jokes new and old —especi-
ally old. Blanche Browne, Palotta and Co.
were quite unrecognisable under their lamp
black. The best joke bandied about was:
“Why did the tin whistle?” “Because Fred
Bluett” (blew it). “Tell me why Fred
Bluett?” “Because Jack Cannot.” Mrs.
Brough met. with a rousing reception as an
auctioneer introduced by G. S. Titheradge.
The autographed tray cloths went well. Sold
by members of the profesh during the inter-
vals, they had the signatures in ink of stars
of various magnitude, and these can be done
at leisure in Mount Mellick stitch by adorers.
Nellie Stewart and her daughter are
coming back bv the Otranto.

“BILL’S IDEES,”
By A. G. STEPHENS.
Fully Illustrated. PRICE 1 /-; POST FREE, 1/1.
In “BILL’S IDEES” the N.S.W. Bookstall Co., Ltd., has the pleasure of introducing Mr.
A. G. Stephens as a new Australian humorist. Mr. Stephens’s reputation as the doyen of
Australasian critics is widespread, and he has several books of prose and verse to his credit.
“Bill’s Idees” is the first book in which he has given his natural humor free play-—as we venture
to think, with the happiest results.
Many years ago the original of. “Bill” was a larrikin in Sydney—the leader of a “push.” He
reformed himself, and for a long time has been a highly-respected citizen ; as he says, improving
his mind, cultivating his good instincts, and trying to live up to his ideal. Bill’s ideal is “a
quiet bloke with a quiet wife and a quiet dog with a quiet name.” Bill had had to struggle to
reach his ideal, because be had a lot of bad instincts to get rid of, and because he never had much
chance in the way of education. Still, with the help of his favorite author, Vere Foster, he is
getting along nicely.
Bill’s story is told in his own words. He is interesting when he looks back upon his
unregenerate past, and is no less interesting when he contemplates his reformed present. Hard
life has taught him a philosophy of his own, worth considering. His native humor is of the kind
called “pawky,” and he .enjoys a joke against himself. Bill isn’t a fool and he isn’t a clown;
perhaps his readers will seldom laugh right out. But they will get a few broad grins and a
whole heap of chuckles.
Bill’s “missus” is several chapters to herself.

You Know Dickens,
But DO You Know Schumann?

THE creator of “Little Hell” you surely know and love. His books have
furnished hour after hour of amusement. David Copperfield and
Oliver Twist, Jenny Wren and Agnes Wakefield —all of his characters
are almost as living friends to you.
Suppose by an accident of environment you had never enjoyed
Dickens —that his works had no meaning: for you—that you
had never read them. And, further, suppose that not only
Dickens, hut Shakspeare, Milton, Thackeray, Irving and all
literature were forbidden you. The world would be dull
indeed.
Yet, do you know Eobert Schumann—as great in music as Dickens in
literature? Dickens wrote “Nicholas Nickelby,” but Schumann composed
“Carnaval.” “A Christmas Carol” is no greater or more appealing than
“Traumerei.” Who can listen to “The Two Grenadiers’' without a thrill
of exultation? And Schumann is but one of a thousand famous composers.
Don’t imagine that to revel in music you must first study.
You can play to-day if you will only say “I will.” You can
play the

Mechanical? NO! Its effects are no more mechanical than
those of a first-class pianist. The notes are correct—is that a
fault? Paderewski doesn’t think so. Nor does any artist who
strives to get that quality into his own playing.
The expression, the feeling, the interpretation—the gentle
will hive all the true musical atmosphere and feeling it is
possible to have.
Do you realise that in not knowing music intimately you are denying your-
self as great an education and culture as all literature combined—denying
yourself as keen a pleasure ?
