MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, February 24, 1913.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Rider Haggard bears a remarkable like-
ness to Henry Wrixon—or the Henry of 10
years ago. During the few days the Trade
Commissioners spent in Melbourne the
famous romancer seemed to walk in an atmo-
sphere of originality. He says carefully
audacious things that are calculated to shat-
ter the placid calm of any conventional as-
semblage. At the Parliament House lunch-
eon, he sat opposite Alex. Peacock. The
famous Peacock laugh fascinated Haggard,
who seemed to deliberately provoke fresh
cackles with well-directed sallies. Haggard
is alarmingly anxious to pose as an agricul-
turist. Farming and the cult of the Cow
have long been his hobbies. “I’ve lived in
a cornsack for seven years,” was one of his
confessions.
i A hurriedly arranged dinner at Federal
Government ilouse on the evening of landing
was one of the chief diversions of the Trade
Commissioners. It turned out to be a highly
entertaining function. The arrivals were
well-stocked with fresh impressions, and had
heaps to say. A few people were gathered
together to meet them, and there were not
more than 60-diners at table. Gold plate
and crimson rose-bushes in gold basket tubs
made the board groan expensively. The
Denman lady wore white, and blazed with
rubies and diamonds. Only two of the Com-
missioners had their wives with them. Mrs.
Tetlow, one of the fortunate ladies, is quiet
and shy, and much addicted to the protect-
ing arm of her husband. She wore a sub-
dued air and a soft white gown. Mrs. Tom
Garnett, the other guest, looked handsome in
a black and white gown. JJaugnter ivirs.
Geoffrey Syme also wore black and white—
but it was a more flimsy frock in which white
preponderated. Mrs. Anderson, from Syd-
ney, had two pretty daughters with her. Airs.
George Chirnside was resplendent in blue
brocade shot with silver and a fender of
diamonds. (Note. —The fender wasn’t in-
volved in the shooting; it was on her head.)
Mrs. Chapman, another Sydney visitor, was
surrounded by a lovely black lace dress. In
the drawing-room the visitors found a lovely
new carpet to admire —a velvety blue thing
of exceeding beauty that had only been put
down that morning. The little G.-G. had to
climb into uniform and motor off to camp
as soon as the dinner was over.
The Trade Commissioners, during their
brief rush of entertainment, spared one night
for the Theatre Royal and “A Midsummer
Night’s Dream.” The party was rather
badly arranged from a social point of view,
as each man sat with his own wife. (There
is no more effective way of killing conver-
sation.) The Fuller lady rolled along in her
yellow car, and Chamberlain Nevill, of the
G.-G. household, sat in unofficial dress in the
front row of the circle, joining the supper
party later on. The supper was served in
the theatre lounge after the show. Oscar
Asche and his Lily came round in the
dresses they wear in the last act. Lily
looked really beautiful in the bare-armed
wedding garment; but Oscar, in the rough
garb of Bottom, put a long overcoat on to
help him through. His bare brown legs
looked like the plump supports of a grand
piano.
The University did badly in securing only
part of the Commissioners’ sailing day.
Nearly every man was juggling desperately
with baggage and reluctant to spend the last
hurried hours absorbing more hospitality.
A skeleton party finally met the mortar
boarders and mopped weary brows while
they talked and drank the usual cordials. It
was a very jaded half-dozen that finally
escaped in the Maoriland steamer.
The Caulfield course preened itself be-
neath blue skies, on the second Saturday of
the V.A.T.C. Autumn Meeting. Everyone
had contrived to be there. The G.-G. strolled
along just out of tripping distance of his
willowy lady, who dexterously manipulated
trailing lengths of filmy black and white
chiffon, topped off with a white-plumed hat
that gave her the appearance of a major-
general on parade. Among the men in at-
tendance, Captain Burnham was a dream or
study in mole, with the loveliest scrambled-
egg-colored gloves. A fearful eruption of
purple bubbled up everywhere in the form
of shoes, stockings, gloves, hats, bows, and
every conceivable sort of trimming. The
modest violet has certainly been done to
death this time by the drapers. So plentiful
is it that most women are sick of it already.
It will probably go out with a rush before
winter is over.
On the lawns were Millers, Brownes, Mad-
dens, Baillieux and all the motorcracy. Mrs.
Tom Garnett, in black relieved with touches
of pale blue, was attended by daughter Mrs.
Geoffrey Syme. Oscar Asche was proudly in
attendance on the lovely Lily, who wore a
midsummer dream of rose ninon and lace.
Tea was quite a pleasant and easy func-
tion. It was set out on long tables in the
commodious new shelter, and nobody got
trodden on or spattered. Also there seemed
to be enough waiters to go round. Those
who expected to see an array of autumn
fashions just didn’t, for the day turned out
so sunny that all sensible women gladly
hung up the new garments and went out in
summer’frocks. A few poor, misguided crea-
tures broiled in winter felts, and said nasty
things about the calendar, a document the
free Australian climate frequently ignores.
The wife of a military newcomer lately
went to return the call of a Toorak matron,
and, on the way to the house, discovered the
lady in the garden. “I’m not really at home,
you know!” moaned the called-on in an-
guished tones. “Nobody is at home in Mel-
bourne in January,” continued the she-ass
amongst the garden thistles. “I have to be
in Melbourne at present; but I just shut
myself up in the house, you know. I’m not
calling, or being at home to anyone, just
now!” The would-be caller got outside with
all possible speed, nodded brightly to the
absent one, and walked off shaking with
laughter. It seems that some of the sol-
emnly suburbanites hereabout recog-
nise “seasons” for being in and out of town.
January is a socially dead month amongst
this curious sect, and any poor wretch who
has to stay in town during that time uses
the back streets as much as possible, lurks
indoors, and generally ceases to “play
ladies.”
The divorce of the week, not to say of the
season, occurred on Monday when Mr.
Meyer, some time of the Asche-Brayton Co.,
legally tore himself from the lady whom
playgoers knew well, a year or so back, as
Miss Dorothy Grimston. There was no de-
fence. Mr. and Mrs. Meyer were popular
in Society both here and in Sydney. For
one thing, in the dreadful phraseology of
Suburbia, they are “well-connected at
Home.” He is a nephew of Lord Burnham.
She a daughter of Mrs. Kendall, who for
many years held the proud record of being
the most virtuous female on the English
stage. She was in the habit of saying so
herself, and it was generally admitted that
she ought to know. There is a story that a
crowd of admiring playgoers presented Mrs.
Kendall with a pearl necklace as a tribute
to her superb uprightness. Also, there is a
sequel to it, in the form of a bon mot or
epigram, or envious sneer, or something,
uttered by Mrs. Langtry when she learned
of the event. But the latter is not for the
readers of this serious old family journal.
Miss Grimston acted in “The Flag Lieuten-
ant” with Tith., Miss Ethel Warwick and
others, and being tall and svelte and comely,
made fairly good. Her late husband does
not act. He was one of Asche’s business
managers. At the present time he is mana-
ging a farm.
A party of girls and a demure chaperone
went boating the other night on the covered
aqueduct or roofed gutter that trickles round
Luna Park. Their boat bumped another,
and the result was an upset. Their pretty
dresses were ruined, their tempers likewise,
and, in addition, the chaperone developed
symptoms of rheumatism. The damp crew
sought out the manager, tearfully detailed
the facts, and asked what he intended to do
about it. The genial official thoughtfully
masticated his cigar awhile. At last he spoke.
“Look here! Would you care for a free
ticket for to-morrow night, or a free ride on
the river naow?” He certainly was most
surprised when those people curtly refused
both offers and trailed off home to change.
Guess you couldn’t squeeze any thanks out
of some people with a hydraulic press.
A party of Ramsays and Baillieux are
booking their passages to England by an
outgoing steamer. Mary Ramsay, who mar-
ried the absurdly wealthy son of a promi-
nent whisky, is joining the party with her
husband.
Ernestine Stephenson is back again in
Melbourne. Ernestine is one of the daugh-
ters of the late squatter Stephenson, who be-
longed to the early days, and at one time
had money to burn. The conflagration con-
cluded some time ago. When he departed
this life, he left his wife and numerous
daughters little more than a huge, towered
and turreted castle in Toorak which needs
an impossible army of retainers and a for-
tune to keep up. In these domestically help-
less days, it is a white elephant of vast
dimensions to private lifers, and even
frightens the intending buyer who wants a
commodious place for a paying guestery.
Daughter Ernestine has been ‘the favored of
Fate, having spent long and frequent periods
abroad with the lady who once was Mrs.
Rupert Clarke. Ernestine was her brides-
maid when she took Rupert for better or for
worse; and recently, in London, she acted
in the same capacity, when her friend made
her second marriage and became the wife of
Grey-Egerton. It looks rather like a record
in bridesmaiding.
A black-eyed French-Australian youth,
reckoned by the wisely fond mothers of Mel-
bourne as an eligible of the first rank, has
captured a Tasmanian squatteress. Adequate
preparations are being made to proclaim the
alliance from the roof gardens. (This is
merely an advance warning, in order that
you may get ready to cheer and caper like
the chorus when the hero wins the leading
soprano and the village policeman has his
foot on the villain’s throat.)
Dr. Archie Hug, who only recently hoisted
the engaged signal, is hurrying the wedding
along. He will wed Miss May Duckett the
day after the Feast of April Fool.
The mother-in-law joke will have to be re-
vised. The devotion of a young husband to
his pretty young mother-in-law has caused
trouble —serious trouble that may end in
court. If there can be said to be any moral
about the affair it is that mothers-in-law
have no right to be young and fascinating.
Lawyer Kilpatrick is selling out, home
and baggage, law books and all, and depart-
ing to Kngland. His health, for some time
past, has been a thing of shreds and patches.
He has always been a friend and admirer
of Alfred Deakin, and it may be that the
prospect of public life without Alfred is too
depressing.
Lawyer James Lawson, of Melbourne, has
impressive if not absolutely staggering tid-
ings re his brother, Major-General Lawson.
The warrior was recently married, in
London, to the Countess Lathom, a re-
nowned beauty, and widow of a nobleman
who was something or other in the house-
hold of the late Edward. The daughter of
the Countess was married, on the same day
as mamma, to a lordling. Lawyer James has
another brother who supports a Judge’s wig
in Dublin. It gives him a thrill of pride to
think how greatness has been thrust upon
his relations.
Solicitor Macdonald and his wife returned
from foreign places to tind themselves be-
sieged for parental blessings. Son Hector,
who is a partner in the legal business, had
become engaged to June Clapp, and the
youngest Macdonald daughter was confi-
dently asking for a ticket of admission to the
family circle for wealthy flour-miller Ivimp-
ton. Mr. and Mrs. Macdonald had iust left
their eldest daughter in England, receiving
congratulations on her engagement to a mon-
eyed young planter from the Canary Islands.
She is to hunt the trousseau in London, and
be married from the house of Macdonald in
Scotland. Australia will not see her again
before the wedding.
The death of young Frank McLaughlan
came as a great shock to his father, “Jimmy”
McLaughlan, the popular president of the
Australian Club. The family were boating,
launching and summering generally at the
Gippsland Lakes, and Frank, a youngster of
21, took a brief holiday to Sydney. There he
was suddenly attacked by appendicitis, and
was carried off to hospital, only to die almost
immediately. The boy was engaged to Miss
Gibson, daughter of the wealthy Gibsons
who take prizes with their sheep from the
Deniliquin run which adjoins the Mac pro-
perty. The youngster was a grand nephew
of the well-known “Bobbie” Rand, the rich
old bachelor who died intestate, like Tyson,
and whose enormous wealth was distributed
amongst the Rand family. The first Mrs.
McLaughlan, the boy’s mother, was a Rand,
and came in for a big portion. The present
Mrs. Jim, an American beauty, is daughter
of a millionaire boss of some big electrical
companies in Boston.
A person called “Seatholder” has been
pouring forth inky lamentations about the
stiff-necked, chillsome -ways of the modern
parson, Anglican or “dissenting.” The par-
ish incumbrance collects his pew rents as of
old, but he seems to have quite lost his relish
for afternoon tea. weak gossip and garden
party loitering. What is the advantage in
paying for a pew if it leads to no social or
vestry dissipations of an improving nature?
“Seatholder,” apparently, is a gentle spin-
ster with a weakness for curates, and it is
too cruel to think that her mite is powerless
to procure so much as one soul-sustaining
glance or blessed hand-clasp. The purchas-
ing power of the sovereign is waning in all
directions.
Agnes Murphy is still wandering from one
resort to another, trying to regain some of
the health spent in 18 busy years of work
and travel. Still she asserts that, were she
free of her Quinlan contract and loose in
London, she would muster up sufficient
energy to go suffragetting.
Croquet, the sedate dissipation of our
grandmothers, will fairly rage in March, and
a number of the Very Best People are now
engaged knocking fat wooden spheres round
smooth, hoop-littered lawns, by way of pre-
paration for the first of the month, when the
grim work is scheduled to start. The cham-
pionship is down for March 6.
A flaming scarlet poster, issued by the
Essendon River League, bears witness that
Henley-on-Maribyrnong is imminent. The
trickle has a geographical association with
the Saltwater River, a stream well known to
horse-racing enthusiasts who like to see the
Flemington gallops cheaply. The boiling-
down studios adjoining the stream are a bit
of a handicap from a social point of view:
but I am told that it isn’t so bad when the
tide is high. Presumably, carriages may be
ordered just before the ebb.

Beatrice Day is at present suffering with
a sprained arm and wrist. The damage was
done by jumping off a St. Kilda tram and
colliding with a buggy. In these days of
motor accidents, only a thoroughly original
woman would be run down by a horse-drawn
vehicle.
From my Adelaide corresnondent:—
The Turner-Ayers, Dean-Gordon, Milne-Fotherine-
ham and Moulden-Wood knot-tyings are all fixed for
March or early April. Mrs. Box and her daughter
leave for Sydney next week, and the marriage of the
latter to Lieut. J Lewis, R.N., will take place at
Garden Island on March 11.
Talking of weddings, the fine old English sport
of nce-throwing on railway platforms is doomed. At
the final scene of a recent marriage celebration a total
stranger to the joyous party got about a pound and
a half of raw rice in the left eye. He protested to
the Railways Commissioner, and now the manly old
pastime has been put on a legal par with brick-
heaving.
A number of guests at the Peake farewell banquet
were smitten with ptomaine poisoning. It might
have been worse. There were some politicians there
who deserved prussic acid.
The death of Mr. A. Williams will mean a general
move-on in the Education Department. The late
director, who was born at Kaumantoo in ’63, began
at Moonta in ’76 as a pupil teacher. He became
director early in 1906, when the late Thomas Price
was Premier and Minister of Education. He was
keen on his job, and by study, experiment and trav.i
did what he could to boost it along.
The Mount Crawford polo team met Adelaide at
Plympton on Saturday, and made the haughty citv
sportsmen sit up considerably. Mrs. George Bagot
administered tea to the Bosanquets, Mr. and Mrs.
Row, the Leonard Bakewells, Alick Murrays, Warrens,
Mrs. Tom Barr Smith, Mrs. Stanley Murray and
Mrs. O’Leary, and others.
The Willie Popes are on their way back from
England, and with them is Margaret, the good-look-
ing daughter of Mr. Herbert Hughes, of Booyoolie
station. The Bishop Thomases are leaving us.
Their holy roof will shelter the Teasdale Smiths while
they are away.
The Light Horse camp at Smithfield narrowly
missed death by starvation. In fact, if the men had
sat down and waited for food to be pushed into their
mouths, none would have survived. As it was, they
walked out and got it, and so there are no deaths to
record. The Y.M.C.A.’s “Field Service Department’’
helped to obviate what would have been one of the
grimmest tragedies in our brief island story.
A man who weighs 7cwt. has arrived here from
England. He had to be carted from the Outer
Harbor to his hotel in a truck, and now his mana-
ger is wondering deliriously how he is to be hauled
over our vast and mostly narrow-gauged railway sys-
tem in a manner that will show a profit on the
outlay. The working exes, of a venture of this sort
have to be reasonable, or loss is a certainty. A fat
man is not like a classical opera or a brilliant play.
People do not patronise him over and over again.
They merely come once, say “How awful!” or words
to that effect, and then go home and basely tell their
friends that, as a work of art, he is not worth the
3d. Meanwhile, the immense person is eating and
drinking furiously, lest he decline in weight anil
lose his market value. If freight-troubles are added
to the others, the day will dawn when entrepreneurs
will give over elevating the minds of their fellows by
carting outsize specimens of humanity about the
world.
The Turf and the Church are on nodding terms in
friendly S. Aus. Whitmore Carr, who (as “Tar-
quin”) was sporting editor of the Register for
many years, made money in shares, then married a
small mint, and now chairmans both the Adelaide
Racing Club and Stock Exchange. Lately, he has
presented a beautiful reproduction of Holman’s
“Christ and the Rich Ruler” to St. Peter’s Anglican
Church, Glenelg, of which his late wife was a com-
municant. And at a neighboring tabernacle, the
Glenelg Congregational, you can see S. Australia's
best-known trainer, John Hill, in devout attendance
every Sabbath.
Sincerely yours,
JOHANNA.

POLITICAL
POINTS
A Freetrade paper seems to be able to
exult over anything, so long as it has not
given employment to its readers. Last week,
the S. M. Herald joyfully skied its bonnet
about a consignment of 500 cases of oranges
which had just arived from Palestine, re-
marking that it was not often that Sydney
people had (he privilege of eating oranges
grown in the Holy Land. Then, to cheer
up those who had not secured any of the
far-fetched fruit, it added that further con-
signments were expected. It looks like a
hard and unnecessary kick in the waistcoat
to the dear old sheet’s orange-growing Aus-
tralian readers. One would almost think
that the local orange grower was a blastifer-
ous manufacturer of something, instead of
being a decent primary producer.
THE LEGACY.
The FINANCIAL NEWS states that it is estimated
that colonial loans aggregating £120,000,000 must
be renewed or paid before the middle of 1914.
C OHEN: “I want a settlement. Do you re-
member this little bit of business?"
A USTRALIA: “Oh, yairs! That's a legacy what
these coves left me."
“F.C”: Anent Vic. Premier Watt’s pro-
fessed inability to see how the State is to
regulate prices, and how a railway depart-
ment is to be run other than chaotically if
aa outside body fixed the wages it should
pay. The wages of all employees on the
largest employing concern in the State—the
Melbourne Tramway Co. —have been fixed
by an outside body, and Watt’s own State
has all along regulated the prices it might
charge for its wares— i.e., its fares. And—
marvellous thing!—so satisfied is the com-
pany with its position under this “outside
interference” and “State regulation” that
Watt himself has for months been vainly
trying to get the co. to reduce its “State-
regulated” prices.
John Bull’s Territorial force, which re-
presents the final effort to show that a vol-
untary army is still possible, continues to
fade. Last year it declined by 6600 men. It
has been doing that sort of thing almost
since the beginning, and even the beginning
was more of a slump than a commencement.
“F.C.” : Australia’s hybridists are notified
that the Panama-Pacific Exhibition offers a
£2OO trophy for a new rose. If anybody
knows how to make a blue or black one, let
him produce the article and beat Luther
Burbank on his own place of advertisement.
On the whole, though a £2OO trophy is a
desirable possession, Australian brains' might
be better employed on inventing a new ri\j.e,
or some other hurtful piece of defence
machinery.
WHAT DEAKIN SAW.
DEAKIN (one month retired): “Good riddance
to the old thing. If it stays where it is
it'll go to pieces. If it wants to move it
must go either one track or the other, and
if it tries to go on both tracks at once
there's going to be a big smash."
Ballarat (Yic.) provides the first detected
case of false pretences in connection with
the Maternity Bonus, and it cost the lady
£lB Bs. in all. The baby was born 20 days
before the bonus came into force, and the
lady decided that that was near enough to
chance it. Wherefore she forged the local
nurse’s signature and sent in a claim, which
was paid. Then an inquisitive clerk noticed
a difference in the two signatures—and there
was more work for the beak and the bobby.
The State bummers now carry on their
cadging business under a sort of partner-
ship or cobber arrangement. When Barnes
dodges into J. Bull’s, Watt and Peake stay
outside the gate. “Wotcher git?” says Watt
when Barnes emerges. “Million!” says
Barnes with bulging eyes. Then they sit
down and cut it up. Next time Watt does
the “touching”—he is on the way now —and
after him Peake. Then they’ll cut their
swags up if the others’ tucker-bags are thin
at the moment. It’s a beautiful arrange-
ment, and shows, anyhow, that the cadgers
have got down to their right level at last.
Some pernicketty individual in England
opines in a London paper that the Aus-
tralian fleet will have no status, and will
rank merely as a privateer or pirate institu-
tion, because Australia isn’t, by itself, a
“sovereign State.” Yet the Australian fleet
has some precedent behind it. Its status
looks better than that of the old East India
Company’s armed ships. Bulgaria kept a
little fleet at sea when it was merely a
tributary State; and Egypt used to show its
flag on the waters, though it was, and still
is, nominally Turkish territory. Greece
had, by permission, a fleet of a sort when it
was still Turkish property. And seeing
that the Australian States have had their
own naval and military forces (also of a
sort) for 50 years or more, without any
question being raised as to their status, it
seems rather late to begin building a fuss.
In Japan the heavenly attributes of the
Mikado seem to be fading a trifle, for the
population of the capital and other cities has
been rioting with the cheerful abandon of a
crowd of “greasers” hunting the scalp of a
twopenny Mexican President. The chief
fury. was directed against newspapers and
politicians that supported the Government,
and the smash done was memorable. At
Tokio the intense cold finally drove the
wreckers indoors. Japan is a beastly cold
place in winter, which is doubtless the reason
why certain politicians want to fill Aus-
tralia’s Northern Territory with the Chry-
santhemum folk on the plea that they' are
just the sort of people for a hot climate.
“Red Tape, Jun.”:—
The N.S.W. Government has apparently succeeded
at last in temporarily dishing a public servant. He
was a pumper at the Meat Works, Glebe Island, Syd-
ney, for many years, and on his retirement was paid
£lO9 as gratuity instead of the £5OO he expected.
So he went to law. In 1894 his Department was
handed over to the Department of Public Health, and
in 1895 the Public Service Board arrived. The
Board sat on the pump driver, graded him, gazetted
him, and parcelled him out generally as a public
servant. On his retirement, the Government blandly
informed him that he wasn’t a public servant at all,
the Board and the Gazette and the other investigating
parties having made a mistake when they said he
was. This callous view has been upheld by the State
Pull Court with costs against the pumper.
The Board or the Government Gazette are not
charged anything. Probably, the decision of the
Court will be blown out on appeal. If not, the
Government’s luck in trying to beat old servants
for their pensions is changing.
“K.” : Statistician Knibbs is still heaving
out masses of information gathered from the
’ll census. A late bulletin considers the occu-
pations of the Commonwealth’s citizens, and
from this it appears that while persons en-
gaged in industrial pursuits increased, be-
tween ’Ol and ’ll, from 429,466 to 562,337,
the primary producers simultaneously ad-
vanced only from 533,107 to 586,088. The
day is apparently close at hand when the
industrial toilers will outnumber the blessed
“backbone,” which circumstance really
means that Australia is slowly quitting the
foolish practice of dragging masses of raw
material from the land and sending them
away to be turned into merchandise, and is
beginning to do the work herself. Knibbs
divides these primary producers into seven
sub-classes: (1) agricultural, (2) pastoral,
(3) fisheries, (4) forestry, (5) water con-
servation, (6) mining and quarrying, and
(7) the capture of wild animals —and it ap-
pears that while some 15,000-women follow
the first two callings, 78 go a-mining, 17
conserve water, 12 have daily dealings with
forests, 10 live by snaring fish, and 25 earn
a crust at the game of capturing wild ani-
mals. The latter are, however, most likely
organisers of the Women’s Liberal Leagues,
engaged in stalking to its lair the ferocious,
untamed tiger—Sosh.
ON THE EVE OF BATTLE.—
A DILEMMA.
The daily press loudly urges Mr. Cook on to vigor-
ous, aggressive leadership, whilst from various out-of-
way places is heard the faint voice of an occasional
Fusionist timorously advocating a live, national, pro-
gressive Protectionist policy, etc.
Private Bigjohn : “Pity we can't load her up
with real proper powder an' some good
solid shot , Joe; then we MIGHT stand a
chance."
General Jokooic : “No good f me , John!
Think of the danger. The old gun couldn't
stand it. 1 here d be a ’omble disaster,
an' that'd be the end of all of us."
The beautifully comforting gag that “all
Australians are natural fighters” looks
rather foolish in the light of a statement made
recently by Major F. B. Heritage, during a
lecture at Victoria Barracks (Sydney). The
expert said that “at a camp at La Perouse
80 p.c. of the men became gun-shy.... The
preliminary training was non-existent, and
the men shut their eyes before they pulled
the trigger.” It doesn’t seem the sort of
thing that “natural fighters” would do. But
perhaps “natural fighters” are like “natural
jumpers.” One of the breed was entered for
a V.R.C. Grand National some years ago,
and several people, including the writer, went
out to Flemington to watch it beat the
leaping animals that had been merely taught
their job. The “natural” brute hit five suc-
cessive jumps with his nose, four feet and
tail; then the jockey who was steering him
ran him off to save a tragedy. The gallant
brute was quite willing to go on clouting
the timber; but, bv that time, the other
horses that hadn’t stayed to play death-
rattles on their fences were furlongs ahead,
and it wasn’t worth anyone’s while to go on
risking necks. Training may not be abso-
lutely necessary, but the man or horse that
has it possesses a devil of an advantage.
From the London Times :
Mr. A. Metheringham, lace manufacturer r,f
Beeston, Nottinghamshire, announces that, in o’otk, ’
quence of the effect of hostile tariffs, he is Z,
to remove the whole of his plant and machinery t
Hungary, where he is now erecting a factory
will afford employment to 400 hands. 1 Wctl
Why, indeed, should a manufacturer want
ing a market, stay in a Freetrade countrvV
If he does stay, it is the one free market lie
has, while by shifting to a protected country
he can get at least two markets—the Free
trade market, which is open to him wher
ever he goes, and the protected market
which is only free to him if he settles him’
self inside it.
AUSTRALIA'S OPEN WINDOW.
Mr. Gee War, Chinee missionary, of Ade-
laide, speaking lately in the City Mission
Hall on the iniquity of Australians in shut-
ting out his countrymen, quoted trium-
phantly : “God hath made of one blood all
the nations of men.” But why didn’t Gee
War finish his text? It winds up: “And
hath fixed the hounds of their habitation.”
The Sydney gas employees are demanding
an increase of wages because, they say, the
cost of maintaining a family has increased
since 1911 by Bs. or 10s. a week. That, at
all events, isn’t due to the tariff. The poor
old tariff hasn’t moved for quite a number
of years except when it moved downward
by the granting of preference to the Ma
Country. Perhaps it is that foolish approach
towards Freetrade that has caused the
trouble.
Henry Lucy, who perpetrates a London
letter for the S. M. Herald, did a blaze of
glory last week about the vast export and
import trade of the British islands. The
trade is certainly huge, but it isn’t all that
it seems, a great deal depends on the way
the figures are put together. Thus, Britain
includes as foreign trade all
its dealings with Canada.
Newfoundland, the British
West Indies, British Guiana,
the vast British territories in
Africa, India, Ceylon, Aus-
tralia, Maoriland, Hongkong,
Cyprus and a multitude of
other dependencies. But Rus-
sia doesn’t reckon as foreign
trade the buyings and sellings
of the mother country with
Russian Poland, Lithuania,
the Crimea, Bessarabia, Fin-
land, Siberia, Russian Tar-
tary or other comparatively re-
cent possessions. Nor do the
original States of the U.S.
reckon as foreign trade
their dealings with the newer
oues—California, Florida,
Louisiana. Colorado, Texas
and the like. In like manner,
Austria doesn't reckon the
goods it sends to Hungary in
its trade returns. A good deal
of the magnificent commerce of
Britain would disappear if the
Ma Country kept its accounts
in the fashion of Russia or
Austria. In fact, hut for this
fake, or whatever it may be
called, Germany would have beaten it badly
as an exporting country in 1911.
The announcement that a Greek fleet has
set out for the invasion of Asia suggests
that the mills of God generally get there in
the end. Over 2400 years ago the Persian
came to Europe and made Greece, despite
its ultimate success, feel very tired. A cen-
tury or two later the Greek went across to ■
Asia to look for the Persian, and made hiim
feel very dead. Then the Roman conquered'
the Greek, and later the Greek succeeded to
the throne of the Roman. The revived Per-
sian, came to look for that throne, and nearly
got it; and next the revived Greek went out
under Heraclius and obliterated the Per-
sian. Later, the Turk came and stamped
the Greek flat, though the Greek, in the
days of the imperial house of Comnenus
stamped considerably on the Turk before he
went down. And now the Greek, having
taken a long breath and re-inflated himself,
goes out to look for the Turk in the old Per-
sian realm, and to see if he can’t make a
final settlement. Demetrius Phriedphish is
doing pretty well these days, and his prawn
is shining in the forefront of the battle, and
public opinion, which for years has held
him in contempt, begins to slew round his
way. In the old, old days when he was Mil-
tiades Phriedphish he was a hefty warrior,
and there is half a dream that the old days
are coming back.
A COOK ON TRUST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
As to the policy of the Fusion, I have already said,

Government out in the interests of the country.
—Jokook in Melbourne.
It was a sullen, envious cook,
And up against the public pane
He flattened hard his nose to look
At one concocting dishes plain,
Whom of his nice, fat, easy job
It was his heart’s desire to rob.
He pleaded: “Turn that fellow out,
And put me in. He cannot cook.
His meals will make you all too stout.
Oh, let the rascal take his hook,
And give to me the place and pay,
For all your sakes, I humbly pray.
“His menu’s shameful. True that I
Have none at all, and cannot say
If I will give you meat or pie
This day, or next, or any day.
But if I give you none, no doubt
’Twill do you good to go without.
“Your chief, your one concern should be
To toss this fellow from the pub,
And in his office station me,
Regardless of gross thoughts of grub.
He gives you food to make you fit.
But I’ll give you my word for it!”
VICTORIA.
SILAS SNELL.
IMMATERIAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A. Lewis, organiser of the Carters and Drivers’
Union (Melb.), says: “So far as the working classes
are concerned, it is immaterial whether the fiscal
policy is Freetrade or Protection.”
The year is 1933.
Freetrade has bust each industry
In White Australia. Lewis finds
His union scattered to the winds.
We see the poor, benighted man,
A. stowaway to far Japan,
To seek a job where trades exist,
As organising unionist.
VICTORIA.

The contest between Balzac and Jerry
l 6:, 0 ' 1 ? 6 ’ Q. ueens land aboriginal, could hardly be
Svdnev a afaa”” 8 ’ affa T' huge cro ' vd the
Sydney Stadium,. and the grinning blackfellow
t’l •“ hif l h t avor - The g rin was the chief
point about him. As he came crouching towards his
opponent with his abnormally long arms extended
and his copper-brown black with its darker scars
gleaming under the electric light, the writer could
not help a creepy sensation about the spine, for this
seemed to be no fight between man and man, but a
struggle between a partly-tamed forest animal and a
white man whose ancestors had forgotten 50 cen-
turies ago the stealth and craft of primitive tree war-
fare. Erect, grave and looking curiously white by
contrast with his opponent, Balzac seemed uneasy from
the start. Those long arms of the wild thing "beside
him in the ring had put him to sleep in the first
round of an engagement all too recently, and the
frenchman was taking no risks. When they fell
together in the clinches, and white skin touched
black, there seemed to be something uncanny and
unclean m the contact; but the big crowd was un-
conscious of it, and the blackfellow was hailed in
terms of fellowship and intimacy, and urged on to
destroy the white. For one who called the
f renchman s name there seemed to be a dozen
yelling in favor of the nigger. The disparity in
weight was considerable. The Frenchman was
handicapped to the extent of about 81b., and this,
coupled with the black’s greater reach, made it prac-
tically a foregone conclusion that the white would
not win. Still, Balzac carries a punch of some mag-
nitude, and once or twice he got home thrilling wal-
lops which put the grin of his opponent out of
action for a few moments. The black showed great
agility in avoiding punishment, and slipped out of
some ugly corners in a way that it would have
puzzled anything wearing paler skin to imitate. The
crowd was hugely diverted at some of Jerome’s antics.
The large, crouched mass of Binghi in shabby loin-
rag, standing upon big feet, which seemed to be at-
tached to the legs with sections of broomsticks in-
stead of ankles, pawing at his foeman’s glove at long
range, seemed to afford infinite joy to the onlookers.
The game was soon over. Up to the end of the
fourth round the Frenchman must have established
a good lead on points. In the middle of the fifth
round a blow from the black fist to the white body
sent Balzac to the boards, where he remained writli-
ing, and took the count of 10. The last the writer
saw of the battlefield was a Oinghi in a shabby over-
coat waving triumphantly to several thousands of
white supporters, and a Frenchman wounded in his
body and his pride sitting in his corner. The
fight was not much as a spectacle. As an exhibition
of boxing it was a howling absurdity. As a solid
argument for keeping every sort of nigger out of
every white man’s sport it" had its points. All the
same, Jerome played the game with strict fairness,
only it wasn’t the game that white men learn and
practise.

There is no change in the position of the
competing teams in Sydney grade cricket. North
Sydney is still in front with 17 points, while University
and Redfern share second place with 14 each. On
Saturday, Middle Harbor put Glebe down by the
small margin of 14 runs. Central Cumberland wal-
loped Balmain by 200 on the first innings. Redfern
scored a win from Paddington by 60, also on the
first innings. In the Uni. v. Sydney match the seat
of learning, not content with a first-innings win,
closed its second innings with one wicket down for
77 in the hone of getting its opponents out in the
time that still remained for nlay. It didn’t manage
the deed, and Sydney scored so briskly that when
stumps were drawn it only wanted 16 runs for a
straight-out win. As it was, Uni. got a first innings
victory by the narrowest shave. Burwood, after
being behind the hunt in the first innings, scored a
good win by six wickets against Gordon. Waverley
went down to Petersham, but with a whole innings in
which to make the 79 runs required, the inland
suburb saw its stumps fall with such a frightful
clatter that it took five wickets to scrape together the
total. During the day, Noble and Pratten both
carried out their bats for centuries. Of the bowlers,
Wilcocks got six wickets for 23, Cotter four for 22,
and Freeman six for 49.
With only two more rounds to be olayed
the Victorian Cricketing Association pennant seems
likely to go to Fitzroy, unless that team takes things
too easily, and loses a match ; and even in that case
either ’Varsity, South or ’Wood must win the re-
maining games outright. ’Roy’s Saturday’s game
was a matter of interest. It had made but 99 on
the wet wicket of the previous week-end, but had
got seven Students out for 29. Then Hartkopf de-
clared, hoping to put ’Roy through the mill again
and make up the deficit on the second day. But
’Roy got its back up at this cavalier treatment,
made 51 for three wickets, and on the second after-
noon kept going till no less than 193 had been gath-
ered in—leaving ’Varsity 263 to make in less than
two hours. The Students, with a hopeless game on
their hands, played out time, making 59 for three
wickets, and thus lost on the first innings by 70
runs. ’Wood increased its 108 for six wickets to
225, against North’s 84, and set about demolishing
its victims again, but the time ran out with North
151 for nine wickets. P’ran collected 207 to ’Don’s
125, in the first innings, and dismissing the oppo-
sition for 86 in the second, won straight out with
10 wickets to spare. ’Cote, with six wickets down
for 243, declared with a lead over Richmond of 200,
but only succeeded in getting six of the other fel-
lows wickets for 159 when the time was up. East
increased its 142 for nine wickets to 189, all out, of
which young Baring made no less than 135 and
South, responding with 312 for seven wickets, won
on the first innings.

Seastream Was one of the riders who accepted £2O of
stiffening money from O’Neill, but it seems clear
that he revealed the fact to the owner of the horse
he Was riding, and would have won the race—
notwithstanding his “stiffening” fee—if his mount
had been good enough. After the race, Seastream
talked openiy of his luck in getting £2O for doing
what he hadn t done, therefore O’Neill was very
anxious to shut his mouth or to get him to swear
before the racing stewards that no stiffening had
occurred. Then Seastream, who regarded his “sacred
word as a thing which might be fairly sold to a
bOPKfflaker under false pretences, arranged to meet
O -Neill and state his terms for signing a false state-
ment. O Neill, for his part, arranged to have a de-
tective concealed on the premises, and when Sea-
stream was standing out for a £5OO bribe for making
a statement which he probably would have contra-
dicted later on, the bookmaker called forth the de-
tective and professed to give Seastream “into cus-
tody for blackmail.” The acquiescent detective, of
course, didn t really take the jockey into custody, but
he bluffed him into going to a policemen’s office.
Hence the claim for wrongful arrest. The plaintiff
had to convince judge and jury that in all his deal-
mg with the bookmaker he had been merely trying to
get money out of a scoundrel with a view to expos-
mg his scoundrelism. He did convince the jud°-e and
jury. The summing-up was not unfavorable to his
case, and the verdict was for £4OO damages. Ex-
posers of public abuses run in all shapes, and Sea-
stream, the jockey who took a bookmaker’s money
under false pretences, and would have taken as much
more of it as he could get on the same terms, must
iank as an exposer of turf malpractices in the cause
of morality. But it is quite certain that the Sea-
stream class of purist couldn’t turn his virtue to
financial account except in Victoria and N.S.W. The
rascality which benefited him to the tune of £2O in
the first instance, and £4OO in the second instance,
flourishes only in those States where bookmakers are
licensed, and the Tote is prohibited. The notorious
Wave case is, in fact, the nastiest brick in the eye
which opponents of the Totalisator have received for
some time. Not that it matters. The ODponents of
the honest, incorruptible democratic betting machine
always bob up serenely after being smote by a brick,
and cry “Never touched me.”

The Australian Jockey Club (Sydney)
medically examined itself last week, decided that it
had a tendency to put on too much fat, and pro-
ceeded to take steps to reduce its weight. So it
decided on some important changes of diet as re-
gards stakes and penalties. Although the total
stakes for the year are considerably over £30,000,
an additional £7500, plus £SOO more for the Sires’
Produce Stakes of 1915, is to be handed out. The
races benefiting most by the change will be: Spring
Meeting, Randwick Plate, £ISOO to £2OOO, and
Spring Stakes, £IOOO to £ISOO. December Meet-
ing: Villiers Stakes, £750 to £IOOO. Summer
Meeting: December Stakes, £IOOO to £1250; Sum-
mer Cup, £1250 to £ISOO. Anniversary Meeting:
Anniversary Handicap, £6OO to £IOOO. Autumn
Meeting: A.J.C. Plate, £ISOO to £2000; and
Autumn Stakes, £IOOO to £ISOO. The Doncaster,
Epsom, Sydney Cup and Metrop. are unchanged.
Previously the winners of races prior to the Epsom,
and Doncaster were penalised in the big events, now
that is to be done away with, and the only penalties;
in the Metrop. and Sydney Cup will be Doncaster
and Epsom wins. And on top of that the A.J.C.’s;
sister club, the V.R.C., has decided to make a charge
of Is. for admission to the flat. Racing clubs seemi
to be in the same street as gas companies.
* * * *
The motor cyclists had a torrid time in
the rush between Sydney and Melbourne last week..
J. Gunn’s record of 25hr. 55min. has been worry-
ing the buzzers for a couple of weeks, and the slaint
who have fallen in the attempt to lower his figures;
resembled Napoleon’s leavings on his retreat from;
Moscow. The Dunlop people dangled a special gold I
watch for the first to throw the distance behind!
under 24 hours, and the prize and honor together,'
were a dazzling lure to every rider of a motor-cycle;.
Eric Tyler left Sydney on Wednesday night at nine-
o’clock, and the first lump was blown off the record i
by his flight over the journey in 24 hours 39}mirri.
Dodging ruts on the N.S.W. roads between Gundagaii
and Germanton, Tyler had three falls, and did the-
cockies a good turn by killing some scores of rab-
bits. It was a splendid effort, and the extermina-
tion of the pest by motor-cycle deserves the consid-
eration of the Government. Just as Tyler was-;
wiping the perspiration off his victorious brow, how-
ever, J. Bolger began a toot-toot from Melbourne to>
Sydney, and fate was so kind to him that the record'
between the cities was hurled beyond the realm of','
Chaos and old Night. Bolger’s time was 23 hours-
41min., and on the way he smashed not only the-
record but a belt, a lamp and some other trifles. Ss>»
great was the enthusiasm over Bolger’s performance-
that at 2.16 on Saturday morning there were owr
200 people at the Sydney G.P.O. to give him at
cheer.
* * * *
The 100 miles ocean race of the Royal
Yacht Club at Hobart provided an exciting finlrik
The race was round Bruni Island and back to tfue
river, and was the best of a dozen similar races
held. The weather was good at the start, but fn
the open water a 40-miles thresh against a strong
head wind tested the craft to the utmost. The
winner, Pandora, did the distance in 22 hours SO
mm. 10 sec., Curlew came across the line 23 see.
later, v anity followed in 7 sec. and Pilgrim was
fourth, only 5 sec. away. It was an astounding!*
close finish after a race of the length, little more
than half a minute separating the first four boats,
and only 2 min. 34 sec. the first five. Actea and
Crescent returned some time later, but they were
within 10 sec. of one another.
* * * *
An innovation was made on Saturday in
the race for the Rawson Cup (Sydney), by allow-
ing the yachts their handicap at the start. It is a
good idea, and makes the exact position of tie
competitors more intelligible to the spectatoas.
Sayonara won the utensil by the narrow margin of
17sec. from White Wings. The win was a populhrr
one, for Sayonara has been having the hardest sort
ot luck lately, and usually carries away something:
before she gets home. On this occasion not Sing
bent or broke or burst, and the yacht sailed Herr
best, both on and off the -wind.

more seriously in choosing inter-State crews.

fr * * *
The visit of the British lawn tennis team
and the interest which surrounded last year’s inter
State matches has put new vim and blood into the
■gentle pastime of sweet maids and afternoon tea as
far at least as Sydney is concerned. The result is
that the club season pushed itself off last Saturday
with 81 teams in three grades—-10 in the first 60-
in the second, and 11 in the third. It is by far’the
biggest entry recorded in the history of Sydney
tennis, and for lack of playing spaces the sport is
almost bursting its waistcoat or camisole or some-
thing. The fact that the first round of matches
produced fairly even games threatens to still further
increase the interest in the gentle craft of the net
and racquet.
After much sorting out and testing, the
Commonwealth rifle team for Bisley (England) has
been completed. The dozen chosen from the thou-
sands of riflemen of Australia are—A. Cutler A
Blacklow, F. Harrison (N.S.W.), S. Edwards’ J
Williams, S. Long (Vic) ; A. Halliday, H. Arm.
strong (Q.), W. Green, H. White (S.' Aus.) • A
Harris (Westralia) ; R. Humphreys (Tas.). ’The
team is strong in marksmen; and providing the
wives and best girls do not put a foot through the
arrangements before May 3, Australia’s rifle-shooting
reputation before the world should be in safe hands*
There is a future Victor Trumper or
-somethfflg attending the Cleveland-street public
school 1 (Sydney) and playing for the Double Bav
Cricket Club. Allen Kippax is his name, and he
has only seen 15 summers, but in the City and
'Suburban competition he has been banging the ball
atfbout to some effect. Here are his scores up to the
jpresent:—18, 25, 68 (not out), 18, 0 (run out)
■SB <not out), 25 (not out), 54 (not out) and 105
!8!l (not out), 8 (not out), 54 (not out) and 105
''(not out) —a total of 583 for seven completed inn-
ings, giving an average of 83.2. In addition he has
knocked up a century or two in school cricket. It
is a remarkable season’s record for a lad of his
wears.

Matt. Wells got the referee’s decision on
against Hiighie Mehegan at the National
(Sporting Club, London. From the meagre details to
ffiand at time of goisg to press the decision seems to
lhave been bad, and Mehegan was moved to rise at
tfhe conclusion of the fight and declare from the ring-
side that he considered he had won. Considering
Wiat he was getting ai'l over his opponent in nearly
®11 the rounds, and that at least half of those present
were hooting the refence, it looks as if the boxer’s
iprotest had plenty to justify it. In the 15th round
«ells appears to have scored a dozen right-hand
Tpunches to the head, but Mehegan shortly after-
wards landed a terrific wallop on the nose, and
(evened things up. In the latter stages of the battle
Wells was merely on the defensive, and Mehegan all
*the time in pursuit.

How these Labor apostles love each other
in Maoriland. Not long ago the local
Worker had occasion to address a few re-
marks concerning the Waihi strike to one
Thomas Walsh, publisher of the Voice of
Labor, and this is how it went about it:—
We say that in the Australasian Labor party there
is not on record an incident so traitorous or so das-
tardly. This Walsh, in the sacred name of the
Labor party—fool and knave b6th—has been asso-
ciated with an official organ rotten to the core, and
time and again has wantonly and wickedly spoken
as a cred« dialled Labor representative in a way
that in a Russian movement would see him hanged,
and in a movement anywhere else than Maoriland
would see him drummed out as worse than Judas.
This kind of jarred Thomas, and he brought
a libel action, in which he demanded a solace
of £450. The jury, however, reckoned the
language was worth only £5O.
The bounding Kink O’Malley, last week,
made a bid for the late Bent’s vituperative
waistcoat by diagnosing the Liberal party
as “a cross between a city guinea-pig and a
country blowfly.” Kink himself baffles classi-
fication, but he is most often off
spring of a hen’s false alarm and a scare-
head.
In the gentle art of cadge, the Oldest In-
habitant of Sydney Domain could sit at the
feet of the bosses of the Chapzander mis-
sionary troupe. Among other things, these
folk have books to sell; and “having come
practically to the close” of their Australian
drumming tour, they find some stock still on
hand. So they have just issued an appeal
in Melbourne for £2OO to cover the cost of
unloading the unsold stock on to the pris-
oners and hospital patients of Australia.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will he
taken of communications insufficiently stamped. Name
and address must appear on MSS. No liability is
undertaken re contributions voluntarily submitted,
whether sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will
be returned unless stamps accompany.
Bosco Blithers: “Myola,” so far as The
Bulletin knows, isn’t aboriginal, and has no mean-
ing whatever. It is one of the hundreds of bogus
names with which humorists take in the unwary. If
you analyse it and think over it carefully- it may
strike you as foolish English slightly disguised. A
similar case was “Biladamz,” a beautiful poetic term
meaning “Beside blue waters.” The man who put
this name on his house after,wards explained that it
was simply “Bill' Adams,” and he invented it be-
cause the banal person next door called his place
“Waterloo.”. .. .Fred 0. M.: Every winter there are
frozen whalers and dod-fishers and such-like folk, who
pass out as a matter of ordinary business, leaving no
thrilling “messages” behind them, and without the
slightest hope that anybody will see their relatives
“properly provided for.” Try to get the perspective
of the affair... .Curlew: The tough wood argument
has been battened down' with i.2in. spikes. Can only
suggest on the evidence' forwarded that your hard
tree would be a commercial success if it were used
to make false teeth. Ordinary ivory, besides being
expensive, wears out too soon, and granite chips....
Boswell: Why waste paper and ink on all that trash?
The one prominent democratic barrister was shut out
on the strength of a tale about his bad health. His
health is as good as yours—perhaps better. Then the
choice lay mainly between conservative barristers
with a reputation for knowing the law
thoroughly, and conservative barristers with no
such reputation. And the jobs went to
the barristers with the small reputation. .. .
Comduek: Gohannas and the like are non-venomous i;
hut as they don’t clean their teeth their bite isn t
healthy J. Smith: Does a child think before the
age of five. Certainly. It doesn’t think the gigantic
thoughts of the philosopher or scientist of 45, but it
thinks. For instance, it thinks medicine is beastly
at an earlier age than five.... Wet Bulb: Profound
meteorological dissertation to hand. .. .H.W.C.: This
is a poor market for bushranger yarns. .. .A Militant
Suffragette: Dunno about London houses, but in the
poor quarters of Glasgow and Edinburgh the gas-
fittings go with the house. Curiously enough the
kitchen grate doesn’t —or didn’t —and the new tenant
arrives dragging that piece of ironmongery along
with him James C.: Something of the kind .was
needed to commemorate the splendid spectacle. We
have taken the liberty of christening it for you:—
LINES ON SEEING WATT WEEP.
We jumped on bord a melbourne Tram .
Which brought us to the water near
Where William Wat had sought to jam
On bord the deck of the Morea
Just then the engine whisteled well
A signal for his friends apart
From those who did in melbourne tell
That he must now for London start
Their stood the premier galy drest
For he was bent on buisness more
Than all the men who now confest
That every premier sailed before
■ And then we were with tears, bereft
For we had felt the pain also
As we at home to work was left
That he might now to london go
And when that ship to oceion wave
Had turned to diner Billoes plough
Than all the tears we had to save
Which williams eyes was shedding now
He said in tears that downward rained
Upon the yard rail of the poop
If george ou'r king his friendship gained
He would to knight hood proudly stoop
Then in the murk and through the haze
When saddness silenced every lunge
Till one had said we sing the praise
Our Willey’s boat to England swung.
(Note. —At this stage, the lino, man’s feelings over-
came him. May print the rest when Watt returns.)..
Thomas C.: The bagpipes have been carefully mur-
dered with one of Hop’s barbed spears. Their dying
shriek was horrible Concertina: Poem about 5 the
plains contains so many queries that it reads'like an
examination paper. You might have saved labor by
tabulating it this way:—
Q. 1. ’Ave yer seen th’plains in springtime, when all the
earth is green ?
Q. 2. ~ ~ „ ~ ~ summer, when the land
is burnt an’ dry?
Q. 3. „ ~ ~ ~ ~ floodtime when the
, rivers all are up ? (
Q. 4. ~ ~ „ ~ at night time when the
stars are over’ead ?
Then there are the other queries about the., wind,
dying sheep, suicide longings, moonrise, homestead
lights and other matters. Really a poet should not
set out to wring information out of his reader. A
faint hostility is the only emotion aroused... Trever:
Oh, d — n! Mary B.: Four lines only of —
THE SACRIFICE.
A little while and I would watch alone;
A little while, or longer, it may be;
Betwixt a-sleeping and a-waking gone—
And we shall meet —so, comrades, let it be.

Spare your Spares!
too late to spare, when all is spent.” —PROVERB.
Ħ
H
Urdess you protect vour spares from sun, rain, friction, and sudden
changes in temperature—so frequent in this country —they will be rendered
worthless, even before the occasion for using them arises.
A cover should never be left uncovered—in wet weather or in dry. It
stands to reason that a tyre which is constantly exposed to rain or even damp
must soon begin to deteriorate. Strong sunlight is equally injurious, causing
the best of rubber to harden, grow brittle, and lose its resiliency.
Use the
Michelin Waterproof Wrapper,
which effectually protects tire cover, and can be quickly removed at will.

I am not an advertising quack, but am a business
man. My hair began to grow thin about the time
1 reached the age of thirty. When I had reached the
age of thirty-five the top of my head was quite bald.
I had considerable pride, and sought for something
to cause renewed growth. I invested in every pre-
paration that I could see or hear of, and here let me
say that not only did I try all the English so-called
restorers, but those of Continental Europe. I also
visited specialists. My experimenting cost me many
sovereigns and considerable time —yet the hairs came
not. I had given up the idea of ever growing hair
again. One afternoon, while enjoying a brief bu>i-
ness respite by an Alpine trip, I fell in with an old
collegiate chemist, whose hair was particularly plen-
tiful. I jokingly remarked that I wished he could
spare some of his hair for my benefit. This lead him
to tell me that he understood the human hair as well
as a good dentist understands teeth. He wrote out a
formula, and suggested that I have a quantitv of the
compound prepared. I did this a few days later, i
was astonished to see the hair coming forth after
several applications, and within six weeks my head
was covered by a firm, yet soft, black growth of hair.
Since then the hair on my head has been as luxuriant
as when in my school days. Having arranged for
the legal right to do so, 1 have for some time been
privately selling this marvellous hair-growing com-
pound. I hold over one thousand letters from people
who have actually grown new hair through the
medium of my true hair grower. I do not pretend
to say it is the only hair grower in the world —I am
not interested in decrying others —but I do assert
that it is the most reliable, and in the end by far the
cheapest.

Business, Robbery, etc.
Tom Hood’s “Song of the Shirt’’ needs only to be
brought up to date by adapting it to sewing machine
conditions. Human life is cheaper and food re dearer
than when Hood’s verses raised a brief outcry against
the sweating of the sempstress. Nowadays, the machin-
ist in London gets twopence halfpenny for making the
gent.’s shirt that costs Bs. 6d. or 10s. 6d. in a Shop.
She has to find her own cotton and carry the goods fro
and to the factory that gives out this wicked work.
Another lovely industry is envelope-folding. Girls
make up envelopes for three-ha’pence a thousand and
find their own paste.
“C.E.” : The motor-bus appears in Melbourne, and
is bound to push its way as it has done in Lon-
don. I often cannot reach a tram because of the
thick wheel traffic. The motor-bus comes right up
to the kerb, and in London is allowed to keep travel-
ling near it for the convenience of the nervous and
weak. Also, it stops anywhere. These are advantages
that will grow with the growth of traffic. The lack
of traffic is, of course, the big reason why, though the
new vehicles are wanted here even now, they will not
be at once the huge financial success they are in Lon-
don. But wait. Time is on their side.
* * * *
An Orange (N.S.W.) auctioneer has just found
out that the Law looks with a cold eye on one of
the tricks of his trade. As soon as word goes
round that a man has made £27,400 or some other
tidy sum through canny land-dealings, auctioneers
and land-subdivisionists enrol him as a bell wether.
If he buys, or can be paraded as a buyer, the fool-
public will flock in. Lately, at a local land sale,
two reputedly successful speculators were on hand,
and when the bidding hung fire, the auctioneer
knocked the first blocks down to them for £l9
That was enough. The flock followed the bell-
wethers, and the bidding became brisk. Then some
absurd person who had bought a block nosed around
and found out that the two lucky speculators hadn’t
really bought the blocks that had been knocked
down to them to give things a start, and he howled
for damages in respect ef alleged fraudulent mis-
representation. And the Law,, sitting inside a P.M.,
said there had been misrepresentation, and gave "the
man some of the damages he asked for.

Patent Attorney’’: Last Commonwealth Gazette
advertises for an assistant examiner in the Patents
Office, who must possess the following qualifications:
(1) Ability in criticism of mechanical descriptions
and preparation of precis; (2) general knowledge
of mechanical science, electricity and metallurgy;
(d) practical experience in the use of tools in one
or j m S e mam| t' a ctures; (4) experience in designing,
and the preparation of drawing’s of machinery.”
The vacancy is in the professional branch, and the
salary offered is £72 per annum. How is this for
sweating? England, even, pays twice as much for
similar work.

„ ‘.‘£h tmar !” : . The car &° pilfering described in B.
6/2/ 13 makes it fairly evident that a big firm is at
work a firm that has some capital at its back. Mv
theory is that packing cases to receive the stolen
goods are consigned from the port of departure, os-
tensiWy holding merchandise, and that in these cases
the stolen goods are brought away and signed for in
the usual manner of business. One way of checking
wouM be to mark each package with its gross weight
when leaving the warehouse; then, if the weights
on arrival did not agree, the suspects should be
opened and examined. If goods were found in any
other package than the one in which they were
originally packed, the consignee might be requested
to give a full explanation. He might of course be
able to do so—and again he might not. This, I
think, should lead to the discovery of a new and
vigorous growth of Private Enterprise.
“Mimosa”: Thomas Behan, the starter of the
ostrich-breeding industry in Queensland, thinks of
taking a trip shortly through England and America
via Egypt, and intends to give the ostrich some
study. Writer, who put in a few years with the
birds in South Africa, advises him gratis to get hold
of a few specimens of the genuine article; and, as
export from South Africa is banned, this will mean
trekking 500 miles S.W. of Tripoli to the southern
borders of Barbary. That is where the original tame
Cape, birds came from. In 1865 there were 82 such
birds in Cape Colony; in 10 years they had in-
creased to 32,247 ; by the end of 1904 the number
was 357,408; seven years later (1911) it stood at
746,736. The figures for 20 years will show what
money there is in the biz. In 1891 the declared
value of the exported feathers was £468,221, or an
average of £3 os. 6d. per bird. In 1904 the returns
showed £1,058,998, or £2 19s. 3d. per bird. The
1911 figures jumped to £2,253,044, equal to
£3 os. 4d. per bird. To get some of that ric - '
crop for Australia we must go to Barbary and sta v t
a foundation stud with double-flue birds. At
present, to put it plainly, we have very little except
rubbish in Australian pens. ,ne writer t inks
knows a good-quality feather and bird, and he has to
admit with regret that he has not yet seen an Aus-
tralian-growth sample of either.
* * * *
When the tactful Johnson can spare a few minutes
from his job of giving lessons in manners and gram-
mar to his bosses, he might inquire into the system
under which the railway refreshment-rooms are run.
It is a rotten example of Private Enterprise methods.
The rooms, instead of being run by the Department,
are let to lessees —usually by tender. The highest
tenderer invariably gets tiie place for three years. If
he makes a success of it and popularises the “stand,”
he has to bid higher next time, for there is then al-
most bound to be competition. The result is that, pretty
often, as a lessee's term draws to a close, he let’s
things run themselves, so that for a while trade may
be driven away and possible competitors scared off.
Or, if he has decided to go out of the business, he
takes the last possible farthing out of the travelling
public and gives the smallest possible service in re-
turn. The wretched system, which is grossly unfair
to the lessees themselves, is also grossly unfair to
travellers, who naturally can’t find more than two or
three well-run railway refreshment-rooms in the
whole of N. S. Wales.
A JOKE ON US.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Some amusing exchanges between witnesses and
members of the Federal Fruit Commission took
place yesterday at Hobart — Argus.
When fruit is dear, good Stout can part
The price, and still have apple-tart;
But when we toiling blighters pay
Excessive prices for our jam
He laughs, in an insulting way,
As though he did not care a *
When, too, the common people spread
Not jam, but treacle, on their bread,
Good Stout is thoroughly amused,
And rocks with hearty merriment,
Seeing this simple fodder used
The jammeries to circumvent.
Is pumpkin-raspberry compo. sold
At full all-raspberry rates of old ?
Well, fruit is very dear, just now,
And pumpkins readily obtained.
Pumpkin ! It’s Stout who makes that row,
Howling with laughter unrestrained.
Do we expend a bob or more
At some dashed Dago’s smellful store?
Stout’s pleasant spirits fail to flag
When he observes, with cackles, what
A small, absurd, ill-furnished bag
Falls to the Common Person’s lot.
Daily the public, wrathy, dumb,
Adventuring, dismayed and glum.
Among fruit specked and second-best,
Gives witty Stout new tales to te11....
But, when good Stout has had his jest,
We fruitless ones may go to * * *
VICTORIA.
