IN
BANANA LAND
The Queensland Labor party wants to
abolish assisted immigration on the ground
that it is flooding the country with cheap
labor; and harrowing tales have been told
of flocks or shoals of disappointed new-
chums pouring over the country eager to
snap up any job that offers. Now comes the
cold, hard yarn of the Commonwealth Statis-
tician to the effect that Queensland’s immi-
grants last year numbered 6462 all told,
assisted and unassisted —a mighty flood of
one to every 130 square miles. If the State
Labor party were a branch of the Jap pub-
lic service it could hardly do the tricky little
brown person’s work better.
There is more difference between tweedle-
dum and tweedledee than is generally al-
a e ™c F ? r instance > the appeal of one P.
A. D Sullivan against a conviction placed
upon him in Brisbane for running a lottery
was heard in Hobart last week bv the High
Court. The scheme evolved in Queensland
by appellant took the form of a building
society at one shilling a share. At intervals
there was to be a sale, the winning share-
holder receiving a cash bonus. It was a
thinly-designed sweep; in fact, the promo-
ters advertised that it was an improvement
on Tattersall’s. The High Court upheld the
conviction and adjourned in time to enable
it D f it desired) to buy a hatful of tickets
in Tatt. s sweeps on its way home. If one
of the Judges hadn’t time to call in himself
he could have sent a policeman for the
tickets with perfect propriety.
“Don Q.” : There are some reliable sweat-
ers in Queensland, and even the Govern-
ment is getting a reputation in this respect.
A week ago several painters employed by
the Railway Department shed their tools
because the Commissioner refused to pay the
ra C of wages for painting provided under
a Wages Board award. They werfe merely
casual hands, and as everybody knows
casual workers are not as hungry as men
in constant employment, and their children
can be kept in charitable institutions at a
trifling expense. A few days later an em-
ployee in the Health Department suicided.
But before doing so he wrote a letter in
which he explained that as pay cashier he
was receiving from the Government the lofty
sum of 30s. ner week, and that a warrant
was out for him on a charge of embezzle-
ment. If a Labor party had been in power
instead of a Government representing
Money-bags what a dust would have ob-
scured the landscape! As it is, it doesn’t
matter.
The. Barnes-Denham crowd is continually
advertising the fact that it has abufidance
of loan money for all purposes. If this be so
why has the work on the Tobermorey exten-
sion ceased? Why has the Blackall-Win-
dorah railway come to a dead finish? What
is the matter with Mount Cuthbert and one
or two other lines already started, where not
a pick is now to be heard? Perhaps Trea-
surer Barnes’s pockets are so plugged up
with loan dollars that he can’t get them out
even as the over-educated youth failed in his
exam, because his superfluous information
got jammed in the doorway of his mind.
Queensland Full Court mildly expressed
the opinion that a former Full Court of
N. S. Wales was talking through its wig.
The question was whether a pilot, employed
by the State, was liable if he piled up a
ship which the State said had to be piloted
—liable, that is, to the owners of the ship,
who had no voice in choosing him. Ma’s
Full Court said in the Bannockburn case
that “the Crown does not retain the
power of controlling the pilot’s move-
ments in the actual discharge of the
duties of navigating a ship.” Queens-
land’s _ Judges said that, by the same
reasoning, no employer would ever be re-
sponsible for anything; and it told the
F. and A. Co. that it might go ahead and
assume that the Government was liable for
the grounding of the Eastern last year. The
co. wants £50,000.
How the tropic
North thins the
blood of the Euro-
pean ! The other
day two Charters
Towers cabmen
had a barney, and
as a result, one
shot the other —
fatally. Both of
these hot - heads
were over 70 years
of age, and but for
the unfortunate
proximity of a re-
volver would pro-
bably have gone
on driving cabs in-
to their 90th year.
At 90 the Queens-
land climate be-
comes almost un-
bearable to nearly
everybody.
Old-man Barlow, Queensland’s journey-
man Minister and acting Premier, cursed
Federation the other day, and declared that
“the results for Queensland had been disas-
trous.” The same day or thereabouts one
of Barlow’s old banking pals was talking
officially to the shareholders of the Q. N.
Bank in Brisbane. The disastrous concern
smashed with a dreadful smell in 1893. It
owed about £1,050,000 to the public, for
which it had no assets whatever. All it
could promise was to try to make up the
shortage out of future profits. Down to
1903 —10 years after the smelly smash—it
had paid Is. in the £ of this debt. That is
to say, in seven years before Federation
arrived and in the three years when Fed-
eration was getting into its stride, the Bank
that had smashed in the days before Fed-
eration got rid of only one-twentieth of the
dead horse. In the next three years an-
other twentieth of the obscene animal was
removed. Then as Federation went on pro-
ducing further disaster to Queensland, it
took only two years to shift the next twen-
tieth. . In the following three years one-
twentieth was shifted every nine months.
Now the carcase is disappearing at the rate
of a twentieth every six months ; and while
Barlow was talking vaguely of the dreadful
results of Federation, Barlow’s banking pal
was telling shareholders that he expected
things to be so good during the next seven
years that by that time the bad smell left
by the pre-Federation smash should be com-
pletely removed from the State’s premises.
Probably Barlow, being a mournful old
crow, reckons the removal of pre-Federation
carcases a calamity. If so, he should make
himself clearer.
Farewell, Yesterday!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I would not have Yesterday back, not I!
I’ve pretty near done with To-day !
Some folk for the sweets of the past may
sigh,
joys in the future I pray!
For Yesterday’s stale as an old wives’ tale,
And its sorrows and cares are forgot.
Bring it sunshine or sorrow, I look for To-
morrow ;
It’s as like to be sunshine as not.
Oh, rot!
It’s as like to be sunshine as not!
I would not have Yesterday back, not much !
Let clocks and the calendar waste;
With the hour that’s gone I have quite lost
touch ;
On our journey we must make haste.
Oh ! the wake gleams white in the morning
light,
And the coastline is fading away.
But no talk of returning! The Past lam
spurning!
Let us travel ahead while we may!
Hooray!
Let us travel ahead while we may.
I would not have Yesterday back! ’Tis
done —
Its sorrows, its joys and its tears;
They sank out of sight with the setting sun
And merged with the welter of years.
I bid you forget if you would not fret —
Ev’ry retrospect costs you a sigh.
Oh ! the brave, forward fighting our strength
is delighting,
But astern is a lowering sky.
Good-bye !
For its unrelieved grey is a lie!
I would not have Yesterday back! Who will
May pray lie will sight it ahead ;
But the log of my life’s too short to fill
With days that are sunken and dead.
I would journey on till my strength is gone,
Living, every day in its turn ;
Though it made us or broke us, the Past’s
out of focus.
But ahead we have something to learn.
To learn!
Some new joy or some sorrow to learn !
E. O’F.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, February 22, 1913.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
We’re having our share of the crews of
the two Japanese instructional warships
which are nosing along the Australian coast.
On Tuesday Governor Macgregor will per-
sonally conduct a mixed party of white and
brown down the river in the old hulk Lucin-
da. This will give the visitors an oppor-
tunity to observe the alleged defence works
at Lytton.
By the way, his Ex. will havb to give up
gay bachelordom shortly. Mrs. Ex., daughter
and the Paget grandaughter are due at Fre-
mantle on Monday (24th inst.) aboard the
Frederich der Grosse. Thence to Brisbane.
The city’s new Gaud Mayor, Alderman
Henry Doggett, is a small, shrewd person
with a cropped grey beard and a humorous
eye. Likewise he wears his feet at the
usual end of his body, and when he walks
only one of them is twirled in the atmos-
phere at a time. He rarely leaps, especi-
ally at meals. His wife is a retiring little
woman, who is somewhat fearful of her new
gauddess-ship, but intends to hold it on to its
halter with determination and both hands.
The Gaud’s first flutter will be a reception
to Britain’s Asiatic allies, who are parading
round with a couple of war vessels.
IN THE COW COUNTRY.
INSPECTOR (to pupil who has just finished
reading about an orphan child): "What
is an orphan?”
PUPIL: “ Please sir, I don’t know."
INSPECTOR: “Well, what would you be
called if you had the misfortune to lose
your father and mother?”
P UPIL (triumphantly): “Oh! then I’d
be a poddy.”
Died on Sunday morning, Lewis Thomas,
who, during the last 10 years, has sat in
that anarchistic body, the Legislative Coun-
cil of this State. He came to Queensland
nearly 60 years ago. The day before he
sailed from Wales (the old one) he married
the gentle little Welshwoman who has here-
abouts done so much, in such a quiet way,
for charity. For ten years he toiled in
Queensland before he could send for
his wife, who, meanwhile, was working in
the mills. Thomas began his workaday
career at the age of eight, when he took
his place in the coal pits in Cardigan.
He was 27 when he came to Australia, and
went first to Victoria. In 1866 he cut the
first ton of coal in the old Aberdare colliery
in Bqndamba, and from that day his wealth
started to grow and flourish like the More-
ton Bay tree. One child only (a daughter
who is now married) was born to him.
Another military man (from Victoria this
time) has annexed the sole remaining
daughter of Robert Collins. About a fort-
night ago sister Bertha accepted the visible
badge of matrimony from one Harris, who
is something in the A.F.A., but whether he
is the brigadier-sergeant or the adjutant-
bos’n, I know not. History is silent and
Who’s Who is mum. Anyhow, he is sta-
tioned at Sydney. Now Dorothea has capi-
tulated to the Cupid battery brought into
action by Captain Lloyd of the same arm of
the service, so that the man behind the field-
gun seems to be a weakness with the Col-
lins girls. Captain Lloyd polishes his war
ironmongery at Melbourne. His bride-to-be
keeps up the family’s horse reputation and
also whirls a capable tennis club. ’
The committee of the Lady Lamington
Hospital has proved its right to be con-
sidered enterprising. A “Kismet” ball will
be held in March, to help build a new wing
and already burly Oscar Asche and his Lily
have promised to decorate the affair with
their gorgeous selves in full Bagdad dress.
Architect Brady, who leaves for England
next week with his wife and daughter, was
given a send-off on the Otter this afternoon
by the Institute of Polite Engineers. The
usual toast list, besprinkled with speeches
was the circumstance of the afternoon. N 0!
body had a word to say against Brady, and
if there were any complaints against the
whisky they were not made public.
Another boat picnic was that of the Bris-
bane Merchants’ Association, which munched
its lunch on board the Lucinda. That levia-
than had been partially cleansed for the
occasion. The. liquid refreshment aboard
would have cheered the heart of a peram-
bulating cold water press agent who was
short of “copy.”
Frances Willard, of W.C.T.U. fame, was
“commemorated” on Monday by the sister
hood she helped to found. On Thursday, at
the monthly meeting of the dry-bone society,
the opportunity was grabbed to mention
Frances some more. But it was an unsatis-
fying celebration, offering nothing more sub-
stantial than a pint of froth with a dash of
blue ribbon.
The daily newspapers are full of the hys-
terical capers of the British suffragettes just
now and how not to deal with them. All
that is required, Thargomindah, my dear, is
a large, calm washer-lady with a hand like
a corrugated pancake. Let her be ap-
pointed Public Spanker, and then pass along
the insurrectionists one at a time.
That clever comedy company. The Moths,
has left Brisbane for the smalls. Leathley,
who runs the show, has no reason to love
Brisbane. Last year he took up his stand
with the “Musical Oorricks” one day before
the strike fiasco started. Needless to say.
he had to shut down. Then he organised
“The Moths,” who gave a bright and pleas-
ant little programme. For the first month
the place was well filled with people; after
that it was merely well filled with what the
people used to sit on ; and each performance
meant a bigger hole in Leathley’s savings.
So he has decided to shake Brisbane and
lead his co. to a better grazing ground.
“The Monk and the Woman” has been
played to packed houses during the week.
It will move on next Tuesday.
Yours in Lent,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
“Ivuscobin” : Weinholt, so his friends say,
knows that he has no hope of winning Wide
Bay against Andrew Fisher; but he is
anxious to enter National politics, and he
knows that the sacrifice of his Fassifern
seat in the State House will be a fine advt.
If the halo doesn’t afterwards win him a
nomination as one of the candidates
|he will probably be a very disappointed
young man, as well as a very rich one.
“Boswell,” still in defence:—
“Northern Democrat” (B. 20/2/’l3), disputing the
Big Australianism of the Bananaland Labor party,
dwells on one topic only, the question of immigra-
tion. But what of the great Referenda questions?
Has “Northern Democrat” no word to say for the
Labor party’s attitude towards them ? Has he no
note of admiration in either eye for the way State
Labor members are already skirmishing with the
heavy infantry of the Meat Trust and other similar
demons? The probable victory of the Referenda in
Bananaland will be largely achieved by the activity
of State Laborites, from whose councils the State
Fright midrobe is as conspicuously absent as it is
conspicuously present in the McGowen-Holman crowd
of N. S. Wales.
Says Treasurer Barnes :
The policy now pursued of spending certain sums
of money each year is much better than the policv
of plunging and then coming to a stoppage. I
would not be a partner to such a policy.
Barnes expended about three millions of loan
money last year on a population of slightly
over '600,000 —say, £22 10s. of borrowed
money per household. But this is not plung-
ing ; it’s only “keeping a tight hand on
things.” Some people think the hand is only
tight in the sense that the owner of it is on
the financial drunk.
Doggett, Brisbane’s new Mayor, is a man
of few words, and a contractor. He threa-
tens to be a negative success. He is the type
of Mayor who is sentenced to 12 months’
limelight and boredom for his failure to make
enemies. Of the 14 who took a hand in his
election, seven were ex-Mayors, namely,
McMaster, Buchanan, Crase, Iletherington,
Raymond, Wilson and C.M.G. Diddams.
Hitherto the contest has been a tug-of-war,
Citv versus Others, seven aside. This year
ex-Mayor Buchanan, a city representative,
threw in his lot with the others; so the old
Town Hall site struggle, the godsend of the
local papers, will probably be renewed with
vigor.

The Starrett Level
and Transit 99 B,
For Farmers, Builders,
Contractors ——= 80s.
Farmers, as a rule, have most of the latest tools and implements for
doing farm work, but rarely will you find a levelling instrument
included in the kit-simply because they do not care to pay the
price usually charged for a surveyors’ or engineers level and transit.
The result is that jobs needing a level have been done by guesswork,
and therefore unsatisfactorily.
H
The Starrett 99 B gets you out of
the difficulty. The price complete with
long legs and Telescope is but 80/-
Starrett 99 B guarantees ac
curacy on all kinds of drainage, under-
drainage, irrigation work, terracing
land, road making, setting out orchards,
grading of all kinds, fencing, getting
angles, and every farm job requiring
such an instrument. Your neighbours
would gladly hire it from you at times.
The Starrett 99 B is also for architects, carpenters, and contrac-
tors to lay out building sites, and to determine the levels : for masons
and millwrights in the construction of foundation and the setting of
water wheels, building of dams and raceways. _
The surveyor will find this superior to the ordinary needle compass,
for any angles may be taken where, from local attraction, it would be
impossible to set them off with the needle. .
There is nothing like The Starrett 99 B for the money. It is
made of finest materials —the body is of iron and brass, tripod of
iron, the sight tube, level case, and graduated arcs, nickel-plated ;
all other parts are japanned. Packed weight about 151bs.
Full instructions for use sent with each instrument.

graduated level glass —£7 10 /-

London Daily Telegraph, concerning
the battleship Maoriland, “presented” to
Britain :
_ She is charged with the aspirations of the Em-
pire, which is searching for a means of translating
the proverb, “Union is strength,” into terms which
the world will understand.
Well, the strength of the terms is to be found
in the first word of the proverb—lOU.
A British committee is to investigate the
causes of failing eyesight among factory
workers and others engaged on indoor jobs.
Legislation has been passed in most coun-
tries providing that the worker shall have
enough air to breathe, but little interest has
been taken in the matter of light, provided
there seemed to be enough of it to go round.
Yet it is possible to have enough light and
still have the factory or workroom so ar-
ranged as to put the eyes under a perpetual
strain. Some schools in Australia are about
as bad in this respect as anything need be ;
and as Australia wants its young eyes to
look along a rifle, the matter ought to be
seen to.

Government Notices.
COMMONWEALTH OF AUSTRALIA.
Department of the Treasury,
Melbourne, 28th December, 1913.
COUNTERFEIT COINAGE PLANT.
£lOO Reward.
WHEREAS certain imitations of silver
coins have recently been passed into
circulation at Sydney, in the State of New
South Wales : Notice is hereby Aven that a
reward of One hundred pounds will be paid
by the Government of the Commonwealth
for information leading to the apprehension
of the coiners and the discovery of the plant
used in the manufacture of the coins referred
to. The Commonwealth Government will
request the State Government to extend a
full pardon to any accomplice who will give
the desired information. This offer will
remain open for thre months from this
date.
ANDREW FISHER,
Treasurer.
KALQOORLIE TO PORT
AUGUSTA RAILWAY.
SUPPLY AND DELIVERY OF JARRAH
SLEEPERS.
rTUQNDERS will be received until 11
JL a.m. on TUESDAY, the 11th March,
1913, for the supply and delivery of
Three Hundred Thousand (300,000) Sawn
or Hewn Jarrah Sleepers—Dimensions 9ft.
x lOin. x sin., to be of full size, rectangular
in section, cut from large trees and free
from all faults. Price to include delivery
in ships’ slings on railway trucks at Port
Augusta Wharf, but not to include Wharf-
age Charges at Port Augusta.
Alternative quotations may be made for
delivery on trucks alongside ship at Bun-
bury (W.A.).
Tenderers to state date of first delivery,
also rate of subsequent deliveries, and may
tender for the whole or any portion of the
quantity required. No tender necessarily
accepted.
Forms of Tender and Conditions of Con-
tract can be obtained at the office of the
Engineer-in-Chief, Commonwealth Railways
Construction Branch, 84 William Street,
and at the offices of the Commonwealth Pub-
lic Service Inspectors at Adelaide and Perth
respectively.
Tenders should be endorsed, “Tender for
Jarrah Sleepers for Kalgoorlie-Port Augusta
Railway”; to be addressed to “The Secre-
tary, Department of Home Affairs, Mel-
bourne.”
KING O’MALLEY,
Minister of State for Home Affairs.

ARE YOU
DEAF?
If you are deaf, or going deaf, you will be glad to
hear of a simple and scientific device which restores
hearing, no matter how old you are. No mystery or
secrecy about it; we describe it fully, and explain
just how it acts. You use spectacles as sight magni-
fiers, and in the same way you use this sound magni-
fier to hear better. Unlike spectacles, the Wilson
Common-sense Ear-Drums are invisible and comfort-
able. No one can tell you are wearing them. They
rest the ear nerves by taking the strain off them —the
strain of trying to hear indistinct sounds. What a
relief to have done with that tiring strain. Being
made of the softest Para rubber they cannot be felt,
even when the head rests on a pillow. For the same
reason, a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. They protect the tender inner parts of the
ear, that are so sensitive, from cold winds, dust, sud-
den and piercing sounds.
So that you may clearly understand how the Sound
Magnifiers restore hearing, we give an illustration of
one as it lies in the ear. You will notice that the
Sound Magnifier is shaped like a trumpet, and so con-
veys even the faintest sounds right to the natural drum.
Being of the softest rubber, it cannot hurt the ear.
It fits the orifice of the ear
with precision and comfort.
The division that runs
across the mouth is to al-
low the removal and inser-
tion of the Ear-Drum, and
to brace it.
The shape of the Wilson
Ear-Drum has been chosen
after years of experience as
the best adapt-
ed to carry the
faintest sound.
Of course, if
you have lost
the h e a r i ng-
nerve co m-
pletely, or were
born deaf, we
cannot pretend
to cure you. Only a miracle could
do that, in spite of some of the
absurd claims made to-day. But
of you can hear the faintest sound,
do not despair. You will never
regret trying the Wilson Ear-
Drums. We have letters to show
you from deaf people in Australia
and New Zealand, who have been
cured, and you can be cured, too. You may wear
the Ear-Drums day and night, and take them out
whenever you wish. Children can wear them (we
have special sizes for children from six to twelve).
Even a child can insert and remove them without the
least danger.
The price is £1 (the fee for a doctor’s single
visit). This includes a pair of drums (they are only
sold in pairs), a fine nickel remover, and also an
inserter. After the first pair is bought you may pur-
chase a single drum at any time for Bs., but the
pair you get at first will last quite two years. Do
not ask for a trial pair. We do not send them out
on trial, and so they are never secondhand. Use the
Order form below, and the Outfit will be sent you
at once, with the fullest instructions. Don’t delay,
but have the comfort of good hearing as soon as you
can.
OHDEKPOHM.

The Talented § Charming Young Artiste,
Miss Dolly Nepean, Praises Rexona Soap
Miss. Dolly Nepean, late of the Melbourne Grand Opera Co., is one of
Australia’s most charming and clever young artistes. By many critics she is

Old John the Hermit.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
fINHERE was a pious patriarch,
I When I was quite a lad,
Who lived the cave life in a park
In rags and whiskers clad.
He stalked abroad on sunny days
And worried youthful sports
By warning them to mend their ways
And snorting holy snorts.
His staff with bright barbed wire was tied,
So he was greatly feared ;
And none his warnings dared deride,
Though all in secret jeered.
“Old John the Hermit,” he was called ;
About the life he led
He wrote long articles as bald
As his tall, egg-shaped head.
“Myself—My Soul” —on such a theme
He would enlarge for weeks
And revel in “The Spirit’s Dream—
Soul Essence—How It Leaks.”
He did a wondrous trade in Soul
With “Those Who Are in Doubt” ;
Fools waited humbly at his hole
Until he should come out.
When after hours and hours he came
Out of his draughty den.
He only cried : “Wealth —Glory—Fame !”
And then went in again.
Whene’er one ventured to intrude
To ask him what he meant,
The Hermit was extremely rude,
But still magnificent.
“Hence. Doubter, hence ! The Grave !
The Grave!”
Or “Who mocks Death? Beware!’’
The fool rushed headlong from the cave
Into the open air.
But ere he fled this counsel strange,
Into the begging bowl
He dropped a handful of small change
For his supply of Soul.
John’s vogue grew greater as he aged,
And ruder grew and stout;
Fool controversies round him raged ;
No fool could make him out.
They started then a magazine,
And once a month it taught
Of how “The Master” had been seen
Full of tremendous thought.
Sometimes “The Master’s” whiskers waved
Upon the title page ;
’Twas always splendidly engraved
And titled thus: The Sage.
His contributions —all in “caps”—
Were starred upon Page Two,
Under the heading “Thunderclaps !
My Soul —-A Distant View.”
Each convert panted as he read
About The Master’s Soul.
(It sent one grocer off his head
And drove him up the pole.)
But other fools with larger brains
Remained on earth intent,
And regularly missed last trains
Explaining what He meant.
But all agreed the whiskered one
Had solved all mysteries,
Possessed of brain about a ton
And held Life’s secret keys.
One morning as the Sage reclined
Upon his granite seat,
A female who “just worshipped Mind”
Crept humbly to his feet.
She told him she was after Truth —
A Helpless W T retch in Doubt;
Her hair was down, she’d lost her youth,
Her Soul refused to sprout.
So thickly did she lay it on,
The Hermit said at last
He’d ponder massively upon
The problem—yes, and fast.
With that he rose and sought his cave
To analyse his Soul,
Making an inclination grave
Towards his begging bowl.
The Soul in Doubt dropped in a cheque
And cried “O Happy Day!”
Old John stretched forth his arm and neck
And snaked the bowl away.
An interval of hours went by
Before the Sage came out;
He looked the female in the eye
And roared : “No longer doubt.
“Get you a robe and follow me
To take the faithful’s dole !
My first disciple shalt thou be.
And bear the begging bowl!’
Next day the Hermit took the air,
The female walked behind ;
His whiskers and her unbound hair
Waved nobly in the wind.
The sight electrified the park,
And one unlettered churl
Cried suddenly : “Hi! ’ere’s a lark .
The ’Ermit’s gotter girl!’
But still the pair did not turn back,
Though both were seen to start.
Till someone in the front said, JacK,
Where didger get th’ tart ?
Old John the Hermit raised his staff,
His brow as thunder black ;
The crowd fled from him with a laugn
And gaily called him “Jack . ’
Then someone threw banana skin,
And others bits of bark ;
Then nearly ev’ryone joined in
And chased them o’er the park.
“Old Jack” and his disciple sped
Like rabbits to their lair;
Pursuers aimed at his bald head
And bade her bind her hair.
They got to ground at last. The mob
Stood round and made a row.
Till some stout copper found the job
And growled : “Goo wan there now .
For three drear days the couple stayed
In their obscurity:
Then some religious paper made
A grave discovery.
It asked: “Is John the Hermit wed,
Or does he live in Sin?”
Then Mrs. Grundy raised her head
And made a frightful din.
At once a moral army marched
Out to the draughty cave,
Its manner, like its collar, starched
The dwellers’ Souls to save.
And when the patriarchal John
Beheld the holy mass
And cried: “What mission come ye on?”
It sighed at him : “Alas !”
The Hermit purpled where he stood
And shook his wire-wound pole.
“O Man!” they droned, “ that is no good !
We come to save your Soul!”
“My soul’s my own to save!” he yelled.
“I saved it long ago!
Get out !” The awful staff he held
Went whistling to and fro.
“This is your wedding day!” they cried,
“Begone ! !” was his reply ;
The leader of the army sighed:
“Jack” smote him in the eye.
The army squealed and at him ran,
Then over him careered.
He rose a dazed and humble man
With mud upon his beard.
They showed him what the press had said
And told him sundry tales;
Meekly at last his bride he led
Unto the altar rails.
So they were wed and left the cave
Some better home to seek;
They got a cottage called “The Nave”
At fifteen bob per week.
And thereabout the cult expired ;
The good old begging bowl
Stood empty ; not a fool desired
To learn about thp Soul.
The magazine no more came out
With pictures of “The Sage”;
There was a scarcity of Doubt,
But lots of debt and rage.
To fortune-telling by the card
The she-disciple took;
“The Master” loafed in the backyard
Reading some trashy book.
But often he would heave a sigh
And d—n the rented hutch.
Saying he had been ruined by
The cheap suburban touch.
N.S.W.
KODAK.
Not Right in His Head.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
STINKER WILLIAMS had become re-
flectively silent. So quiet was he
that for a time he almost ceased to
exist as far as the other “vags” were con-
cerned.
“Asleep, Stinker?” somebody asked at
last.
“I was on’y finkin’,” said Stinker.
At this there was a hubbub of sarcastic
remarks, due to the fact that Stinker was
more noted for talk than for thought. He
took it all unconcernedly.
“Be quiet a while, blokes,” said the one
who had first called attention to Stinker.
“Let’s hear what he has to say.”
“I wuz on’y finkin’ about a bloke wot got
me beat onct,” he said. “Rummiest bloke
ever I struck.”
The group of derelicts waited expect-
antly, but Stinker reflected some more, and
when he spoke he seemed to get right away
from the point.
“Melbourne isn’t in it with Sydney for
bummin’,” he said. “Before yous know where
yous are the John Dunns are onter yer
trail. They gives yer 24 hours to do er
bunk.”
“Is that wot the cow was thinkin
about?” said a disgusted individual who had
expected something different.
“I mention that,” said Stinker, quietly,
“becos that is ’ow I come to meet the un-
usual bloke.”
“Spit it out. Spit it out,” yelled an im-
patient bull-necked vagrant.
“You go to blazes,” said Stinker. “Who
are yer talkin’ ter? Two pins I’d shut up.”
“Never mind ’im,” said another bummer,
conciliatingly.
“He gets onter me nerves, said Stinker.
“Fair gives me the ’ump. It’s iggerance.
Don’t know ’ow he wuz brought up. En
jest as I wuz goin’ ter tell somefing serious,
somefing wif a funeral in it.” He reflected
a second or two. “If there wuzn’t a funeral
there oughter bin.” Then, after another
pause: “Might a bin a funeral for all I
knows. . , , .
“He wuz a unusual bloke that. My oat,
’e took the bun all right. I wuz comin
back w’en I meets him”
“Coming back?” said the man with the
bull neck, inquiringly.
“From Melbourne, blast yer! said Stin-
ker, irritably. “Didn’t I tell yer the slops
give yer 24 hours to do a bunk? The bloke
wuzn’t a bummer, but ’e wuz fair on his
uppers, en ’ungry, en before we’d bin an
hour paddin’ along togevver I could see 1 d
’ave ter get shot of ’im. Ard enough bum
min’ for yerself along that road. I wuz
never keen on the bush meself, but wot
little I seed of it I soon gripped that if yer
didn’t want ter starve yer’d want to keep
off the main roads, en ter ave a bloke wit
yer who would ravver starve than ask tor a
bit of scran —well, it wuz no cop.
“Well, jest as I wuz wonderin ow 1
could get shot of ’im, wevver to pretend I
wuz balmy or tell him straight I wuz fair
sick of ’im, ’e told me ’e ’ad half a crown,
earned ’onestly a-puttin’ up a fowl- ouse for
the Christmas poultry for a woman, en e
says ’e’d get some tucker wif it, en first
nub we’d ’ave a beer a-piece.
“It didn’t take me long to see e wuz wot
they calls a gentleman. ’E wuz very nuiet,
en spoke c’rectly, en never contradicted yer,
en gave way for yer to pass fust, en useter
say ‘Please’ en ‘fank yer.’ en if ’e wanted
ter laugh at yer ’e’d always wait till yer
wuzn’t lookin’, en I gave up finkin I d get
shot of him, en stick to im like grim deaf.
Mv oaf, ’e wuz a fair cop.
“We ’ad the beers. Or, ravver, I did, be-
cos ’e on’v sipped his, en I pretended after

Sometimes I useter go a bit ’ungry meself,

“’Ow did yer manage yerself, Stinker?”
“I useter tell ’im to go ahead en we’d
meet later on,” explained Stinker. “Or I’d
go on ahead while ’e looked for work. Some-
times I’d git tucker w’en we wuz togevver, but
I’d wolf it before ’e knowed I ’ad ennyfing.
He seemed such a d— —- fool that I begun
to ’ate him like poison, en I told ’im fings I
wonder ’e didn't stiffen me. en w’en ’e
didn’t, I fought ’e must be frightened.”
Stinker looked thoughtful a moment or so.
“But ’e wuzn’t frightened,” he said slowly.
“I found that out before long, en I found
out ’e could use his ’ands. We ’ad a scrap
at last, en I fought I’d struck an ’urricane.
Yer see, we’d bin let sleep at a farmer’s in
the ’ay-loft one wet night, en there wuz a
bit of calico which I fought would come in
’andy for a tent, en w’en we’d got on five
miles I told ’im wot I’d got, en ’e wanted
me to take it back.”
“And you didn’t?” said one of the vag-
grants.
“Didn’t I?” said Stinker. “Not ’alf.
W’en I found out ’ow ’e could hit the dis-
tance didn’t seem so far, en we bof went
back, en the unusual bloke told the farmer
I’d taken it be mistake, en ’e apologised
for me.
“I couldn’t make ’ead nor tail of ’im after
that. He wuz a marvel. Fancy a bloke
who could walk rings round yer en hit yer
wherever ’e wanted ter, en you ’elpless agin’
’im, atakin’ of a back seat en excusin’ ’im-
self for bein’ on the earf all the time. Gifts
is fair frown away on some blokes.”
Stinker paused so long that it seemed he
had finished with the subject, when some-
body said :—-
“But didn’t I ’ear yer say there wuz a
funeral in it?”
Stinker looked very thoughtful.
“Yes, but I didn’t mean ter tell yer about
it; but, after all, I don’t see w’y I shouldn’t.
It wuzn’t my funeral.”
“Gome on, spit it out,” grunted the bull-
necked vagrant.
“I tole yers about it raining, en me takin’
of the calico for a tent?” he asked. “Well,
if we’d kept that calico it woulder bin or-
right, but instead we ’ad ter sleep wherever
we got a chanct to keep dry. One night we
slep’ under a railway bridge on a bit of a
rise. Yer see. it wuz the on’y dry place w*e
could get wifout enny stones, en we wuz
sheltered be one of the pillars of the bridge.
In the night I woke up afeelin’ a funny
feelin’ all a-comin’ over of me, en suddenly
I jumped up en found it wuz water. The
river wuz up, en there we wuz on a bit of a
island wif the water jest beginnin’ to cover
the last dry spot.
“ ‘Seems to me, Mr. Williams,’ he says,
‘that we are in a bit of a fix.’ En then ’e
says: ‘Kin yer swim?’ En I says ‘No.’
En I asks, ‘Kin you?’ En ’e says, ‘Not a
stroke. I wuz finkin’ if you could swim,
Mr. Williams, that you might git ashore en
bring assistance.’
“To tell the truf, I could swim a little,
but I wuzn’t goin’ to tell ’im I could, for I
guessed wot ’e’d be a-askin’ of me, en I
wuzn’t exactly an hero to try en git over to
the bank in a flood like that. I wuzn’t
takin’ chances, not me.
“ ‘Better wait till mornin’ en trust ter
luck,’ I says.
“ ‘The water’s risin’,’ ’e says. ‘En before
mornin’ comes it will be too late,’ ’e says.
‘Pity one of us can’t swim,’ ’e says sorrow-
fully. .
“As the water wuz comm up over our
ankles, en then over our knees, ’e gives me
a lecture on the vally of bein’ able to swim,
en ’e says: ‘lf you gits out of this, Mr.
Williams, en ever comes to marry, en to
’ave children, let this be a warnin’ to yer,
en ’ave them all taught ter swim.’
“W’en the water wuz to our waists en
runnin’ so strong we could ’ardly keep our
feet, ’e says, ‘lf on’y somefing would come
in our reach which we could git onter. Keep
yer eyes open,’ ’e says, ‘en we might yet be
saved.’
“Then I fought ’ow I hated him. I opened
up on ’im, en tole ’im wot I fought of ’im.
I told ’im ’ow I hated ’im from wifin a ho rn
of seein’ ’im, en wot a cow en a fool ’e wuz.
All ’e said wuz :
“ ‘This is no time for recriminals, but to
look forward to deaf,’ he says. ‘En seein’
it is seemingly fated we are to die togevver,
let’s face it bravely en wif brovverly love.’
“My oaf, I could ’ave stoushed ’im, stand-
in’ there like a ’oly Joe. I could ’ave give
him a push en be rid of him, but somefing
’eld me back, en, ennyway, he might ha’
give me first push if I’d tried. It seemed
all up wif us.
“Suddenly ’e says: ‘Ah, this might prove
of assistance.’ En wot does ’e collar ’old of
but a small log of wood wot is floatin’ by?
Then ’e says, ‘lt will on’y bear the weight
of one,’ ’e says. ‘Wot shall we do?’ ’e says.
Then, after finkin’ awhile, I says, ‘I ’ave it.
I’ll lay on it, en I’ll ’old you be the ’and, en
we’ll trust ter Providence.’ ‘We can’t stay
’ere enny longer,’ ’e says, for the water wuz
up to our armpits. So ’e puts one leg over
it en grips wif one ’and, en then ’e gives me
his ovver ’and, en we floats off. After a
little while we found it ’ad two little
branches which kept it from rollin’ over en
over, but all the same it wuz no cop. It wuz
me in the water ’ad the worst of it, en I
found meself wishin’ I wuz on the log, en
’im in the water, en I wuz wonderin’ ’ow I
could get there.
“ ‘You must find it heavy holdin’ of me,’
I says.
“ ‘Yes,’ ’e says. ‘But do not fear. I’ll
not let yer go,’ ’e says. ‘Enny minute,’ he
says, ‘we might float to shore.’
“ ‘ ’Ow about us takin’ it in turns to hold
each uvver?’
“ ‘Good idea,’ ’e says, en ’e slipped off the
log, en I laid on it en held ’im.
“’E wuz right about it bein’ heavy a-
holdin’ of ’im. Sometimes it seemed as if
the current wuz a-tuggin’ of ’im away, en I
fought we’d bof go. But I ’eld ’im. At
last me arm started to ache, en I fought,
‘Oh, I’ll not be burvened.’ ”
“Wot did yer do?” he was- asked.
“1 let go.”
A silence followed. Nobody offered to
make a comment, and Stinker seemed to be
thinking hard. At last he said: —
“I often fink ’e wuz the biggest fool ever
I met. Sometimes I fink ’e couldn’t ’ave
bin right in his ’ead.”
ROBERT BROTHERS.
The Hat.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WOULD you kindly show me that
hat?” !
The words were addressed to
a tall, duchess-like person in clinging black.
She lifted it reverently from the case and
placed it at a becoming tilt on my
head.
I looked at myself in the mirror.
I felt 16. I am 30 !
“What is the price?” I queried trem-
blingly.
“Only 355., and it’s a French model,” said
the imitation duchess.
I had exactly ninepence ha’penny in the
world !
I looked again at myself. It was so
lovely to feel 16.
I sighed, and handed it back to the
duchess, and murmured something about it
being copied in less expensive materials —
knowing full well that such a creation could
not be copied. A Parisienne milliner
makes a hat once like that in a lifetime,
and then dies! And no one knows where
she is buried.
I walked sadly out of the shop.
Outside it was Spring. Already she had
given each little twig of the elms a dear
bunchy little mob cap of softest green petals
to wear —while I was to go on wearing my
old dowdy winter hat —and up there in the
case
I walked on with heavy heart, and pre-
sently found myself in the Gardens. There,
too, Spring had been giving every shrub,
every tree, every ,rp, some soft delicate
raiment in which to dance in the soft warm
breeze.
I sat down on the velvety cool grass.
If only I could feel 16 again on such a
glorious day. But I felt more 30 than ever.
Then I lost myself in thought, and when
a desperate, hatless woman thinks, she most
often arrives.
At last the idea came.
I hurried out of the Gardens, and jumped
on to the first tram that happened my way.
I knocked timidly at his door.
“Come in,” he answered.
“Are you very busy, Billy dear?” I
queried.
“Never too busy to see you.”
“It’s very charming of you to say so.”
“But what’s the matter? You look quite
glum. And on such a fine Spring day. too.”
“Oh, what’s the good of a Spring day if
you haven’t Spring in your heart?” I an-
swered.
“P’raps it’s indigestion,” he said, as he
offered me a cigarette.
“Billy, you’re an unsympathetic brute.
I’ve a good mind not to ask you to come
round to my room while I make you a cup
of French coffee.”
“Good!” he exclaimed. “Just what I’d
like.” And he reached for his hat.
“Come along, then,” I said, walking to-
wards" the door. Then, stopping and clutch-
ing the armchair, I turned and sank into its
depths, my eyes glistening with tears (not
too many to overflow, as I had taken great
care in the making up of my lashes that
morning). Drooping my head, ana with
hands clasped, I sought an attitude of utter
despair.
“Why, what’s the matter, old girl? Are
you ill? Let me get you someth’ ”
“No, no, Billy, dear —I’ve, I’ve just re-
membered,” and I choked hysterically. “I
CAN’T make you a cup of coffee because —
because,” and I paused helplessly, “the beast
of a gasman cut off my meter this morning !
What AM I to do?”
“Good Lord! Is that all? I thought
you had appendicitis or something.”
Then I looked at him with the soul of an
angel.
‘How much is it?” he asked.
“Thirty-five shillings,” I answered, with-
out looking at him.
“Why, I thought it must be 35 pounds,”
he said, smiling deliciously.
“It’s as much to me,” I said, as I smiled
back, blinking away an unmanageable tear.
Then like a wizard he extracted two
bright golden coins from a tiny case, and
pressed them into my hand.
“Go at once and settle that dreadful
matter with your gasman. Then meet me
at the Cafd Parisian at a quarter after six,
and we’ll have a jolly little dinner, just
because it’s Spring-time, and you need
cheering up, and I need most of all your
charming company.”
“Billy, dear, I can’t take the money. I
can NEVER pay you back.” And he
thought I meant it, for he urged me to the
door, patting me affectionately on the shoul-
der, and telling me not to keep him waiting.
Once outside I almost flew on to the tram.
Then upstairs, with feet like air, pausing
only when I reached the top, I walked
slowly over to where “The Hat” posed allur-
ingly on its pedestal.
“I want to buy that hat,” I said lan-
guidly.
“Yes, madame ; will you try it on?”
I looked once more at myself. Was there
ever such a temperamental lining, or such an
elusive bow. And the curve of the brim
could express anything one could wish-
while the “tout ensemble” one of charming
simplicity.
“I will wear it now,” I said. “How much
is it?”
I gave her the gas money.
Then, hurrying home, I sat before my
mirror and went through all the intricacies
of a toilet (some women’s toilet).
And placing the dear Hat —the very dear
Hat —on my demurely parted hair, I went
to meet Billy.
As I came up to him he said, “Why, what
has changed you? You look positively 14
years younger than when I saw you last,
and prettier than I’ve ever seen you before.
What on earth have you done to accomplish
it?”
“Paid my gas account, Billy dear, and it’s
lifted a weight off my "find!”
(My old hat was SO heavy.)
VIVA SOMMERS.
